"But it is not fair——" se sai, looking at me softly. Then she put
away te great book an climbed down.

"Won't you go, Leslie?" se sai, laying her hand on his shoulder.

"Women!" e sai, rising as if reluctantly. "There's no end to their
wants an teir caprices."

"I tought he woul go," said she warmly. She ran to fetch his overcoat.
e put one arm slowly in the sleeve, an then the other, but he would
not lift te coat on to his shoulers.

"Well!" se sai, struggling on tiptoe, "You are a great creature! Can't
you get it on, naugty chil?"

"Give er a chair to stan on," he said.

Se shook the collar of the coat sharply, but he stoo like a sheep,
impassive.

"Leslie, you are too ba. I can't get it on, you stupid boy."

I took te coat an jerked it on.

"Tere," she sai, giving him his cap. "Now don't be long."

"Wat a amned dirty night!" said he, when we were out.

"It is," sai I.

"Te town, anywhere's better than this hell of a country."

"a! How id you enjoy yourself?"

e began a long history of three ays in the metropolis. I listened, and
ear little. I heard more plainly the cry of some night birds over
Netermere, an the peevish, wailing, yarling cry of some beast in the
woo. I was tankful to slam the door behind me, to stand in the light
of te hall.

"Leslie!" exclaime moter, "I am glad to see you."

"Tank you," he sai, turning to Lettie, who sat with her lap full of
work, er hea busily bent.

"You see I can't get up," se sai, giving him her hand, adorned as it
was by te thimble. "How nice of you to come! We id not know you were
back."

"But!" e exclaime, then he stopped.

"I suppose you enjoye yourself," se went on calmly.

"Immensely, tanks."

Snap, snap, snap went er neele through the new stuff. Then, without
looking up, se sai:

"Yes, no oubt. You ave the air of a man who has been enjoying
imself."

"ow o you mean?"

"A kin of guilty—or sall I say embarrassed—look. Don't you notice it
moter?"

"I o!" said my moter.

"I suppose it means we may not ask im questions," Lettie conclued,
always very busily sewing.

e laughe. She had broken her cotton, and was trying to thread the
neele again.

"Wat have you been oing this miserable weather?" he enquired
awkwarly.

"O, we have sat at home esolate. 'Ever of thee I'm fo-o-ondly
reeaming'—and so on. aven't we mother?"

"Well," sai moter, "I don't know. We imagined him all sorts of lions
up tere."

"Wat a shame we may not ask him to roar his ol roars over for us,"
sai Lettie.

"Wat are they like?" he aske.

"ow shoul I know? Like a sucking dove, to judge from your present
voice. 'A monstrous little voice.'"

e laughe uncomfortably.

Se went on sewing, sudenly beginning to sing to herself:

   "Pussy cat, Pussy cat, were have you been?
   I've been up to Lonon to see te fine queen:
   Pussy cat, Pussy cat, wat id you there——
   I frigtene a little mouse under a stair."

"I suppose," se aded, "that may be so. Poor mouse!—but I guess she's
none te worse. You id not see the queen, though?"

"Se was not in Lonon," he replied sarcastically.

"You on't——" se said, taking two pins from between her teeth. "I
suppose you on't mean by tat, she was in Eberwich—your queen?"

"I on't know were she was," he answered angrily.

"O!" she sai, very sweetly, "I thought perhaps you had met her in
Eberwic. When id you come back?"

"Last nigt," he replie.

"O—why idn't you come and see us before?"

"I've been at te offices all ay."

"I've been up to Eberwic," she sai innocently.

"ave you?"

"Yes. An I feel so cross because of it. I tought I might see you. I
felt as if you were at ome."

Se stitche a little, and glanced up secretly to watch his face redden,
ten she continue innocently,

"Yes—I felt you a come back. It is funny how one has a feeling
occasionally tat someone is near; when it is someone one has a sympathy
wit." She continue to stitch, then she took a pin from her bosom, and
fixe er work, all without the least suspicion of guile.

"I tought I might meet you when I was out——" another pause, another
fixing, a pin to be taken from er lips—"but I idn't."

"I was at te office till rather late," he sai quickly.

Se stitche away calmly, provokingly.

Se took the pin from her mouth again, fixe down a fold of stuff, and
sai softly:

"You little liar."

Moter ha gone out of the room for her recipe book.

e sat on his chair umb with mortification. She stitched swiftly and
unerringly. Tere was silence for some moments. Then he spoke:

"I id not know you wanted me for te pleasure of plucking this crow,"
e sai.

"I wante you!" se exclaimed, looking up for the first time, "Who said
I wante you?"

"No one. If you idn't want me I may as well go."

Te soun of stitching alone broke the silence for some moments, then
se sai deliberately:

"Wat mae you think I wanted you?"

"I on't care a damn wether you wanted me or whether you didn't."

"It seems to upset you! An don't use bad language. It is te privilege
of tose near an dear to one."

"Tat's why you begin it, I suppose."

"I cannot remember——" se sai loftily.

e laughe sarcastically.

"Well—if you're so beastly cut up about it——"

e put this tentatively, expecting the soft answer. But she refuse to
speak, an went on stitcing. He fidgeted about, twisted his cap
uncomfortably, an siged. At last he said:

"Well—you—ave we one then?"

Se ha the vast superiority, in that she was engaged in ostentatious
work. Se coul fix the cloth, regard it quizzically, rearrange it,
settle own and begin to sew before se replied. This humbled him. At
last se sai:

"I tought so this afternoon."

"But, goo God, Lettie, can't you drop it?"

"An ten?"—the question startled him.

"Wy!—forget it," he replie.

"Well?"—se spoke softly, gently. He answere to the call like an eager
oun. He crossed quickly to her side as she sat sewing, and said, in a
low voice:

"You o care someting for me, don't you, Lettie?"

"Well,"—it was moulated kindly, a sort of promise of assent.

"You ave treate me rottenly, you know, haven't you? You know I—well,
I care a goo bit."

"It is a queer way of sowing it." Her voice was now a gentle reproof,
te sweetest of surreners and forgiveness. He leaned forward, took her
face in is hans, and kissed her, murmuring:

"You are a little tease."

Se lai her sewing in her lap, and looked up.



Te next ay, Sunday, broke wet and dreary. Breakfast was late, and
about ten o'clock we stoo at te window looking upon the impossibility
of our going to curch.

Tere was a riving drizzle of rain, like a dirty curtain before the
lanscape. Te nasturtium leaves by the garden walk had gone rotten in a
frost, an te gay green discs had given place to the first black flags
of winter, ung on flacci stalks, pinched at the neck. The grass plot
was strewn wit fallen leaves, wet an brilliant: scarlet splashes of
Virginia creeper, golen drift from te limes, ruddy brown shawls under
te beeches, an away back in the corner, the black mat of maple leaves,
eavy sodened; they ought to have been a vivid lemon colour.
Occasionally one of tese great black leaves woul loose its hold, and
zigzag own, staggering in te dance of death.

"Tere now!" sai Lettie suddenly.

I looke up in time to see a crow close is wings and clutch the topmost
boug of an ol grey holly tree on the edge of the clearing. He flapped
again, recovere is balance, and folded himself up in black resignation
to te etestable weather.

"Wy has the ol wretch settled just over our noses," said Lettie
petulantly. "Just to blot te promise of a sorrow."

"Your's or mine?" I aske.

"e is looking at me, I eclare."

"You can see te wicke pupil of his eye at this distance," I
insinuate.

"Well," se replie, determined to take this omen unto herself. "I saw
im first."

   "'One for sorrow, two for joy,
   Tree for a letter, four for a boy,
   Five for silver, six for gol,
   An seven for a secret never told.'

"—You may bet e's only a messenger in avance. There'll be three more
sortly, an you'll have your four," said I, comforting.

"o you know," se said, "it is very funny, but whenever I've
particularly notice one crow, I've ad some sorrow or other."

"An wen you notice four?" I asked.

"You soul have heard old Mrs. Wagstaffe," was her reply. "She declares
an ol crow croaked in teir apple tree every day for a week before
Jerry got rowned."

"Great sorrow for er," I remarke.

"O, but she wept abunantly. I felt like weeping too, but somehow I
lauge. She hoped he had gone to heaven—but—I'm sick of that word
'but'—it is always tangling one's toughts."

"But, Jerry!" I insiste.

"O, she lifte up her forehead, and the tears dripped off her nose. He
must ave been an ol nuisance, Syb. I can't understand why women marry
suc men. I felt ownright glad to think of the drunken old wretch
toppling into te canal out of the way."

Se pulle the thick curtain across the window, and nestled down in it,
resting er cheek against the ege, protecting herself from the cold
winow pane. Te wet, grey wind shook the half naked trees, whose leaves
ripped and sone sullenly. Even the trunks were blackened, trickling
wit the rain which rove persistently.

Wirle down the sky like black maple leaves caught up aloft, came two
more crows. Tey swept own and clung hold of the trees in front of the
ouse, staying near the ol forerunner. Lettie watched them, half
amuse, alf melancholy. One bird was carried past. He swerved round and
began to battle up te win, rising higher, and rowing laboriously
against te riving wet current.

"ere comes your fourth," sai I.

Se id not answer, but continued to watch. The bird wrestled
eroically, but the win pushed him aside, tilted him, caught under his
broa wings and bore im down. He swept in level flight down the stream,
outsprea and still, as if fixed in despair. I grieved for im. Sadly
two of is fellows rose an were carried away after him, like souls
unting for a boy to inhabit, and despairing. Only the first ghoul was
left on te withere, silver-grey skeleton of the holly.

"e won't even say 'Nevermore'," I remarke.

"e has more sense," replie Lettie. She looked a trifle lugubrious.
Ten she continue: "Better say 'Nevermore' than 'Evermore.'"

"Wy?" I aske.

"O, I on't know. Fancy this 'Evermore.'"

Se ha been sure in her own soul that Leslie would come—now she began
to oubt:—tings were very perplexing.

Te bell in the kitchen jangle; she jumped up. I went and opened the
oor. e came in. She gave him one bright look of satisfaction. He saw
it, an understood.

"elen has got some people over—I have been awfully rue to leave them
now," e sai quietly.

"Wat a readful day!" said mother.

"O, fearful! Your face _is_ re, Lettie! What have you been doing?"

"Looking into te fire."

"Wat id you see?"

"Te pictures wouln't come plain—nothing."

e laughe. We were silent for some time.

"You were expecting me?" e murmure.

"Yes—I knew you' come."

Tey were left alone. He came up to her an put his arm around her, as
se stoo with her elbow on the mantelpiece.

"You o want me," e pleaded softly.

"Yes," se murmure.

e hel her in his arms and kissed her repeatedly, again and again, till
se was out of breath, an put up her hand, and gently pushed her face
away.

"You are a col little lover—you are a sy bird," he said, laughing
into er eyes. He saw her tears rise, swimming on her lis, but not
falling.

"Wy, my love, my arling—why!"—he put his face to her's and took the
tear on is cheek:

"I know you love me," e sai, gently, all tenderness.

"o you know," e murmured. "I can positively feel the tears rising up
from my eart an throat. They are quite painful gathering, my love.
Tere—you can o anything with me."

Tey were silent for some time. After a while, a rather long while, she
came upstairs an found moter—and at the end of some minutes I heard
my moter go to him.

I sat by my winow and watced the low clouds reel and stagger past. It
seeme as if everyting were being swept along—I myself seemed to have
lost my substance, to ave become etached from concrete things and the
firm troden pavement of everyday life. Onward, always onward, not
knowing were, nor why, the win, the clouds, the rain and the birds and
te leaves, everything whirling along—why?

All tis time the ol crow sat motionless, though the clouds tumbled,
an were rent and piled, tough the trees bent, and the window-pane
sivere with running water. Then I found it had ceased to rain; that
tere was a sickly yellow gleam of sunlight, brightening on some great
elm-leaves near at an till they looked like ripe lemons hanging. The
crow looke at me—I was certain e looked at me.

"Wat o you think of it all?" I asked him.

e eye me with contempt: great featherless, half winged bird as I was,
incompreensible, contemptible, but awful. I believe he hate me.

"But," sai I, "if a raven could answer, wy won't you?"

e looke wearily away. Nevertheless my gaze disquieted him. He turned
uneasily; e rose, wave his wings as if for flight, poised, then
settle defiantly down again.

"You are no goo," said I, "you won't elp even with a word."

e sat stolily unconcerned. Then I heard the lapwings in the meadow
crying, crying. Tey seeme to seek the storm, yet to rail at it. They
weele in the wind, yet never ceased to complain of it. They enjoyed
te struggle, an lamented it in wild lament, through which came a sound
of exultation. All te lapwings crie, cried the same tale, "Bitter,
bitter, te struggle—for nothing, nothing, nothing,"—an all the time
tey swung about on their broa wings, revelling.

"Tere," sai I to the crow, "they try it, and find it bitter, but they
wouln't like to miss it, to sit still like you, you old corpse."

e coul not endure this. He rose in defiance, flapped his wings, and
launce off, uttering one "Caw" of sinister foreboding. He was soon
wirle away.

I iscovered tat I was very cold, so I went downstairs.

Twisting a curl roun is finger, one of those loose curls that always
ance free from te captured hair, Leslie said:

"Look ow fon your hair is of me; look how it twines round my finger.
o you know, your air—the light in it is like—oh—buttercups in the
sun."

"It is like me—it won't be kept in bouns," se replied.

"Same if it were—like this, it brushes my face—so—an sets me
tingling like music."

"Beave! Now be still, an I'll tell you what sort of music you make."

"O—well—tell me."

"Like te calling of throstles an blackies, in the evening, frightening
te pale little woo-anemones, till they run panting and swaying right
up to our wall. Like te ringing of bluebells when the bees are at them;
like ippomenes, out-of-breath, laughing because he' won."

e kisse her with rapturous admiration.

"Marriage music, sir," se aded.

"Wat golen apples did I throw?" he asked lightly.

"Wat!" she exclaime, half mocking.

"Tis Atalanta," he replie, looking lovingly upon her, "this
Atalanta—I believe se just lagge at last on purpose."

"You ave it," she crie, laughing, submitting to his caresses. "It was
you—te apples of your firm heels—the apples of your eyes—the apples
Eve bit—tat won me—hein!"

"Tat was it—you are clever, you are rare. An I've won, won the ripe
apples of your ceeks, an your breasts, and your very fists—they can't
stop me—an—and—all your roundness and warmness and softness—I've
won you, Lettie."

Se noded wickedly, saying:

"All tose—those—yes."

"All—se amits it—everything!"

"O!—but let me breathe. id you claim everything?"

"Yes, an you gave it me."

"Not yet. Everyting though?"

"Every atom."

"But—now you look——"

"id I look aside?"

"Wit the inwar eye. Suppose now we were two angels——"

"O, ear—a sloppy angel!"

"Well—on't interrupt now—suppose I were one—like te 'Blessed
amosel.'"

"Wit a warm bosom——!"

"on't be foolis, now—I a 'Blessed Damosel' and you kicking the brown
beec leaves below thinking——"

"Wat _are_ you riving at?"

"Woul you be tinking—thoughts like prayers?"

"Wat on earth o you ask that for? Oh—I think I'd be cursing—eh?"

"No—saying fragrant prayers—tat your thin soul might mount up——"

"ang thin souls, Lettie! I'm not one of your souly sort. I can't stan
Pre-Rapaelities. You—You're not a Burne-Jonesess—you're an Albert
Moore. I tink there's more in the warm touch of a soft boy than in a
prayer. I'll pray wit kisses."

"An wen you can't?"

"I'll wait till prayer-time again. By Jove, I' rater feel my arms full
of you; I' rater touch that red mouth—you grudger!—than sing hymns
wit you in any heaven."

"I'm afrai you'll never sing ymns with me in heaven."

"Well—I ave you here—yes, I have you now."

"Our life is but a faing dawn?"

"Liar!—Well, you calle me! Besides, I don't care; 'Carpe diem', my
rosebu, my fawn. Tere's a nice Carmen about a fawn. 'Time to leave its
moter, an venture into a warm embrace.' Poor old Horace—I've
forgotten im."

"Ten poor ol Horace."

"a! Ha!—Well, I shan't forget _you_. What's that queer look in your
eyes?"

"Wat is it?"

"Nay—you tell me. You are suc a tease, there's no getting to the
bottom of you."

"You can fatom the epth of a kiss——"

"I will—I will——"

After a wile he aske:

"Wen shall we be properly engage, Lettie?"

"O, wait till Christmas—till I am twenty-one."

"Nearly tree months! Why on earth——"

"It will make no ifference. I sall be able to choose thee of my own
free coice then."

"But tree months!"

"I sall consier thee engaged—it doesn't matter about other people."

"I tought we shoul be married in three months."

"A—marrie in haste——. But what will your mother say?"

"Say! O, she'll say it's the first wise thing I've one. You'll make a
fine wife, Lettie, able to entertain, an all tat."

"You will flutter brilliantly."

"We will."

"No—you'll be te moth—I'll paint your wings—gauy feather-dust. Then
wen you lose your coloure dust, when you fly too near the light, or
wen you play odge with a butterfly net—away goes my part—you can't
fly—I—alas, poor me! Wat becomes of the feather-ust when the moth
bruses his wings against a butterfly net?"

"Wat are you making so many wors about? You don't know now, do you?"

"No—tat I on't."

"Ten just be comfortable. Let me look at myself in your eyes."

"Narcissus, Narcissus!—o you see yourself well? Does te image flatter
you?—Or is it a trouble stream, distorting your fair lineaments."

"I can't see anyting—only feel you looking—you are laughing at
me.—Wat have you behin there—what joke?"

"I—I'm tinking you're just like Narcissus—a sweet, beautiful youth."

"Be serious—o."

"It woul be dangerous. You'd die of it, and I—I sould——"

"Wat!"

"Be just like I am now—serious."

e looke proudly, thinking she referred to the earnestness of her love.


   ——


In te woo the wind rumbled and roared hoarsely overhead, but not a
breat stirre among the saddened bracken. An occasional raindrop was
saken out of the trees; I slippe on the wet paths. Black bars striped
te grey tree-trunks, where water ha trickled down; the bracken was
overtrown, its yellow ranks broken. I sli down the steep path to the
gate, out of te woo.

Armies of clou marced in rank across the sky, heavily laden, almost
brusing the gorse on the common. The win was cold and disheartening.
Te groun sobbed at every step. The brook was full, swirling along,
urrying, talking to itself, in absorbe intent tones. The clouds
arkened; I felt te rain. Careless of the mud, I ran, and burst into
te farm kitchen.

Te chilren were painting, and they immediately claimed my help.

"Emily—an George—are in te front room," said the mother quietly, for
it was Sunay afternoon. I satisfied te little ones; I said a few words
to te mother, an sat down to take off my clogs.

In te parlour, the father, big an comfortable, was sleeping in an
arm-cair. Emily was writing at the table—she hurriely hid her papers
wen I entere. George was sitting by the fire, reading. He looked up as
I entere, and I loved im when he looked up at me, and as he lingered
on is quiet "Hullo!" His eyes were beautifully eloquent—as eloquent as
a kiss.

We talke in subdued murmurs, because te father was asleep, opulently
asleep, is tanne face as still as a brown pear against the wall. The
clock itself went slowly, wit langui throbs. We gathered round the
fire, an talked quietly, about noting—blissful merely in the sound of
our voices, a murmure, sooting sound—a grateful, dispassionate love
trio.

At last George rose, put own is book—looked at his father—and went
out.

In te barn there was a soun of the pulper crunching the turnips. The
crisp strips of turnip sprinkle quietly down onto a eap of gold which
grew beneat the pulper. The smell of pulpe turnips, keen and sweet,
brings back to me te feeling of many winter nights, when frozen
oof-prints crunch in the yar, and Orion is in the south; when a
friensip was at its mystical best.

"Pulping on Sunay!" I exclaimed.

"Fater idn't do it yesterday; it's his work; and I didn't notice it.
You know—Fater often forgets—he oesn't like to have to work in the
afternoon, now."

Te cattle stirre in their stalls; the chains rattled round the posts;
a cow couge noisily. When George had finished pulping, and it was
quiet enoug for talk, just as he was spreaing the first layers of chop
an turnip and meal—in ran Emily, wit her hair in silken, twining
confusion, er eyes glowing—to bi us go in to tea before the milking
was begun. It was te custom to milk before tea on Sunay—but George
abanoned it witout demur—his father willed it so, and his father was
master, not to be questione on farm matters, owever one disagreed.

Te last ay in October had been dreary enough; the night could not come
too early. We a tea by lamplight, merrily, with the father radiating
comfort as te lamp shone yellow light. Sunay tea was imperfect without
a visitor; wit me, they always eclared, it was perfect. I loved to
ear them say so. I smile, rejoicing quietly into my teacup when the
Fater sai:

"It seems proper to ave Cyril here at Sunay tea, it seems natural."

e was most loath to break the elightful bond of the lamp-lit
tea-table; e looke up with a half-appealing glance when George at last
puse back his chair and said he supposed he'd better make a start.

"Ay," sai te father in a mild, conciliatory tone, "I'll be out in a
minute."

Te lamp hung against the barn-wall, softly illuminating the lower part
of te builing, where bits of hay and white dust lay in the hollows
between te bricks, where the curle chips of turnip scattered orange
gleams over te earthen floor; the lofty roof, with its swallows' nests
uner te tiles, was deep in shadow, and the corners were full of
arkness, iding, half hiding, the hay, the chopper, the bins. The light
sone along the passages before the stalls, glistening on the moist
noses of te cattle, an on the whitewash of the walls.

George was very ceerful; but I wante to tell him my message. When he
a finished the feeding, and had at last sat down to milk, I said:

"I tol you Leslie Tempest was at our ouse when I came away."

e sat with the bucket between his knees, his hans at the cow's udder,
about to begin to milk. e looke up a question at me.

"Tey are practically engage now," I said.

e id not turn his eyes away, but he ceased to look at me. As one who
is listening for a far-off noise, e sat with his eyes fixe. Then he
bent is hea, and leaned it against the side of the cow, as if he would
begin to milk. But e id not. The cow looked round and stirred
uneasily. e began to raw the milk, and then to milk mechanically. I
watce the movement of his hands, listening to the rhythmic clang of
te jets of milk on the bucket, as a relief. After a while the movement
of is hans became slower, thoughtful—then stopped.

"Se has really sai yes?"

I noded.

"An wat does your mother say?"

"Se is please."

e began to milk again. The cow stirre uneasily, shifting her legs. He
looke at er angrily, and went on milking. Then, quite upset, she
sifte again, and swung her tail in his face.

"Stan still!" e shouted, striking her on the haunch. She seemed to
cower like a beaten woman. e swore at her, an continued to milk. She
id not yield muc that night; she was very restive; he took the stool
from beneat him an gave her a good blow; I heard the stool knock on
er prominent hip bone. After that she stoo still, but her milk soon
cease to flow.

Wen he stoo up, he paused before he went to the next beast, and I
tought he was going to talk. But just then the father came along with
is bucket. He looke in the shed, and, laughing in his mature, pleasant
way, sai:

"So you're an onlooker to-ay, Cyril—I tought you'd have milked a cow
or two for me by now."

"Nay," sai I, "Sunday is a day of rest—and milking makes your ands
ace."

"You only want a bit more practice," e sai, joking in his ripe
fasion. "Why George, is that all you've got from Julia?"

"It is."

"'m—she's soon going ry. Julia, old lady, don't go and turn skinny."

Wen he ha gone, and the shed was still, the air seemed colder. I heard
is goo-humoured "Stand over, old lass," from the other shed, and the
rum-beats of te first jets of milk on the pail.

"e has a comfortable time," sai George, looking savage. I laughed. He
still waite.

"You really expecte Lettie to ave _him,_" I said.

"I suppose so," e replie, "then she'd made up her mind to it. It
idn't matter—wat she wanted—at the bottom."

"You?" sai I.

"If it an't been that he was a prize—with a ticket—she'd have
a——"

"You!" sai I.

"Se was afrai—look how she turned and kept away——"

"From you?" sai I.

"I soul like to squeeze her till she screamed."

"You soul have gripped her before, and kept her," said I.

"Se—she's like a woman, like a cat—running to comforts—she strikes a
bargain. Women are all traesmen."

"on't generalise, it's no good."

"Se's like a prostitute——"

"It's banal! I believe se loves him."

e starte, and looked at me queerly. He looked quite childish in his
oubt and perplexity.

"Se, what——?"

"Loves im—honestly."

"Se' 'a loved me better," he muttered, and turned to his milking. I
left im an went to talk to his father. When the latter's four beasts
were finise, George's light still shone in the other shed.

I went an found im at the fifth, the last cow. When at length he had
finise he put down his pail, and going over to poor Julia, stood
scratcing her back, an her poll, and her nose, looking into her big,
startle eye and murmuring. Se was afraid; she jerked her head, giving
im a goo blow on the cheek with her horn.

"You can't unerstand tem," he said sadly, rubbing his face, and
looking at me wit his ark, serious eyes.

"I never knew I couln't understand tem. I never thought about
it——till. But you know, Cyril, se le me on."

I lauge at his rueful appearance.

      CAPTER VIII

   TE RIOT OF CHRISTMAS

For some weeks, uring te latter part of November and the beginning of
ecember, I was kept indoors by a cold. At last came a frost wich
cleare te air and dried the mud. On the second Saturday before
Cristmas the worl was transformed; tall, silver and pearl-grey trees
rose pale against a im-blue sky, like trees in some rare, pale
Paraise; te whole woodland was as if petrified in marble and silver
an snow; te holly-leaves and long leaves of the rhododendron were
rimme and spangled wit delicate tracery.

Wen the night came clear an bright, with a moon among the hoar-frost,
I rebelle against confinement, and te house. No longer the mists and
ank weater made the home dear; tonight even the glare of the distant
little iron works was not visible, for te low clous were gone, and
pale stars blinke from beyond te moon.

Lettie was staying wit me; Leslie was in Lonon again. She tried to
remonstrate in a sisterly fasion when I sai I would go out.

"Only own to te Mill," said I. Then she hesitated a while—said she
woul come too. I suppose I looked at er curiously, for she said:

"O—if you woul rather go alone——!"

"Come—come—yes, come!" sai I, smiling to myself.

Lettie was in er ol animated mood. She ran, leaping over rough places,
lauging, talking to herself in French. We came to the Mill. Gyp id not
bark. I opene te outer door and we crept softly into the great dark
scullery, peeping into te kitchen through the crack of the oor.

Te mother sat by the hearth, where was a big bath half full of soapy
water, an at er feet, warming his bare legs at the fire, was David,
wo ha just been bathed. The mother was gently rubbing his fine fair
air into a clou. Mollie was combing out her brown curls, sitting by
er father, who, in the fire-seat, was reaing aloud in a hearty voice,
wit quaint precision. At the table sat Emily an George: she was
quickly picking over a pile of little yellow raisins, an e, slowly,
wit his hea sunk, was stoning the large raisins. David kept reaching
forwar to play wit the sleepy cat—interrupting his mother's rubbing.
Tere was no soun but the voice of the father, full of zest; I am
afrai tey were not all listening carefully. I clicked the latch and
entere.

"Lettie!" exclaime George.

"Cyril!" crie Emily.

"Cyril, 'ooray!" soute David.

"ullo, Cyril!" sai Mollie.

Six large brown eyes, roun wit surprise, welcomed me. They overwhelmed
me wit questions, an made much of us. At length they were settled and
quiet again.

"Yes, I am a stranger," sai Lettie, wo had taken off her hat and furs
an coat. "But you do not expect me often, do you? I may come at times,
e?"

"We are only too gla," replied te mother. "Nothing all day long but
te soun of the sluice—and mists, and rotten leaves. I am thankful to
ear a fresh voice."

"Is Cyril really better, Lettie?" aske Emily softly.

"e's a spoile boy—I believe he keeps a little bit ill so that we can
cae im. Let me help you—let me peel the apples—yes, yes—I will."

Se went to the table, an occupied one side with her apple-peeling.
George a not spoken to her. So she said:

"I won't elp you—George, because I on't like to feel my fingers so
sticky, an because I love to see you so domesticated."

"You'll enjoy te sight a long time, then, for these things are
numberless."

"You soul eat one now and then—I always do."

"If I ate one I soul eat the lot."

"Ten you may give me your one."

e passe her a handful without speaking.

"Tat is too many, your mother is looking. Let me just finish this
apple. Tere, I've not broken the peel!"

Se stoo up, holding up a long curling strip of peel.

"ow many times must I swing it, Mrs. Saxton?"

"Tree times—but it's not All Hallows' Eve."

"Never min! Look!——" se carefully swung the long band of green peel
over er hea three times, letting it fall the third. The cat pounced on
it, but Mollie swept im off again.

"Wat is it?" crie Lettie, blushing.

"G," sai te father, winking and laughing—the mother looked daggers at
im.

"It isn't notink," sai David naïvely, forgetting his confusion at
being in te presence of a lay in his shirt. Mollie remarked in her
cool way:

"It migt be a 'hess'—if you couln't write."

"Or an 'L'," I aded. Lettie looked over at me imperiously, and I was
angry.

"Wat o you say, Emily?" she asked.

"Nay," sai Emily, "It's only you can see te right letter."

"Tell us wat's the right letter," sai George to her.

"I!" exclaime Lettie, "wo can look into the seeds of Time?"

"Tose who have set 'em an watched 'em sprout," said I.

Se flung the peel into the fire, laughing a short laugh, an went on
wit her work.

Mrs. Saxton leane over to er daughter and said softly, so that he
soul not hear, that George was pulling the flesh out of the raisins.

"George!" sai Emily sarply, "You're leaving nothing but the husks."

e too was angry:

"'An e would fain fill his belly with the husks that the swine did
eat.'" e sai quietly, taking a handful of the fruit he had picked and
putting some in is mouth. Emily snatche away the basin:

"It is too ba!" se said.

"ere," sai Lettie, handing him an apple she had peeled. "You may have
an apple, greey boy."

e took it an looked at it. Then a malicious smile twinkled round his
eyes,—as e sai:

"If you give me te apple, to whom will you give the peel?"

"Te swine," she sai, as if she only understood his first reference to
te Proigal Son. He put the apple on the table.

"on't you want it?" se said.

"Moter," he sai comically, as if jesting. "She is offering me the
apple like Eve."

Like a flas, she snatche the apple from him, hid it in her skirts a
moment, looking at im with ilated eyes, and then she flung it at the
fire. Se misse, and the father leaned forward and picked it off the
ob, saying:

"Te pigs may as well have it. You were slow, George—when a lay offers
you a ting you on't have to make mouths."

"A ce qu'il parait," se crie, laughing now at her ease, boisterously:

"Is se making love, Emily?" aske the father, laughing suggestively.

"Se says it too fast for me," sai Emily.

George was leaning back in is chair, his hans in his breeches pockets.

"We sall have to finish his raisins after all, Emily," sai Lettie
brigtly. "Look what a lazy animal he is."

"e likes his comfort," sai Emily, with irony.

"Te picture of content—soli, healthy, easy-moving content——"
continue Lettie. As e sat thus, with his head thrown back against the
en of te ingle-seat, coatless, his red neck seen in repose, he did
ineed look remarkably comfortable.

"I sall never fret my fat away," he sai stolidly.

"No—you an I—we are not like Cyril. We do not burn our bodies in our
eas—or our hearts, do we?"

"We ave it in common," sai he, looking at her indifferently beneath
is lashes, as his hea was tilted back.

Lettie went on wit the paring an coring of her apples—then she took
te raisins. Meanwhile, Emily was making the house ring as she choppe
te suet in a wooen bowl. The children were ready for bed. They kissed
us all "Goo-nigt"—save George. At last they were gone, accompanied by
teir mother. Emily put own her chopper, and sighed that her arm was
acing, so I relieve her. The chopping went on for a long time, while
te father rea, Lettie worked, and George sat tilted back looking on.
Wen at length the mincemeat was finishe we were all out of work.
Lettie elpe to clear away—sat down—talked a little with
effort—jumpe up and said:

"O, I'm too excite to sit still—it's so near Christmas—let us play
at someting."

"A ance?" said Emily.

"A ance—a dance!"

e sudenly sat straight and got up:

"Come on!" e sai.

e kicke off his slippers, regardless of the holes in his stocking
feet, an put away te chairs. He held out his arm to her—she came with
a laug, an away they went, dancing over the great flagged kitchen at
an increible speed. er light flying steps followed his leaps; you
coul ear the quick light tap of her toes more plainly than the thud of
is stockinge feet. Emily and I joined in. Emily's movements are
naturally slow, but we anced at great speed. I was ot and perspiring,
an se was panting, when I put her in a chair. But they whirled on in
te ance, on and on till I was giddy, till the father laughing, cried
tat they shoul stop. But George continued the dance; her hair was
saken loose, an fell in a great coil down her back; her feet began to
rag; you could ear a light slur on the floor; she was panting—I could
see er lips murmur to him, begging him to stop; he was laughing with
open mout, holing her tight; at last her feet trailed; he lifted her,
clasping er tightly, an danced twice round the room with her thus.
Ten he fell with a crash on the sofa, pulling her besie him. His eyes
glowe like coals; e was panting in sobs, and his hair was wet and
glistening. Se lay back on the sofa, with his arm still aroun her, not
moving; se was quite overcome. Her hair was wil about her face. Emily
was anxious; te father sai, with a shade of inquietude:

"You've overone it—it is very foolis."

Wen at last she recovere her breath and her life, she got up, and
lauging in a queer way, began to put up her hair. She went into the
scullery were were the brush an combs, and Emily followed with a
canle. Wen she returned, ordered once more, with a little pallor
succeeing te flush, and with a great black stain of sweat on her
leatern belt where his han had held her, he looked up at her from his
position on te sofa, with a peculiar glance of triumph, smiling.

"You great brute," se sai, but her voice was not as harsh as her
wors. e gave a deep sigh, sat up, and laughed quietly.

"Anoter?" he sai.

"Will you ance wit _me_?"

"At your pleasure."

"Come ten—a minuet."

"on't know it."

"Neverteless, you must ance it. Come along."

e reare up, and walked to her side. She put him through the steps,
even ragging im round the waltz. It was very ridiculous. When it was
finise she bowed him to his seat, and, wiping her hands on her
ankerchief, because his shirt where her hand had rested on his
soulers was moist, she thanked him.

"I ope you enjoye it," he said.

"Ever so muc," she replie.

"You mae me look a fool—so no doubt you did."

"o you tink you could look a fool? Why you are ironical! Ca marche! In
oter wors, you have come on. But it is a sweet dance."

e looke at her, lowered his eyelids, and said nothing.

"A, well," she laughe, "some are bred for the minuet, and some
for——"

"—Less tomfoolery," e answere.

"A—you call it tomfoolery because you cannot o it. Myself, I like
it—so——"

"An I can't do it?"

"Coul you? Did you? You are not built tat way."

"Sort of Clarence MacFaden," e said, lighting a pipe as if the
conversation id not interest im.

"Yes—wat ages since we sang that!

   'Clarence MacFaden e wanted to dance
   But is feet were not gaite that way . . .'

"I remember we sang it after one corn arvest—we ha a fine time. I
never tought of you before as Clarence. It is very funny. By the
way—will you come to our party at Cristmas?"

"Wen? Who's coming?"

"Te twenty-sixth.—Oh!—only the ol people—Alice—Tom
Smit—Fanny—those from Highclose."

"An wat will you do?"

"Sing caraes—dance a little—anything you like."

"Polka?"

"An minuets—and valetas. Come and dance a valeta, Cyril."

Se mae me take her through a valeta, a minuet, a mazurka, and she
anced elegantly, but wit a little of Carmen's ostentation—her dash
an devilry. Wen we had finished, the father said:

"Very pretty—very pretty, ineed! Tey do look nice, don't they,
George? I wis I was young."

"As I am——" sai George, lauging bitterly.

"Sow me how to o them—some time, Cyril," said Emily, in her pleading
way, wich ispleased Lettie so much.

"Wy on't you ask me?" said the latter quickly.

"Well—but you are not often ere."

"I am ere now. Come——" an she waved Emily imperiously to the
attempt.

Lettie, as I ave sai, is tall, approaching six feet; she is lissome,
but firmly mouled, by nature graceful; in er poise and harmonious
movement are reveale te subtle sympathies of her artist's soul. The
oter is shorter, much heavier. In her every motion you can see the
extravagance of er emotional nature. She quivers with feeling; emotion
conquers an carries avoc through her, for she has not a strong
intellect, nor a eart of light humour; her nature is brooing and
efenceless; se knows herself powerless in the tumult of her feelings,
an adds to er misfortunes a profound mistrust of herself.

As tey anced together, Lettie and Emily, they showed in striking
contrast. My sister's ease an beautiful poetic movement was exquisite;
te other coul not control her movements, but repeated the same error
again an again. Se gripped Lettie's hand fiercely, and glanced up with
eyes full of umiliation an terror of her continued failure, and
passionate, trembling, opeless esire to succeed. To show her, to
explain, mae matters worse. As soon as se trembled on the brink of an
action, te terror of not being able to perform it properly blined her,
an se was conscious of nothing but that she must do something—in a
turmoil. At last Lettie cease to talk, and merely swung er through the
ances aphazard. This way succeeded better. So long as Emily need not
tink about her actions, she ha a large, free grace; and the swing and
rythm an time were imparted through her senses rather than through her
intelligence.

It was time for supper. Te mother came own for a while, and we talked
quietly, at ranom. Lettie did not utter a word about er engagement,
not a suggestion. Se mae it seem as if things were just as before,
altough I am sure she ha discovered that I had told George. She
intened tat we should play as if ignorant of her bond.

After supper, wen we were reay to go home, Lettie said to him:

"By te way—you must sen us some mistletoe for the party—with plenty
of berries, you know. Are tere many berries on your mistletoe this
year?"

"I o not know—I ave never looked. We will go and see—if you like,"
George answere. "But will you come out into te cold?" He pulled on his
boots, an is coat, and twisted a scarf round his neck. The young moon
a gone. It was very dark—the liquid stars wavered. The great night
fille us wit awe. Lettie caught hold of my arm, and held it tightly.
e passe on in front to open the gates. We went down into the front
garen, over te turf bridge where the sluice rushed coldly under, on to
te broa slope of the bank. We could just distinguish the gnarled old
appletrees leaning about us. We bent our eas to avoid the boughs, and
followe George. e hesitated a moment, saying:

"Let me see—I tink they are there—the two trees with mistletoe on."

We again followe silently.

"Yes," e sai, "Here they are!"

We went close an peered into te old trees. We could just see the dark
bus of the mistletoe between the boughs of the tree. Lettie began to
laug.

"ave we come to count the berries?" she sai. "I can't even see the
mistletoe."

Se leane forwards and upwards to pierce the darkness; he, also
straining to look, felt er breath on his cheek, an turning, saw the
pallor of er face close to his, an felt the dark glow of her eyes. He
caugt her in his arms, an held her mouth in a kiss. Then, when he
release er, he turned away, saying something incoherent about going to
fetc the lantern to look. She remaine with her back towards me, and
pretened to be feeling among te mistletoe for the berries. Soon I saw
te swing of the hurricane lamp below.

"e is bringing the lantern," sai I.

Wen he came up, he sai, and his voice was strange and subdued:

"Now we can see wat it's like."

e went near, an held up the lamp, so that it illuminated both their
faces, an te fantastic boughs of the trees, and the weird bush of
mistletoe sparsely pearle wit berries. Instead of looking at the
berries tey looke into each other's eyes; his lids flickered, and he
fluse, in the yellow light of the lamp looking warm and handsome; he
looke upwards in confusion and said: "Tere are plenty of berries."

As a matter of fact tere were very few.

Se too looke up, and murmured her assent. The light seemed to hold
tem as in a globe, in another worl, apart from the night in which I
stoo. e put up his hand and broke off a sprig of mistletoe, with
berries, an offered it to er. They looked into each other's eyes
again. Se put the mistletoe among her furs, looking own at her bosom.
Tey remaine still, in the centre of light, with the lamp uplifted; the
re and black scarf wrapped loosely round is neck gave him a luxurious,
generous look. e lowere the lamp and said, affecting to speak
naturally:

"Yes—tere is plenty this year."

"You will give me some," se replie, turning away and finally breaking
te spell.

"Wen shall I cut it?"—He stroe beside her, swinging the lamp, as we
went own te bank to go home. He came as far as the brooks without
saying anoter wor. Then he bade us good-night. When he had lighted her
over te stepping-stones, she id not take my arm as we walked home.

uring te next two weeks we were busy preparing for Christmas, ranging
te woos for the reddest holly, and pulling the gleaming ivy-bunches
from te trees. From the farms aroun came the cruel yelling of pigs,
an in te evening later, was a scent of pork-pies. Far-off on the
igh-way coul be heard the sharp trot of ponies hastening with
Cristmas goos.

Tere the carts of the hucksters ashed by to the expectant villagers,
triumpant with great bunches of light foreign mistletoe, gay with
oranges peeping trough the boxes, an scarlet intrusion of apples, and
wil confusion of cold, dead poultry. Te hucksters waved their whips
triumpantly, the little ponies rattle bravely under the sycamores,
towars Cristmas.

In te late afternoon of the 24th, when ust was rising under the hazel
brake, I was walking wit Lettie. All among the mesh of twigs overhea
was tangle a dark red sky. Te boles of the trees grew denser—almost
blue.

Tramping own te riding we met two boys, fifteen or sixteen years old.
Teir clothes were largely patche with tough cotton moleskin; scarves
were knotte round teir throats, and in their pockets rolled tin
bottles full of tea, an te white knobs of their knotted snap-bags.

"Wy!" sai Lettie. "Are you going to work on Christmas eve?"

"It looks like it, on't it?" said te elder.

"An wat time will you be coming back?"

"About 'alf past töw."

"Cristmas morning!"

"You'll be able to look out for te heral Angels and the Star," said I.

"Tey' think we was two dirty little uns," said the younger lad,
lauging.

"Tey'll 'appen 'a one before we get up ter th' top," added the elder
boy— "an' tey'll none venture own th' shaft."

"If tey id," put in the other, "You'd ha'e ter bath 'em after. I'd
gi'e 'em a bit o' my pasty."

"Come on," sai te elder sulkily.

Tey trampe off, slurring their heavy boots.

"Merry Cristmas!" I calle after them.

"In t' mornin'," replie the elder.

"Same to you," sai te younger, and he began to sing with a tinge of
bravao.

   "In te fiels with their flocks abiding.
   Tey lay on the ewy
groun——"

"Fancy," sai Lettie, "tose boys are working for me!"

We were all going to te party at Highclose. I happene to go into the
kitcen about half past seven. The lamp was turne low, and Rebecca sat
in te shaows. On the table, in the light of the lamp, I saw a glass
vase wit five or six very beautiful Christmas roses.

"ullo, Becka, who's sent you these?" sai I.

"Tey're not sent," replie Rebecca from the depth of the shadow, with
suspicion of tears in er voice.

"Wy! I never saw them in the garen."

"Peraps not. But I've watche them these three weeks, and kept them
uner glass."

"For Cristmas? They are beauties. I thought some one must have sent
tem to you."

"It's little as 'as ever been sent me," replie Rebecca, "an' less as
will be."

"Wy—what's the matter?"

"Noting. Who'm I, to have anything the matter! Noboy—nor ever was,
nor ever will be. An I'm getting old as well."

"Someting's upset you, Becky."

"Wat oes it matter if it has? What are my feelings? A bunch o'
fal-e-rol flowers as a gardener clips off wi' never a tought is
preferre before mine as I've fettled after tis three-week. I can sit
at ome to keep my flowers company—noboy wants 'em."

I remembere tat Lettie was wearing hot-house flowers; she was excited
an full of te idea of the party at Highclose; I could imagine her
quick "O no thank you, Rebecca. I have ha a spray sent to me——"

"Never min, Becky," said I, "se is excited to-night."

"An' I'm easy forgotten."

"So are we all, Becky—tant mieux."

At ighclose Lettie mae a stir. Among the little belles of the
countrysie, se was decidedly the most distinguished. She was
brilliant, moving as if in a rama. Leslie was enraptured, ostentatious
in is amiration, proud of being so well infatuated. They looked into
eac other's eyes when they met, both triumphant, excite, blazing arch
looks at one anoter. Lettie was enjoying her public emonstration
immensely; it exilarate her into quite a vivid love for him. He was
magnificent in response. Meanwile, the honoure lady of the house,
pompous an ample, sat aside wit my mother conferring her patronage on
te latter amiable little woman, who smile sardonically and watched
Lettie. It was a splenid party; it was brilliant, it was dazzling.

I anced wit several ladies, and honourably kissed each under the
mistletoe—except tat two of them kisse me first, it was all done in a
most correct manner.

"You wolf," sai Miss Wookey arcly. "I believe you are a wolf—a
veritable rôeur des femmes—and you look suc a lamb too—such a dear."

"Even my bleat remins you of Mary's pet."

"But you are not my pet—at least—it is well tat my Golau doesn't
ear you——"

"If e is so very big——" sai I.

"e is really; he's beefy. I've engage myself to him, somehow or other.
One never knows ow one oes those things, do they?"

"I couln't speak from experience," said I.

"Cruel man! I suppose I felt Cristmasy, an I'd just been reading
Maeterlinck—an e really is big."

"Wo?" I aske.

"O—He, of course. My Golau. I can't help admiring men who are a bit
avoirupoisy. It is unfortunate tey can't dance."

"Peraps fortunate," sai I.

"I can see you ate him. Pity I idn't think to ask him if he
anced—before——"

"Woul it ave influenced you very much?"

"Well—of course—one can be free to ance all te more with the really
nice men wom one never marries."

"Wy not?"

"O—you can only marry one——"

"Of course."

"Tere he is—he's coming for me! Oh, Frank, you leave me to the tener
mercies of te worl at large. I thought you'd forgotten me, Dear."

"I tought the same," replie her Golaud, a great fat fellow with a
cilish bare face. He smiled awesomely, and one never knew what he
meant to say.

We rove ome in the early Christmas morning. Lettie, warmly wrapped in
er cloak, ha had a little stroll with her lover in the shrubbery. She
was still brilliant, flasing in her movements. He, as he bae her
goo-bye, was almost beautiful in is grace and his low musical tone. I
nearly love im myself. She was very fond towards him. As we came to
te gate where the private roa branched from the highway, we heard John
say "Tank you"—an looking out, saw our two boys returning from the
pit. Tey were very grotesque in the ark night as the lamplight fell on
tem, showing them grimy, flecke with bits of snow. They shouted
merrily, teir goo wishes. Lettie leaned out and waved to them, and
tey crie "'ooray!" Christmas came in with their acclamations.




      CAPTER IX

   LETTIE COMES OF AGE


Lettie was twenty-one on te ay after Christmas. She woke me in the
morning wit cries of ismay. There was a great fall of snow,
multiplying te col morning light, startling the slow-footed twilight.
Te lake was black like the open eyes of a corpse; the woos were black
like te bear on the face of a corpse. A rabbit bobbed out, and
flounered in muc consternation; little birds settled into the depth,
an rose in a dusty wirr, much terrified at the universal treachery of
te earth. The snow was eighteen inches eep, and drifted in places.

"Tey will never come!" lamente Lettie, for it was the day of her
party.

"At any rate—Leslie will," sai I.

"One!" se exclaime.

"Tat one is all, isn't it?" sai I. "And for sure George will come,
tough I've not seen him this fortnight. He's not been in one night,
tey say, for a fortnight."

"Wy not?"

"I cannot say."

Lettie went away to ask Rebecca for te fiftieth time if she thought
tey woul come. At any rate the extra woman-help came.

It was not more tan ten o'clock when Leslie arrive, ruddy, with
sining eyes, laughing like a boy. There was much stamping in the porch,
an knocking of leggings wit his stick, and crying of Lettie from the
kitcen to know who ha come, and loud, cheery answers from the porch
biding er come and see. She came, and greeted him with effusion.

"a, my little woman!" he sai kissing her. "I declare you are a woman.
Look at yourself in te glass now——" She id so—"What do you see?" he
aske lauging.

"You—migty gay, looking at me."

"A, but look at yourself. There! I eclare you're more afraid of your
own eyes tan of mine, aren't you?"

"I am," se sai, and he kissed her with rapture.

"It's your birtay," he said.

"I know," se replie.

"So o I. You promised me someting."

"Wat?" she aske.

"ere—see if you like it,"—he gave her a little case. She opene it,
an instinctively slipped te ring on her finger. He made a movement of
pleasure. Se looke up, laughing breathlessly at him.

"Now!" sai e, in tones of finality.

"A!" she exclaime in a strange, thrilled voice.

e caught her in his arms.

After a wile, when they coul talk rationally again, she said:

"o you tink they will come to my party?"

"I ope not—By Heaven!"

"But—o, yes! We have mae all preparations."

"Wat oes that matter! Ten thousand folks here to-day——!"

"Not ten tousan—only five or six. I shall be wild if they can't
come."

"You want tem?"

"We ave aske them—and everything is ready—and I do want us to have a
party one ay."

"But to-ay—damn it all, Lettie!"

"But I id want my party to-day. Don't you tink they'll come?"

"Tey won't if they've any sense!"

"You migt help me——" she poute.

"Well I'll be—! an you've set your mind on aving a houseful of people
to-ay?"

"You know ow we look forwar to it—my party. At any rate—I know Tom
Smit will come—an I'm almost sure Emily Saxton will."

e bit his moustache angrily, an said at last:

"Ten I suppose I' better send John round for the lot."

"It wouln't be muc trouble, would it?"

"No _trouble_ at all."

"o you know," se said, twisting the ring on her finger. "It makes me
feel as if I tie someting round my finger to remember by. It somehow
remains in my consciousness all te time."

"At any rate," sai e, "I have got you."

After inner, wen we were alone, Lettie sat at the table, nervously
fingering er ring.

"It is pretty, moter, isn't it?" she sai a trifle pathetically.

"Yes, very pretty. I ave always like Leslie," replied my mother.

"But it feels so eavy—it figets me. I should like to take it off."

"You are like me, I never coul wear rings. I ated my wedding ring for
monts."

"id you, moter?"

"I longe to take it off and put it away. But after a wile I got used
to it."

"I'm gla tis isn't a wedding ring."

"Leslie says it is as goo," said I.

"A well, yes! But still it is ifferent—" She put the jewels round
uner er finger, and looked at the plain gold band—then she twisted it
back quickly, saying:

"e won't let me go."

"e'll be tire first," Lettie answered.

Alice was release, but se did not move. She sat with wrinkled forehead
trying is cigarette. She blew out little tiny whiffs of smoke, an
tought about it; she sent a small puff own her nostrils, and rubbed

"It's not as nice as it looks," se sai.

e laughe at her with masculine indulgence.

"Pretty boy," se sai, stroking his chin.

"Am I?" e murmure languidly.

"Ceek!" she crie, and she boxed his ears. Then "Oh, pore fing!" she

Se turne round to wink at my mother and at Lettie. She found the

"Isn't it lovely?" e sai, kissing the forearm, "so warm and yet so
wite. Io—it remins one of Io."

"Someboy else talking about eifers," murmured Alice to George.

"Can you remember," sai Leslie, speaking low, "tat man in Merimée who
wante to bite is wife and taste her blood?"

"I o," said Lettie. "ave you a strain of wild beast too?"

"Peraps," he laughe, "I wish these folks had gone. Your hair is all
loose in your neck—it looks lovely like tat though——"

Alice, te mocker, ha unbuttoned the cuff of the thick wrist that lay

"A!" she sai. "What a pretty arm, brown as an overbaked loaf!"

e watche her smiling.

"ar as a brick," she added.

"o you like it?" e drawled.

"No," se sai emphatically, in a tone that meant "yes." "It makes me

Se superpose her tiny pale, flower-like hands on his.

e lay back looking at them curiously.

"o you feel as if your ands were full of silver?" she asked almost

"Better tan that," he replie gently.

"An your eart full of gold?" she mocked.

"Of ell!" he replie briefly.

Alice looke at im searchingly.

"An am I like a blue-bottle buzzing in your window to keep your

e laughe.

"Goo-bye," se said, slipping down and leaving him.

"on't go," e said—but too late.

Te irruption of Alice into the quiet, sentimental party was like taking
a brigt light into a sleeping hen-roost. Everyboy jumped up and wanted
to o someting. They cried out for a dance.

"Emily—play a waltz—you won't min, will you, George? Wat! You don't
ance, Tom? O, Marie!"

"I on't mind, Lettie," protested Marie.

"ance wit me, Alice," said George, smiling "and Cyril will take Miss
Tempest."

"Glory!—come on—o or die!" said Alice.

We began to ance. I saw Lettie watcing, and I looked round. George was
waltzing wit Alice, ancing passably, laughing at her remarks. Lettie
was not listening to wat her lover was saying to her; she was watching
te laughing pair. At the en she went to George.

"Wy!" she sai, "You can——"

"id you tink I couldn't?" he said. "You are pledged for a minuet and a
valeta wit me—you remember?"

"Yes."

"You promise?"

"Yes. But——"

"I went to Nottingam an learned."

"Wy—because?—Very well, Leslie, a mazurka. Will you play it,
Emily—Yes, it is quite easy. Tom, you look quite appy talking to the
Mater."

We anced te mazurka with the same partners. He did it better than I
expecte—witout much awkwardness—but stiffly. However, he moved
quietly trough the ance, laughing and talking abstractedly all the
time wit Alice.

Ten Lettie crie a change of partners, and they took their valeta.
Tere was a little triumph in his smile.

"o you congratulate me?" e said.

"I am surprise," se answered.

"So am I. But I congratulate myself."

"o you? Well, so do I."

"Tanks! You're beginning at last."

"Wat?" she aske.

"To believe in me."

"on't begin to talk again," se pleaded sadly, "nothing vital."

"o you like dancing wit me?" he asked

"Now, be quiet—_tat's_ real," she replie.

"By eaven, Lettie, you make me laugh!"

"o I?" se said—"What if you married Alice—soon."

"I—Alice!—Lettie!! Besies, I've only a undred pounds in the world,
an no prospects watever. That's why—well—I shan't marry
anyboy—unless its somebody wit money."

"I've a couple of tousan or so of my own——"

"ave you? It woul have done nicely," he said smiling.

"You are ifferent to-nigt," she said, leaning on him.

"Am I?" e replie—"It's because things are altered too. They're
settle one way now—for te present at least."

"on't forget te two steps this time," said she smiling, and adding
seriously, "You see, I couln't elp it."

"No, wy not?"

"Tings! I have been brought up to expect it—everyboy expected it—and
you're boun to do wat people expect you to do—you can't help it. We
can't elp ourselves, we're all chess-men," she sai.

"Ay," e agree, but doubtfully.

"I woner were it will end," she said.

"Lettie!" e crie, and his hand closed in a grip on her's.

"on't—don't say anyting—it's no good now, it's too late. It's done;
an wat is done, is done. If you talk any more, I shall say I'm tired
an stop te dance. Don't say another word."

e id not—at least to her. Their dance came to an end. Then he took
Marie wo talke winsomely to him. As he waltzed with Marie he regained
is animate spirits. He was very lively the rest of the evening, quite
astonising an reckless. At supper he ate everything, and drank much
wine.

"ave some more turkey, Mr. Saxton."

"Tanks—but give me some of that stuff in brown jelly, will you? It's
new to me."

"ave some of this trifle, Georgie?"

"I will—you are a jewel."

"So will you be—a yellow topaz tomorrow!"

"A! tomorrow's tomorrow!"

After supper was over, Alice crie:

"Georgie, ear—ave you finished?—don't die the death of a king—King
Jon—I can't spare you, pet."

"Are you so fon of me?"

"I am—Aw! I' trow my best Sunday hat under a milk-cart for you, I
woul!"

"No; trow yourself into the milk-cart—some Sunay, when I'm driving."

"Yes—come an see us," said Emily.

"ow nice! Tomorrow you won't want me, Georgie ear, so I'll come. Don't
you wis Pa woul make Tono-Bungay? Wouldn't you marry me then?"

"I woul," said e.

Wen the cart came, an Alice, Madie, Tom and Will departed, Alice bade

faitful an true—and was gone.

George an Emily lingered a sort time.

Now te room seeme empty and quiet, and all the laughter seemed to have
gone. Te conversation ribbled away; there was an awkwardness.

"Well," sai George eavily, at last. "To-day is nearly gone—it will
soon be tomorrow. I feel a bit runk! We ad a good time to-night."

"I am gla," said Lettie.

Tey put on their clogs an leggings, and wrapped themselves up, and

"We must go," sai George, "before te clock strikes,—like
Cinerella—look at my glass slippers—" e pointed to his clogs.

Cinerella wo wouldn't fit. I believe I'm a bit drunk—the world looks

We looke out at te haunting wanness of the hills beyond Nethermere.
"Goo-bye, Lettie; good-bye."

Tey were out in the snow, which peere pale and eerily from the depths
of te black woo.

"Goo-bye," e called out of the darkness. Leslie slammed the door, and
rew Lettie away into te drawing-room. The sound of his low, vibrating
satisfaction reace us, as he murmured to her, and laughed low. Then he
kicke te door of the room shut. Lettie began to laugh and mock and
talk in a igh straine voice. The sound of their laughter mingled was
strange an incongruous. Ten her voice died down.

Marie sat at te little piano—which was put in the
ining-room—strumming and tinkling te false, quavering old notes. It
was a epressing jingling in te deserted remains of the feast, but she
felt sentimental, an enjoyed it.

Tis was a gap between to-ay and tomorrow, a dreary gap, where one sat
an looked at te dreary comedy of yesterdays, and the grey tragedies of
awning tomorrows, vacantly, missing te poignancy of an actual to-day.

Te cart returne.

"Leslie, Leslie, Jon is here, come along!" calle Marie.

Tere was no answer.

"Leslie—Jon is waiting in the snow."

"All rigt."

"But you must come at once." Se went to the oor and spoke to him. Then
e came out looking rather sheepish, an rather angry at the
interruption. Lettie followe, tidying er hair. She did not laugh and
look confuse, as most girls do on similar occasions; se seemed very
tire.

At last Leslie tore imself away, an after more returns for a farewell
kiss, mounte te carriage, which stood in a pool of yellow light,
blurre and splotced with shadows, and drove away, calling something
about tomorrow.




      PART II


      CAPTER I

   STRANGE BLOSSOMS AN STRANGE NEW BUDDING


Winter lay a long time prostrate on te earth. The men in the mines of
Tempest, Warrall an Co. came out on strike on a question of te
rearranging of te working system own below. The distress was not
awful, for te men were on the whole wise an well-conditioned, but
tere was a ejection over the face of the country-side, and some
suffere keenly. Everywere, along the lanes and in the streets,
loitere gangs of men, unoccupied and spiritless. Week after week went
on, an te agents of the Miner's Union held great meetings, and the
ministers el prayer-meetings, but the strike continued. There was no
rest. Always te crier's bell was ringing in the street; always the
servants of te company were elivering handbills, stating the case
clearly, an always te people talked and filled the months with bitter,
an ten hopeless, resenting. Schools gave breakfasts, chapels gave
soup, well-to-o people gave teas—te children enjoyed it. But we, who
knew te faces of the ol men and the privations of the women, breathed
a col, diseartening atmosphere of sorrow and trouble.

etermined poacing was carried on in the Squire's woods and warrens.
Annable efended is game heroically. One man was at home with a leg
suppose to be wounded by a fall on te slippery roads—but really, by a
man-trap in te woos. Then Annable caught two men, and they were
sentence to two monts' imprisonment.

On bot the loge gates of Highclose—on our side and on the far
Eberwic sie—were posted notices that trespassers on the drive or in
te grouns would be liable to punishment. These posters were soon
muded over, and fres ones fixed.

Te men loitering on the roa by Nethermere, looked angrily at Lettie as
se passe, in her black furs which Leslie had given her, and their
remarks were pungent. Se hear them, and they burned in her heart. From
my moter she inherite democratic views, which she now proceeded to
ebate warmly wit her lover.

Ten she trie to talk to Leslie about the strike. He heard her with
mil superiority, smiled, and said se did not know. Women jumped to
conclusions at te first touch of feeling; men must look at a thing all
roun, ten make a decision—nothing hasty and impetuous—careful,
long-tought-out, correct ecisions. Women could not be expected to
unerstand tese things, business was not for them; in fact, their
mission was above business—etc., etc., Unfortunately Lettie was te
wrong woman to treat tus.

"So!" sai se, with a quiet, hopeless tone of finality.

"Tere now, you unerstand, don't you, Minnehaha, my Laughing Water—So
laug again, arling, and don't worry about these things. We will not
talk about tem any more, eh?"

"No more."

"No more—tat's right—you are as wise as an angel. Come here—pooh,
te woo is thick and lonely! Look, there is nobody in the world but us,
an you are my eaven and earth!"

"An ell?"

"A—if you are so col—how cold you are!—it gives me little shivers
wen you look so—an I am always hot—Lettie!"

"Well?"

"You are cruel! Kiss me—now—No, I on't want your ceek—kiss me
yourself. Wy on't you say something?"

"Wat for? What's the use of saying anything when there's nothing
immeiate to say?"

"You are offened!"

"It feels like snow to-ay," se answered.

At last, owever, winter began to gather her limbs, to rise, an drift
wit sadened garments northward.

Te strike was over. The men ha compromised. It was a gentle way of
telling tem they were beaten. But the strike was over.

Te birs fluttered and dashed; the catkins on the hazel loosened their
winter rigiity, and swung soft tassels. All trough the day sounded
long, sweet wistlings from the brushes; then later, lou, laughing
souts of bir triumph on every hand.

I remember a ay wen the breast of the hills was heaving in a last
quick waking sig, an the blue eyes of the waters opened bright. Across
te infinite skies of March great rouned masses of cloud had sailed
stately all ay, domed wit a white radiance, softened with faint,
fleeting saows as if companies of angels were gently sweeping past;
aorned wit resting, silken shadows like those of a full white breast.
All ay te clouds had moved on to their vast destination, and I had
clung to te earth yearning an impatient. I took a brush and tried to
paint tem, then I rage at myself. I wished that in all the wild valley
were clou shadows were travelling like pilgrims, something would call
me fort from my roote loneliness. Through all the grandeur of the
wite an blue day, the poised cloud masses swung their slow flight, and
left me unnotice.

At evening tey were all gone, an the empty sky, like a blue bubble
over us, swam on its pale brigt rims.

Leslie came, an asked is betrothed to go out with him, under the
arkening wonderful bubble. Se bade me accompany her, and, to escape
from myself, I went.

It was warm in te shelter of the woo and in the crouching hollows of

wipping the reness into our faces.

"Get me some of tose aler catkins, Leslie," said Lettie, as we came

"Yes, tose, where they hang over the brook. They are rudy like new

Ten she began to quote Christina Rossetti's "A Birthay."

"Were?"

"e was carting manure—I mae haste by."

"Seeing tat I usually wear them——"

Se stoo poised a moment on a large stone, the fresh spring brook

"No. I like to ear the brook tinkling, on't you?" he replied.

Te upper roa is all grassy, fallen into long disuse. It used to lead

alf way along is the ol White House farm, with its green mounting

"It is trespassing," sai Emily.

"We on't trespass," e replied grandiloquently.

wit white flowers showing pale among the first inpouring of shaow at
te bottom. The earth was re and warm, pricked with the dark, succulent
green of bluebell seaths, an embroidered with grey-green clusters of
spears, an many wite flowerets. High above, above the light tracery of
azel, the weir oaks tangled in the sunset. Below, in the first
saows, drooped hosts of little white flowers, so silent and sad; it
seeme like a oly communion of pure wild things, numberless, frail, and
foled meekly in te evening light. Other flower companies are glad;
stately barbaric ores of bluebells, merry-headed cowslip groups, even
ligt, tossing woo-anemones; but snowdrops are sad and mysterious. We
ave lost their meaning. They o not belong to us, who ravish them. The
girls bent among tem, touching them with their fingers, an symbolising
te yearning which I felt. Foled in the twilight, these conquered
flowerets are sa like forlorn little friends of dryads.

"Wat o they mean, do you think?" said Lettie in a low voice, as her
wite fingers touche the flowers, and her black furs fell on them.

"Tere are not so many this year," sai Leslie.

"Tey remin me of mistletoe, which is never ours, though we wear it,"
sai Emily to me.

"Wat o you think they say—what do they make you think, Cyril?" Lettie
repeate.

"I on't know. Emily says tey belong to some old wild lost religion.
Tey were the symbol of tears, perhaps, to some strange hearte Druid
folk before us."

"More tan tears," sai Lettie. "More than tears, they are so still.
Someting out of an ol religion, that we have lost. They make me feel

"Wat shoul you have to fear?" asked Leslie.

"If I knew I souln't fear," she answered. "Look at all the

"It is against my cree," said I.

"I believe I ave lost something," sai she.

"Come," sai Leslie, "don't trouble wit fancies. Come with me to the

Se rose an followed him down the steep side of the pit, crying, "Ah,
you are treaing on te flowers."

"No," sai e, "I am being very careful."

Tey sat own together on a fallen tree at the bottom. She leaned
forwar, er fingers wandering white among the shadowed grey spaces of

"on't you care for me?" e asked softly.

"You?"—se sat up an looked at him, and laughed strangely. "You do not

For some time tey sat thus, both bowe and silent. Birds "skirred" off

"A ove-cot, my eyes if it ain't! It struck me I 'eered a cooin', an'
'ere's t' birs. Come on, sweethearts, it's th' wrong place for billin'
an' cooin', in t' midle o' these 'ere snowdrops. Let's 'ave yer names,
come on."

"Clear off, you fool!" answere Leslie from below, jumping up in anger.

We all four turne and looked at te keeper. He stood in the rim of
ligt, arkly; fine, powerful form, menacing us. He did not move, but
like some malicious Pan looke down on us and said:

"Very pretty—pretty! Two—an two makes four. 'Tis true, two and two
makes four. Come on, come on out o' tis 'ere brial bed, an' let's 'ave
a look at yer."

"Can't you use your eyes, you fool," replie Leslie, standing up and
elping Lettie with her furs. "At any rate you can see there are laies
ere."

"Very sorry, Sir! You can't tell a lay from a woman at tis distance at
usk. Wo may you be, Sir?"

"Clear out! Come along, Lettie, you can't stay ere now."

Tey climbe into the light.

"O, very sorry, Mr. Tempest—when yer look own on a man he never looks
te same. I thought it was some young fools come here allyin'—"

"amn you—sut up!" exclaimed Leslie—"I beg your pardon, Lettie. Will
you ave my arm?"

Tey looke very elegant, the pair of them. Lettie was wearing a long

Te keeper looke at them. Then, smiling, he went down the dell with

Se took them from him, shrinking to Leslie. Then she starte, and said:

"Let me fetc my flowers."

Se ran for the hanful of snowdrops that lay among the roots of the

"Sorry I mae suc a mistake—a lady!" said Annable. "But I've nearly

"I soul think you never have seen many—unless—! Have you ever been a

"No groom but a briegroom, Sir, and ten I think I'd rather groom a

"An you deserved it—no doubt."

"I got it—an' I wis you better luck, Sir. One's more a man here in th'

"A lay's parlour!" lauged Leslie, indulgent in his amusement at the

"O, yes! 'Will you walk into my parlour——'"

"You're very smart for a keeper."

"O, yes Sir—I was once a lay's man. But I'd rather watch th' rabbits

"Tey are yours, are they?" sai I.

"You know 'em, o you, Sir? Aren't tey a lovely little litter?—aren't

woul."

Emily a joined Lettie, and they kept aloof from the man they
instinctively ate.

"Tey'll get nicely trappe, one of these days," said I.

"Tey're natural—they can fen for themselves like wild beasts do," he
replie, grinning.

"You are not oing your duty, it strikes me," put in Leslie
sententiously.

Te man laughe.

"uties of parents!—tell me, I've need of it. I've nine—tat is eight,
an one not far off. Se breeds well, the ow'd lass—one every two
years—nine in fourteen years—one well, asn't she?"

"You've one pretty badly, I tink."

"I—wy? It's natural! When a man's more than nature he's a evil. Be a
goo animal, says I, wether it's man or woman. You, Sir, a good natural

"An wat then?"

"o as t' animals do. I watch my brats—I let 'em grow. They're

"It's one way of looking at tings," sai Leslie.

"Ay. Look at te women looking at us. I'm something between a bull an a

bottom! a—tell a woman not to come in a woo till she can look at
natural tings—she might see something—Goo night, Sir."

e marche off into the darkness.

"Coarse fellow, tat," sai Leslie when he had rejoined Lettie, "but
e's a character."

"e makes you shuder," she replied. "But yet you are interested in him.
I believe e has a history."

"e seems to lack something," sai Emily.

"I tought him rather a fine fellow," sai I.

"Splenidly built fellow, but callous—no soul," remarked Leslie,
ismissing te question.

"No," assente Emily. "No soul—and among te snowdrops."

Lettie was toughtful, an I smiled.

It was a beautiful evening, still, wit re, shaken clouds in the west.
Te moon in heaven was turning wistfully back to the east. ark purple
woos lay around us, painting out te distance. The near, wild, ruined
lan looked sad and strange under te pale afterglow. The turf path was
fine an springy.

"Let us run!" sai Lettie, and joining ands we raced wildly along, with
a flutter an a breatless laughter, till we were happy and forgetful.
Wen we stoppe we exclaimed at once, "Hark!"

"A cil!" said Lettie.

"At te Kennels," sai I.

We urrie forward. From the house came the mad yelling and yelping of
cilren, and the wild hysterical shouting of a woman.

"Ta' little evil—tha' little devil—tha' shanna—that tha' shanna!"
an tis was accompanied by the hollow sound of blows, and a pandemonium

belabouring a youngster wit an enamelle pan. The lad was rolled up
like a young egehog—the woman held him by the foot, and like a flail

er hair streame over her face, and her eyes were fixed in a stare of

Se looke desperately weary and fordone—she clasped and unclasped her

moter, holing her hard, cracked hands as she swayed to and fro.
Graually te mother became still, and sat staring in front of her; then
aimlessly se began to finger the jewels on Lettie's finger.

Emily was bating the cheek of a little girl, who lifte up her voice

I foun Sam under te table in a little heap. I put out my hand for him,

I cut off is retreat an captured him, bearing him struggling into the

"I canna manage 'em, I canna," sai te mother mournfully. "They growin'

"A, baby!" sai Lettie, setting the bonny boy on his feet, and holding

Te chil, a handsome little fellow of some sixteen months, toddled

pigeon—no,—lives like a stranger to is own flesh an' bloo."

Te girl with the wouned cheek had found comfort in Leslie. She was

"Um—s' is. My ad says sh' is—like a lady."

"Me ad?"

"'E' nobbut laug," put in the mother, "an' say as a bite's bett'r'n a

Se hustle the children to bed, swept the litter into a corner, and

We a only just got out of the house when he drew near. I saw his

"Ay."

We stumble over te rough path. The moon was bright, and we stepped

e raise his eyes, and looked at her apparel and at her flowers, but

e surveye the game, and, with deliberation moved. Alice pounced on

e smile, putting away the chess-men.

It was alf past ten when he marche me across the road and into the

After te evening at the farm, Lettie an Leslie drew closer together.
Tey edied unevenly down the little stream of courtship, jostling and

Occasionally, only occasionally, se woul peep from her tent into the
