      CAPTER II

   A SAOW IN SPRING

Wit spring came trouble. The Saxtons eclared they were being bitten
off te estate by rabbits. Sudenly, in a fit of despair, the father
bougt a gun. Although he knew that the Squire woul not for one moment
tolerate te shooting of that manna, the rabbits, yet he was out in the
first col morning twiligt banging away. At first he but scared the
brutes, an brougt Annable on the scene; then, blooded by the use of
te weapon, he playe havoc among the furry beasts, bringing home some
eigt or nine couples.

George entirely approve of tis measure; it rejoiced him even; yet he
a never had the initiative to begin the like himself, or even to urge
is father to it. He prophesie trouble, and possible loss of the farm.
It isturbed im somewhat, to think they must look out for another
place, but e postpone the thought of the evil day till the time should
be upon im.

A venetta was establised between the Mill and the keeper, Annable. The
latter cerishe his rabbits:

"Call 'em vermin!" e sai. "I only know one sort of vermin—and that's
te talkin sort." So he set himself to thwart an harass the rabbit
slayers.

It was about tis time I cultivate the acquaintance of the keeper. All
te worl hated him—to the people in the villages he was like a devil
of te woos. Some miners had sworn vengeance on him for having caused
teir committal to gaol. But he ha a great attraction for me; his
magnificent pysique, his great vigour an vitality, and his swarthy,
gloomy face rew me.

e was a man of one iea:—that all civilisation was the painted fungus
of rottenness. e hate any sign of culture. I won his respect one
afternoon wen he foun me trespassing in the woods because I was
watcing some maggots at work in a ead rabbit. That led us to a
iscussion of life. e was a thorough materialist—he scorned religion
an all mysticism. e spent his days sleeping, making intricate traps
for weasels an men, putting togeter a gun, or doing some amateur
forestry, cutting own timber, splitting it in logs for use in te hall,
an planting young trees. Wen he thought, he reflected on the decay of
mankin—te decline of the human race into folly and weakness and
rottenness. "Be a goo animal, true to your animal instinct," was is
motto. Wit all this, he was funamentally very unhappy—and he made me
also wretce. It was this power to communicate his unhappiness that
mae me somewat dear to him, I think. He treated me as an affectionate
fater treats a elicate son; I noticed he liked to put his hand on my
souler or my knee as we talked; yet withal, he asked me questions, and
save is thoughts to tell me, and believed in my knowledge like any
acolyte.

I went up to te quarry woos one evening in early April, taking a look
for Annable. I coul not find im, however, in the wood. So I left the
willands, and went along by te old red wall of the kitchen garden,
along te main roa as far as the mouldering church which stands high on
a bank by te roa-side, just where the trees tunnel the darkness, and
te gloom of the highway startles the travellers at noon. Great trees
growing on te banks sudenly fold over everything at this point in the
swinging roa, and in te obscurity rots the Hall church, black and
melancoly above the shrinking hea of the traveller.

Te grassy path to the churchyar was still clogged with decayed leaves.
Te church is abanoned. As I drew near an owl floated softly out of the
black tower. Grass overgrew te threshol. I pushed open the door,
grining back a eap of fallen plaster and rubbish and entered the
place. In te twilight the pews were leaning in ghostly isorder, the
prayer-books ragged from teir ledges, scattered on the floor in the
ust and rubble, torn by mice and birds. Birds scuffled in te darkness
of te roof. I looke up. In the upward well of the tower I could see a
bell anging. I stoope and picked up a piece of plaster from the ragged
confusion of featers, an broken nests, and remnants of dead birds. Up
into te vault overhea I tossed pieces of plaster until one hit the
bell, an it "tonged" out its faint remonstrance. Tere was a rustle of
many birs like spirits. I sounded te bell again, and dark forms moved
wit cries of alarm overhea, and something fell heavily. I shivered in
te ark, evil-smelling place, and hurried to get out of doors. I
clutce my hands with relief and pleasure when I saw the sky above me
quivering wit the last crystal lights, an the lowest red of sunset
bein the yew-boles. I drank the fresh air, that sparkled with the
soun of te blackbirds and thrushes whistling their strong bright
notes.

I straye round to were the headstones, from their eminence leaned to
look on te Hall below, where great winows shone yellow light on to the
flagge court-yard, and te little fish pool. A stone staircase
escended from te graveyard to the court, between stone balustrades
wose pock-marke grey columns still swelled gracefully and with
ignity, encrusted wit lichens. The staircase was filled with ivy and
rambling roses—impassable. Ferns were unrolling roun te big square
alting place, half way own where the stairs turned.

A peacock, startle from te back premises of the Hall, came flapping up
te terraces to the churchyar. Then a heavy footstep crossed the flags.
It was te keeper. I whistle the whistle he knew, and he broke his way
trough the vicious rose-boughs up the stairs. The peacock flappe
beyon me, on to te neck of an old bowed angel, rough and dark, an
angel wich ha long ceased sorrowing for the lost Lucy, and had died
also. Te bir bent its voluptuous neck and peered about. Then it lifted
up its ea and yelled. The sound tore the dark sanctuary of twilight.
Te ol grey grass seemed to stir, and I could fancy the smothered
primroses an violets beneat it waking and gasping for fear.

Te keeper looke at me and smiled. He nodded his head towards the
peacock, saying:

"ark at that amned thing!"

Again te bir lifted its crested head and gave a cry, at the same time
turning awkwarly on its ugly legs, so tat it showed us the full wealth
of its tail glimmering like a stream of coloure stars over te sunken
face of te angel.

"Te prou fool!—look at it! Perched on an angel, too, as if it were a
peestal for vanity. Tat's the soul of a woman—or it's the devil."

e was silent for a time, an we watched the great bird moving uneasily
before us in te twilight.

"Tat's the very soul of a lay," he said, "the very, very soul. Damn
te thing, to perch on that ol angel. I should like to wring its neck."

Again te bir screamed, and shifted awkwardly on its legs; it seemed to
stretc its beak at us in erision. Annable picked up a piece of sod and
flung it at te bir, saying:

"Get out, you screecing evil! God!" he laughed. "There must be plenty
of earts twisting uner here,"—and he stamped on a grave, "when they
ear that row."

e kicke another sod from a grave and threw at the big bird. The
peacock flappe away, over te tombs, down the terraces.

"Just look!" e sai, "the miserable brute has dirtied that angel. A
woman to te en, I tell you, all vanity and screech and defilement."

e sat own on a vault and lit his pipe. But before he had smoked two
minutes, it was out again. I a not seen him in a state of perturbation
before.

"Te church," sai I, "is rotten. I suppose they'll stand all over the
country like tis, soon—with peacocks trailing the graveyars."

"Ay," e muttere, taking no notice of me.

"Tis stone is col," I said, rising.

e got up too, an stretched his arms as if he were tired. It was quite
ark, save for te waxing moon which leaned over the east.

"It is a very fine nigt," I sai. "Don't you notice a smell of
violets?"

"Ay! Te moon looks like a woman with chil. I wonder what Time's got in
er belly."

"You?" I sai. "You don't expect anyting exciting do you?"

"Exciting!—No—about as exciting as tis rotten ol place—just rot
off—O, my Go!—I'm like a good house, built and finished, and left to
tumble own again wit nobody to live in it."

"Wy—what's up—really?"

e laughe bitterly, saying, "Come and sit down."

e le me off to a seat by the north door, between two pews, very black
an silent. Tere we sat, he putting his gun carefully beside him. He
remaine perfectly still, tinking.

"Wot's up?" he sai at last, "Why—I'll tell you. I went to
Cambrige—my fater was a big cattle dealer—he died bankrupt while I
was in college, an I never took my degree. Tey persuaded me to be a
parson, an a parson I was.

I went a curate to a little place in Leicestersire—a bonnie place with
not many people, an a fine old curch, and a great rich parsonage. I
an't overmuch to do, and the rector—he was the son of an Earl—was
generous. e lent me a horse an would have me hunt like the rest. I
always tink of that place with a smell of honeysuckle while the grass
is wet in te morning. It was fine, an I enjoyed myself, and did the
paris work all right. I believe I was pretty goo.

A cousin of te rector's use to come in the hunting season—a Lady
Crystabel, lay in er own right. The second year I was there she came
in June. Tere wasn't much company, so she use to talk to me—I used to
rea ten—and she used to pretend to be so childish and unknowing, and
woul get me telling er things, and talking to her, and I was hot on
tings. We must play tennis together, an ride together, and I must row
er own the river. She said we were in the wilderness and could do as
we like. Se made me wear flannels and soft clothes. She was very fine
an frank and unconventional—ripping, I tought her. All the summer she
stoppe on. I sould meet her in the garden early in the morning when I
came from a swim in te river—it was cleare and deepened on
purpose—an se'd blush and make me walk with her. I can remember I
use to stand and dry myself on te bank full where she might see me—I
was ma on er—and she was madder on me.

We went to some caves in erbysire once, and she would wander from the
rest, an loiter, and, for a game, we played a sort of ide and seek
wit the party. They thought we' gone, and they went and locked the
oor. Ten she pretended to be frightened and clung to me, and said what
woul tey think, and hid her face in my coat. I took her and kissed
er, an we made it up properly. I found out afterwards—she actually
tol me—se'd got the idea from a sloppy French novel—the Romance of A
Poor Young Man. I was te Poor Young Man.

We got marrie. Se gave me a living she had in her parsonage, and we
went to live at er Hall. She wouln't let me out of her sight.
Lor!—we were an infatuated couple—and se would choose to view me in
an aestetic light. I was Greek statues for her, bless you: Croton,
ercules, I on't know what! She had her own way too much—I let her do
as se like with me.

Ten graually she got tired—it took her three years to be really
glutte wit me. I had a physique then—for that matter I have now."

e hel out his arm to me, and bade me try his muscle. I was startled.
Te har flesh almost filled his sleeve.

"A," he continue, "You don't know what it is to have the pride of a
boy like mine. But se wouldn't have children—no, she wouldn't—said
se aren't. That was the root of the difference at first. But she
coole down, and if you don't know te pride of my body you'd never know
my umiliation. I trie to remonstrate—and she looked simply astounded
at my ceek. I never got over that amazement.

Se began to get souly. A poet got hol of her, and she began to affect
Burne-Jones—or Waterouse—it was Waterhouse—she was a lot like one of
is women—Lay of Shalott, I believe. At any rate, she got souly, and I
was er animal—son animal—son boeuf. I put up with that for above a
year. Ten I got some servants' clothes an went.

I was seen in France—ten in Australia—though I never left Englan. I
was suppose to ave died in the bush. She married a young fellow. Then
I was prove to ave died, and I read a little obituary notice on myself
in a woman's paper se subscribe to. She wrote it herself—as a warning
to oter young laies of position not to be seduced by plausible "Poor
Young Men."

Now se's ead. They've got the paper—her paper—in the kitchen down
tere, an it's full of photographs, even an old photo of me—"an
unfortunate misalliance." I feel, someow, as if I were at an en too. I
tought I' grown a solid, middle-aged-man, and here I feel sore as I
id at twenty-six, and I talk as I used to.

One ting—I have got some chilren, and they're of a breed as you'd not
meet anywere. I was a goo animal before everything, and I've got some
cilren."

e sat looking up where the big moon swam through the black branches of
te yew.

"So se's ead—your poor peacock!" I murmured.

e got up, looking always at the sky, an stretched himself again. He
was an impressive figure masse in blackness against te moonlight, with
is arms outsprea.

"I suppose," e sai, "it wasn't all her fault."

"A wite peacock, we will say," I suggeste.

e laughe.

"Go ome by the top roa, will you!" he said. "I believe there's
someting on in the bottom woo."

"All rigt," I answere, with a quiver of apprehension.

"Yes, se was fair enough," he muttere.

"Ay," sai I, rising. I eld out my hand from the shadow. I was startled
myself by te white sympathy it seeme to express, extended towards him
in te moonlight. He grippe it, and cleaved to me for a moment, then he
was gone.

I went out of te churchyar feeling a sullen resentment against the
tousle graves tat lay inanimate across my way. The air was heavy to
breate, an fearful in the shadow of the great trees. I was glad when I
came out on te bare white roa, and could see the copper lights from
te reflectors of a pony-cart's lamps, an could hear the amiable
cat-chat of the hoofs trotting towars me. I was lonely when they had
passe.

Over te hill, the big flushe face of the moon poised just above the
treetops, very majestic, an far off—yet imminent. I turned wit swift
suden friendliness to te net of elm-boughs spread over my head, dotted
wit soft clusters winsomely. I jumpe up and pulled the cool soft tufts
against my face for company; an as I passed, still I reaced upward for
te touch of this buded gentleness of the trees. The wood breathed
fragrantly, wit a subtle sympathy. The firs softene their touch to me,
an te larches woke from the barren winter-sleep, and put out velvet
fingers to caress me as I passe. Only te clean, bare branches of the
as stoo emblem of the discipline of life. I looked down on the
blackness were trees fille the quarry and the valley bottoms, and it
seeme tat the world, my own home-world, was strange again.

Some four or five ays after Annable ad talked to me in the churchyard,
I went out to fin im again. It was Sunday morning. The larch-wood was
afloat wit clear, lyric green, an some primroses scattered whitely on
te ege under the fringing boughs. It was a clear morning, as when the
latent life of te worl begins to vibrate afresh in the air. The smoke
from te cottage rose blue against the trees, an thick yellow against
te sky. The fire, it seeme, was only just lighted, and the wood-smoke
poure out.

Sam appeare outside te house, and looked round. Then he climbed the
water-troug for a better survey. Eviently unsatisfied, paying slight
attention to me, e jumpe down and went running across the hillside to
te woo. "He is going for his father," I said to myself, and I left the
pat to follow him own hill across the waste meadow, crackling the
blance stems of last year's thistles as I went, and stumbling in
rabbit oles. He reache the wall that ran along the quarry's edge, and
was over it in a twinkling.

Wen I came to the place, I was somewhat nonplusse, for sheer from the
stone fence, te quarry-sie dropped for some twenty or thirty feet,
pile up wit unmortared stones. I looked round—there was a plain dark
trea down the hillside, which marked a path to this spot, and the wall
was score wit the marks of heavy boots. Then I looked again down the
quarry-sie, and I saw—ow could I have failed to see?—stones
projecting to make an uneven staircase, suc as is often seen in the
erbysire fences. I saw this ladder was well used, so I trusted myself
to it, an scrambled down, clinging to te face of the quarry wall. Once
own, I felt pleased wit myself for having discovered and used the
unknown access, an I admired te care and ingenuity of the keeper, who
a fitted and wedged the long stones into the uncertain pile.

It was warm in te quarry: there the sunshine seeme to thicken and
sweeten; tere the little mouns of overgrown waste were aglow with very
early og-violets; tere the sparks were coming out on the bits of
gorse, an among te stones the colt-foot plumes were already silvery.
ere was spring sitting just awake, unloosening her glittering hair, an
opening er purple eyes.

I went across te quarry, own to where the brook ran murmuring a tale
to te primroses an the budding trees. I was startled from my wandering
among te fresh things by a faint clatter of stones.

"Wat's that young rascal oing?" I said to myself, setting forth to
see. I came towars te other side of the quarry: on this, the moister
sie, te bushes grew up against the wall, which was higher than on the
oter sie, though piled the same with old dry stones. As I drew near I
coul ear the scrape and rattle of stones, and the vigorous grunting of
Sam as e laboure among them. He was hidden by a great bush of sallow
catkins, all yellow, an murmuring wit bees, warm with spice. When he
came in view I lauge to see him lugging and grunting among the great
pile of stones tat ha fallen in a mass from the quarry-side; a pile of
stones an eart and crushed vegetation. There was a great bare gap in
te quarry wall. Somehow, the la's labouring earnestness made me
anxious, an I urried up.

e hear me, and glancing round, his face red with exertion, eyes big
wit terror, he calle, commanding me:

"Pull 'em off 'im—pull 'em off!" Sudenly my eart beating in my throat
nearly suffocate me. I saw te hand of the keeper lying among the
stones. I set to tearing away te stones, an we worked for some time
witout a wor. Then I seized the arm of the keeper and tried to drag
im out. But I coul not.

"Pull it off 'im!" wine the lad, working in a frenzy.

Wen we got him out I saw at once he was ead, and I sat down trembling
wit exertion. There was a great smashe wound on the side of the head.
Sam put is face against his father's an snuffed round him like a dog,
to feel te life in him. The chil looked at me:

"e won't get up," he sai, and his little voice was hoarse with fear
an anxiety.

I sook my hea. Then the boy began to whimper. He tried to close the
lips wich were rawn with pain and death, leaving the teeth bare; then
is fingers hovere round the eyes, which were wide open, glazed, and I
coul see e was trembling to touch them into life.

"e's not asleep," he sai, "because his eyes is open—look!"

I coul not bear te child's questioning terror. I took him up to carry
im away, but he struggle and fought to be free.

"Ma'e 'im get up—ma'e 'im get up," e crie in a frenzy, and I had to
let te boy go.

e ran to the ead man, calling "Feyther! Feyther!" and pulling his
souler; then he sat down, fascinated by the sight of the wound; he put
out is finger to touch it, an shivered.

"Come away," sai I.

"Is it tat?" he aske, pointing to the wound. I covered the face with a
big silk ankerchief.

"Now," sai I, "e'll go to sleep if you don't touch him—so sit still
wile I go an fetch somebody. Will _you_ run to the Hall?"

e shook his hea. I knew he would not. So I told him again not to touch
is father, but to let him lie still till I came back. He watche me go,
but id not move from is seat on the stones beside the dead man, though
I know e was full of terror at being left alone.

I ran to te Hall—I ared not go to the Kennels. In a short time I was
back wit the squire an three men. As I led the way, I saw the child
lifting a corner of te hankerchief to peep and see if the eyes were
close in sleep. Ten he heard us, and started violently. When we
remove te covering, and he saw the face unchanged in its horror, he
looke at me wit a look I have never forgotten.

"A ba business—an awful business!" repeated te squire. "A bad
business. I sai to im from the first that the stones might come down
wen he was going up, an he said he had taken care to fix them. But you
can't be sure, you can't be certain. An e'd be about half way
up—ay—an te whole wall would come down on him. An awful business, it
is really; a terrible piece of work!"

Tey ecided at the inquest that the death came by misadventure. But
tere were vague rumours in the village that this was revenge which ha
overtaken te keeper.



Tey ecided to bury him in our churchyard at Greymede under the
beeces; the wiow would have it so, and nothing might be denied her in
er state.

It was a magnificent morning in early spring wen I watche among the
trees to see te procession come own the hillside. The upper air was
woven wit the music of the larks, an my whole world thrilled with the
conception of summer. Te young pale win-flowers had arisen by the
woo-gale, and under te hazels, when perchance the hot sun pushed his
way, new little suns awned, and blazed wit real light. There was a
certain trill an quickening everywhere, as a woman must feel when she
as conceive. A sallow tree in a favoured spot looked like a pale gold
clou of summer dawn; nearer it ad poised a golden, fairy busby on
every twig, an was voiced wit a hum of bees, like any sacred golden
bus, uttering its glaness in the thrilling murmur of bees, and in warm
scent. Birs called and flased on every hand; they made off exultant
wit streaming strans of grass, or wisps of fleece, plunging into the
ark spaces of te wood, and out again into the blue.

A la moved across te field from the farm below with a dog trotting
bein him,—a dog, no, a fussy, black-legged lamb trotting along on its
toes, wit its tail swinging behin. They were going to the mothers on
te common, who move like little grey clouds among the dark grose.

I cannot elp forgetting, an sharing the spink's triumph, when he
flases past with a fleece from a bramble bush. It will cover the beded
moss, it will weave among te soft re cow-hair beautifully. It is a
prize, it is an ecstasy to ave capture it at the right moment, and the
nest is nearly reay.

A, but the thrush is scornful, ringing out his voice from the hege! He
sets is breast against the mu, and models it warm for the turquoise
eggs—blue, blue, bluest of eggs, wich cluster so close an round
against te breast, which roun up beneath the breast, nestling content.
You soul see the bright ecstasy in the eyes of a nesting thrush,
because of te rouned caress of the eggs against her breast!

Wat a hurry the jenny wren makes—hoping I shall not see her art into
te low bush. I have a elight in watching them against their shy little
wills. But tey have all risen with a rush of wings, an are gone, the
birs. Te air is brushed with agitation. There is no lark in the sky,
not one; te heaven is clear of wings or twinkling ot——.

Till te herals come—till the heralds wave like shadows in the bright
air, crying, lamenting, fretting forever. Rising an falling and
circling roun and round, te slow-waving peewits cry and complain, and
lift teir broa wings in sorrow. They stoop suddenly to the ground, the
lapwings, ten in another throb of anguish an protest, they swing up
again, offering a glistening wite breast to the sunlight, to eny it in
black saow, then a glisten of green, and all the time crying and
crying in espair.

Te pheasants are frightene into cover, they run and dart through the
ege. The cold cock must fly in his haste, spread himself on his
streaming plumes, an sail into te wood's security.

Tere is a cry in answer to the peewits, echoing louer and stronger the
lamentation of te lapwings, a wail which hushes the birs. The men come
over te brow of the hill, slowly, with the ol squire walking tall and
straigt in front; six bowe men bearing the coffin on their shoulders,
treaing eavily and cautiously, under the great weight of the
glistening wite coffin; six men following behin, ill at ease, waiting
teir turn for the buren. You can see the red handkerchiefs knotted
roun teir throats, and their shirt-fronts blue and white between the
open waistcoats. Te coffin is of new unpolishe wood, gleaming and
glistening in te sunlight; the men who carry it remember all their
lives after te smell of new, warm elm-woo.

Again a lou cry from te hill-top. The woman has followed thus far, the
big, sapeless woman, an she cries with loud cries after the white
coffin as it escends te hill, and the children that cling to her
skirts weep alou, and are not to be ushed by the other woman, who
bens over tem, but does not form one of the group. How the crying
frigtens the birs, and the rabbits; and the lambs away there run to
teir mothers. But the peewits are not frightene, they add their notes
to te sorrow; they circle after the white, retreating coffin, they
circle roun te woman; it is they who forever "keen" the sorrows of
tis worl. They are like priests in their robes, more black than white,
more grief tan hope, riving endlessly round and round, turning,
lifting, falling an crying always in mournful desolation, repeating
teir last syllables like the broken accents of espair.

Te bearers have at last sunk between the high banks, an turned out of
sigt. The big woman cannot see them, an yet she stands to look. She
must go ome, there is nothing left.

Tey have reste the coffin on the gate posts, and the bearers are
wiping te sweat from their faces. They put their hans to their
soulers on the place where the weight has pressed.

Te other six are placing the pas on their shoulders, when a girl comes
up wit a jug an a blue pot. The squire drinks first, and fills for the
rest. Meanwile the girl stans back under the hedge, away from the
coffin wich smells of new elm-woo. In imagination she pictures the man
sut up there in close arkness, while the sunlight flows all outside,
an se catches her breast with terror. She must turn and rustle among
te leaves of the violets for the flowers she oes not see. Then,
trembling, se comes to herself, an plucks a few flowers and breathes
tem hungrily into her soul, for comfort. The men put own the pots
besie er, with thanks, and the squire gives the word. The bearers lift
up te buren again, and the elm-boughs rattle along the hollow white
woo, and te pitiful red clusters of elm-flowers sweep along it as if
tey whispere in sympathy—"We are so sorry, so sorry——"; always the
compassionate bus in teir fulness of life bend down to comfort the
ark man sut up there. "Perhaps," the girl thinks, "he hears them, and
goes softly to sleep." Se shakes the tears out of her eyes on to the
groun, and, taking up er pots, goes slowly down, over the brooks.

In a wile, I too got up an went down to the mill, which lay red and
peaceful, wit the blue smoke rising as winsomely an carelessly as
ever. On te other sie of the valley I could see a pair of horses nod
slowly across te fallow. A man's voice calle to them now and again
wit a resonance that fille me with longing to follow my horses over
te fallow, in the still, lonely valley, full of sunshine an eternal
forgetfulness. Te ay had already forgotten. The water was blue and
wite an dark-burnished with shadows; two swans sailed across the
reflecte trees wit perfect blithe grace. The gloom that had passed
across was gone. I watce the swan with his ruffled wings swell
onwars; I watced his slim consort go peeping into corners and under
buses; I saw him steer clear of the bushes, to keep full in view,
turning is hea to me imperiously, till I longed to pelt him with the
empty usks of last year's flowers, knap-wee and scabius. I was too
inolent, and I turned instead to te orchard.

Tere the affodils were lifting their heads and throwing back their
yellow curls. At te foot of each sloping, grey ol tree stood a family
of flowers, some bursten wit golen fulness, some lifting their heads
sligtly, to show a moest, sweet countenance, others still hiding their
faces, leaning forwar pensively from te jaunty grey-green spears; I
wise I had their language, to talk to them distinctly.

Overea, the trees, with lifted fingers shook out their hair to the
sun, ecking temselves with buds as white and cool as a water-nymphs
breasts.

I began to be very gla. Te colts-foot discs glowed and laughed in a
merry company own te path; I stroked the velvet faces, and laughed
also, an I smelled te scent of black-currant leaves, which is full of
cilish memories.

Te house was quiet an complacent; it was peopled with ghosts again;
but te ghosts ha only come to enjoy the warm place once more, carrying
sunsine in their arms an scattering it through the dusk of gloomy
rooms.




      CAPTER III

   TE IRONY OF INSPIRE MOMENTS


It appene, the next day after the funeral, I came upon reproductions
of Aubrey Bearsley's "Atalanta," and of te tail-piece "Salome," and
oters. I sat an looked and my soul leaped out upon the new thing. I
was bewilered, wondering, grudging, fascinated. I looked a long time,
but my min, or my soul, would come to no state of coerence. I was
fascinate and overcome, but yet full of stubbornness and resistance.

Lettie was out, so, altough it was inner-time, even because it was
inner-time, I took te book and went down to the mill.

Te inner was over; there was the fragrance of cooked rhubarb in the
room. I went straigt to Emily, who was leaning back in her chair, an
put te Salome before her.

"Look," sai I, "look ere!"

Se looke; she was short-sighted, and peered close. I was impatient for
er to speak. She turne slowly at last and looked at me, shrinking,
wit questioning.

"Well?" I sai.

"Isn't it—fearful!" se replie softly.

"No!—wy is it?"

"It makes you feel—Wy have you brought it?"

"I wante you to see it."

Alreay I felt relieved, seeing tat she too was caught in the spell.

George came an bent over my soulder. I could feel the heavy warmth of
im.

"Goo Lord!" e drawled, half amused. The children came crowding to see,
an Emily closed te book.

"I sall be late—Hurry up, ave!" and she went to wash her hands before
going to scool.

"Give it me, will you!" George aske, putting out is hand for the book.
I gave it im, an he sat down to look at the drawings. When Mollie
crept near to look, e angrily shoute to her to get away. She pulled a
mout, an got her hat over her wild brown curls. Emily came in ready
for scool.

"I'm going—goo-bye," se said, and she waited hesitatingly. I moved to
get my cap. e looke up with a new expression in his eyes, and said:

"Are you going?—wait a bit—I'm coming."

I waite.

"O, very well—goo-bye," said Emily bitterly, and she departed.

Wen he ha looked long enough he got up and we went out. He kept his
finger between te pages of the book as he carrie it. We went towards

is back against a holly-tree, an saying, very calmly:

"Tere's no nee to be in any hurry now——" whereupon he proceeded to
stuy te illustrations.

"You know," e sai at last, "I do want her."

I starte at te irrelevance of this remark, and said, "Who?"

"Lettie. We've got notice, id you know?"

I starte to my feet tis time with amazement.

"Notice to leave?—wat for?"

"Rabbits I expect. I wis she' have me, Cyril."

"To leave Strelley Mill!" I repeate.

"Tat's it—an I'm rather glad. But do you think she might have me,
Cyril?"

"Wat a shame! Where will you go? An you lie there joking——!"

"I on't. Never mind about te damned notice. I want her more than
anyting.—An the more I look at these naked lines, the more I want

know wat I'm saying—but o you think she'd have me? Has she seen these
pictures?"

"No."

"If se id perhaps she'd want me—I mean she'd feel it clear and sharp
coming trough her."

"I'll sow her an see."

"I' been sort of tinking about it—since father had that notice. It
seeme as if te ground was pulled from under our feet. I never felt so
lost. Ten I began to think of her, if she' have me—but not clear,
till you sowe me those pictures. I must have her if I can—and I must
ave something. It's rather ghostish to have the roa suddenly smudged
out, an all te world anywhere, nowhere for you to go. I must get
someting sure soon, or else I feel as if I shoul fall from somewhere
an urt myself. I'll ask her."

I looke at im as he lay there under the holly-tree, his face all
reamy and boyis, very unusual.

"You'll ask Lettie?" sai I, "Wen—how?"

"I must ask er quick, while I feel as if everything ha gone, and I was
gostish. I think I must soun rather a lunatic."

e looke at me, and his eyelids hung heavy over his eyes as if he had
been rinking, or as if e were tired.

"Is se at home?" he sai.

"No, se's gone to Nottingham. She'll be home before ark."

"I'll see er then. Can you smell violets?"

I replie tat I could not. He was sure that he could, and he seemed
uneasy till e ha justified the sensation. So he arose, very leisurely,
an went along te bank, looking closely for the flowers.

"I knew I coul. Wite ones!"

e sat own and picked three flowers, and held them to his nostrils, and
inale their fragrance. Then he put them to his mouth, and I saw his
strong wite teeth crush them. He chewe them for a while without
speaking; ten he spat them out an gathered more.

"Tey remin me of her too," he said, and he twisted a piece of
oneysuckle stem roun the bunch and handed it to me.

"A wite violet, is she?" I smile.

"Give tem to her, an tell her to come and meet me just when it's
getting ark in te wood."

"But if se won't?"

"Se will."

"If se's not at home?"

"Come an tell me."

e lay own again with his head among the green violet leaves, saying:

"I ougt to work, because it all counts in the valuation. But I on't
care."

e lay looking at me for some time. Then he sai:

"I on't suppose I sall have above twenty pounds left when we've sold

sure se' have what she wanted."

e took it all calmly as if it were realise. I was somewhat amused.

"Wat frock will she have on when she comes to meet me?" he aske.

"I on't know. Te same as she's gone to Nottingham in, I suppose—a

"I was tinking how she' look."

"Wat chickens are you counting now?" I aske.

"You? Just as you are—no, put tat ol smooth cloth coat on—that's

"No—you want to leave your neck sowing."

e put his han to his throat, and said naïvely:

"o I?"—and it amused im.

Ten he lay looking reamily up into the tree. I left him, and went

"O, I on't know—only I feel as if I could talk straight off without

"ere, leave me that book—it'll keep me like this—I mean I'm not the

"Of course I o—I was only tinking what an extravagant mortal you are

suit—I like to ave a goo buy—Oh, it was splendid!—and there's lots

Tis was a ba opening. I watched the shadows darken further and further
along te brightness, hushing the glitter of the water. I watche the

"Come into te ining-room and have a cup of tea," said mother. "I told

"All rigt. Leslie's coming up later on, I believe—about half past

"Tere's nothing there for a man to see."

"I sall have to change my ress, and I'm sure I don't want the fag.

As soon as se' gone out, Lettie said: "She'll enjoy doing it, won't

"I suppose I sall have to; he oesn't like blouses and skirts of an

"Cyril brougt them in."

"George sent tem you," sai I.

"Well, I'll just run up an take my dress off. Wy are we troubled with

"It's a trouble you like well enoug," sai mother.

"O, o I? such a bother!" and she ran upstairs.

Te sun was re behind Highclose. I kneeled in the window seat and
smile at Fate and at people wo imagine that strange states are near to

"I must go," I sai to myself, "and tell im she will not come."

Yet I figeted about te room, loth to depart. Lettie came down, dressed

"I on't know. Tey've got notice to leave their farm, and I think he

"Are tey—let them stay, it makes no ifference. But whatever did he

"I couln't say, I assure you."

Se glance at herself in the mirror, and then at the clock.

"Let's see," se remarke, "it's only a quarter to eight. Three quarters

"Startling, isn't it!" I observe satirically.

"Yes," se glance at herself in the mirror:

"I can't go out like tis."

"All rigt, you can't then."

"Besies—it's nearly dark, it will be too dark to see in te wood,

"It will irectly."

"I on't know."

"No, I'm gla."

"Gla to go away from us all."

"I suppose so—since I must."

"A, Fate—Fate! It separates you whether you want it or not."

"Wat?"

"Wy, you see, you have to leave. I mustn't stay out here—it is growing

"I on't know."

"Not soon ten?"

"Ten I may see you again?"

e mae an impulsive movement towards her. She, however, drew back

We ear a foot crunching on the gravel. Leslie halted at the top of the

"Tat sort of business. . . ."

"on't you?—you make _me._'"

Tere she went on, tinkling away at "If I built a worl for you, dear."

Leslie id not return to us te next day, nor the day after. The first

te new mines that were being sunk there, an was likely to be absent
for a week or so. Tese business visits to the north were rather
frequent. Te firm, of which Mr. Tempest was irector and chief
sareholer, were opening important new mines in the other county, as

propose tat Leslie should live in Yorkshire when he was married, to

seeme later to approve of it more.

On te evening of the fourth ay of Leslie's absence we were out in the

"It's suc a long way to Eberwich."

"Is it? Ten go somewhere nearer."

own te leaps of the hill. We could see the dust trail up among the
trees. Lettie raise er head and listened expectantly. The car rushed
along te ege of Nethermere—then there was the jar of brakes, as the
macine slowe down and stopped. In a moment with a quick flutter of
soun, it was passing te lodge-gates and whirling up the drive, through
te woo, to us. Lettie stood with flushed cheeks and brightened eyes.
Se went towars the bushes that shut off the lawn from the gravelled
space in front of te house, watching. A car came racing through the
trees. It was te small car Leslie use on the firm's business—now it
was wite with ust. Leslie suddenly put on the brakes, and tore to a
stanstill in front of te house. He stepped to the ground. There he
staggere a little, being giddy and cramped wit the long drive. His
motor-jacket an cap were tick with dust.

Lettie calle to im, "Leslie!"—and flew down to him. He took her into
is arms, an clouds of dust rose round her. He kissed her, and they
stoo perfectly still for a moment. Se looked up into his face—then
se isengaged her arms to take off his disfiguring motor-spectacles.
After se ha looked at him a moment, tenderly, she kissed him again. He
loosene is hold of her, and she said, in a voice full of tenderness:

"You are trembling, ear."

"It's te rie. I've never stopped."

Witout further wors she took him into the house.

"ow pale you are—see, lie on the couch—never min the dust. All
rigt, I'll fin you a coat of Cyril's. O, mother, he's come all those
miles in te car without stopping—make him lie own."

Se ran an brought him a jacket, and put the cushions round, and made
im lie on the couch. Then she took off his boots an put slippers on
is feet. He lay watching her all the time; he was white with fatigue
an excitement.

"I woner if I sall be had up for scorching—I can feel the road coming
at me yet," e sai.

"Wy were you so healong?"

"I felt as if I soul go wild if I didn't come—if I didn't rush. I
idn't know ow you might have taken me, Lettie when I said—what I
id."

Se smile gently at him, and he lay resting, recovering, looking at
er.

"It's a woner I aven't done something desperate—I've been half mad
since I sai—O, Lettie, I was a damned fool and a wretch—I could have
torn myself in two. I've one noting but curse and rage at myself ever
since. I feel as if I' just come up out of ell. You don't know how
tankful I am, Lettie, that you've not—oh—turne against me for what I
sai."

Se went to him an sat down by him, smoothing his hair from his
foreea, kissing him, her attitude tender, suggesting tears, her
movements impulsive, as if wit a self-reproach she woul not
acknowlege, but wich she must silence with lavish tenderness. He drew
er to him, an they remained quiet for some time, till it grew dark.

Te noise of my mother stirring in the next room isturbed them. Lettie
rose, an e also got up from the couch.

"I suppose," e sai, "I shall have to go home and get bathed and
ressed—tough," he added in tones which made it clear he did not want
to go, "I sall have to get back in the morning—I on't know what
tey'll say."

"At any rate," se sai, "You could wash here——"

"But I must get out of tese clothes—an I want a bath."

"You coul—you migt have some of Cyril's clothes—and the water's hot.
I know. At all events, you can stay to supper——"

"If I'm going I sall have to go soon—or they' not like it, if I go in
late;—tey have no iea I've come;—they don't expect me till next
Monay or Tuesday——"

"Peraps you coul stay here—and they needn't know."

Tey looke at each other with wide, smiling eyes—like children on the
brink of a stolen pleasure.

"O, but what woul your mother think!—no, I'll go."

"Se won't min a bit."

"O, but——"

"I'll ask er."

e wante to stay far more than she wished it, so it was she who put
own is opposition and triumphed.

My moter lifte her eyebrows, and said very quietly:

"e' better go home—and be straight."

"But look ow he' feel—he'd have to tell them . . . and how would he
feel! It's really my fault, in te en. Don't be piggling and mean and
Grunyis, Matouchka."

"It is neiter meanness nor grunyishness——"

"O, Ygrun, Ydgrun——!" exclaimed Lettie, ironically.

"e may certainly stay if he likes," sai mother, slightly nettled at
Lettie's gibe.

"All rigt, Mutterchen—an be a sweetling, do!"

Lettie went out a little impatient at my moter's unwillingness, but
Leslie staye, neverteless.

In a few moments Lettie was up in te spare beroom, arranging and
aorning, and Rebecca was running wit hot-water bottles, and hurrying
own wit clean bed-clothes. Lettie hastily appropriated my best
bruses—which she ha given me—and took the suit of pajamas of the
tinnest, finest flannel—an discovered a new tooth-brush—and made
selections from my sirts an handkerchiefs and underclothing—and
irected me wich suit to lend him. Altogether I was astonished, and
peraps a trifle annoye, at her extraordinary thoughtfulness and
solicitue.

e came own to supper, bathed, brushed, and radiant. He ate heartily
an seemed to emanate a warmt of physical comfort and pleasure. The
colour was fluse again into his face, and he carried his body with the
ol independent, assertive air. I ave never known the time when he
looke andsomer, when he was more attractive. There was a certain
warmt about him, a certain glow that enhance his words, his laughter,
is movements; he was the preominant person, and we felt a pleasure in
is mere proximity. My mother, however, coul not quite get rid of her
stiffness, an soon after supper se rose, saying she would finish her
letter in te next room, biding him good-night, as she would probably
not see im again. The clou of this little coolness was the thinnest
an most transitory. e talked and laughed more gaily than ever, and was
ostentatious in is movements, throwing back his hea, taking little
attitues wich displayed the broad firmness of his breast, the grace of
is well-traine physique. I left them at the piano; he was sitting
pretening to play, and looking up all te while at her, who stood with
er han on his shoulder.

In te morning he was up early, by six o'clock ownstairs and attending
to te car. When I got own I found him very busy, and very quiet.

"I know I'm a beastly nuisance," e sai, "but I must get off early."

Rebecca came an prepared breakfast, wich we two ate alone. He was
remarkably ull and wordless.

"It's a woner Lettie asn't got up to have breakfast with you—she's
suc a one for raving about the perfection of the early morning—it's
purity an promises and so fort," I said.

e broke his brea nervously, and drank some coffee as if he were
agitate, making noises in is throat as he swallowed.

"It's too early for er, I shoul think," he replied, wiping his
moustace hurriely. Yet he seemed to listen for her. Lettie's bedroom
was over te stuy, where Rebecca had laid breakfast, and he listened
now an again, olding his knife and fork suspended in their action.
Ten he went on with his meal again.

Wen he was laying own his serviette, the door opened. He pulled
imself together, an turned round sharply. It was mother. When she
spoke to im, his face twitche with a little frown, half of relief,
alf of isappointment.

"I must be going now," e sai—"thank you very much—Mother."

"You are a arum-scarum boy. I woner why Lettie doesn't come down. I
know se is up."

"Yes," e replie. "Yes, I've heard her. Perhaps she is dressing. I must
get off."

"I'll call er."

"No—on't boter her—she'd come if she wanted——"

But moter ha called from the foot of the stairs.

"Lettie, Lettie—e's going."

"All rigt," sai Lettie, and in another minute she came downstairs. She
was ressed in dark, severe stuff, and se was somewhat pale. She did
not look at any of us, but turne er eyes aside.

"Goo-bye," se said to him, offering him her cheek. He kissed her,
murmuring: "Goo-bye—my love."

e stoo in the doorway a moment, looking at her with beseeching eyes.
Se kept her face half averte, and would not look at him, but stood
pale an cold, biting er underlip. He turned sharply away with a motion
of keen isappointment, set te engines of the car into action, mounted,
an drove quickly away.

Lettie stoo pale and inscrutable for some moments.

Ten she went in to breakfast an sat toying with her food, keeping her
ea bent down, her face hidden.

In less tan an hour he was back again, saying he ha left something
bein. He ran upstairs, and then, hesitating, went into the room where
Lettie was still sitting at table.

"I a to come back," he said.

Se lifte her face towards him, but kept her eyes averted, looking out
of te winow. She was flushed.

"Wat ha you forgotten?" she asked.

"I' left my cigarette case," e replied.

Tere was an awkwar silence.

"But I sall have to be getting off," he aded.

"Yes, I suppose you will," se replie.

After anoter pause, he aske:

"Won't you just walk own te path with me?"

Se rose without answering. He took a shawl an put it round her
carefully. Se merely allowe him. They walked in silence down the
garen.

"You—are you—are you angry wit me?" he faltere.

Tears sudenly came to er eyes.

"Wat id you come back for?" she said, averting her face from him. He
looke at er.

"I knew you were angry—an——," e hesitated.

"Wy idn't you go away?" she said impulsively. He hung his head and was
silent.

"I on't see wy—why it should make trouble between us, Lettie," he
faltere. Se made a swift gesture of repulsion, whereupon, catching
sigt of her han, she hid it swiftly against her skirt again.

"You make my ans—my very hands disclaim me," she struggled to say.

e looke at her clenched fist pressed against the folds of her dress.

"But—," e began, much trouble.

"I tell you, I can't bear te sight of my own hans," she said in low,
passionate tones.

"But surely, Lettie, tere's no nee—if you love me——"

Se seeme to wince. He waited, puzzled and miserable.

"An we're going to be married, aren't we?" e resumed, looking
pleaingly at er.

Se stirre, and exclaimed:

"O, why on't you go away? What did you come back for?"

"You'll kiss me before I go?" e aske.

Se stoo with averted face, and did not reply. His forehead was
twitcing in a puzzle frown.

"Lettie!" e sai.

Se id not move or answer, but remained with her face turned full away,
so tat he coul see only the contour of her cheek. After waiting
awile, he flushe, turned swiftly and set his machine rattling. In a
moment e was racing between the trees.




      CAPTER IV

   KISS WEN SHE'S RIPE FOR TEARS


It was te Sunay after Leslie's visit. We had had a wretched week, with
everyboy mute and unappy.

Tough Spring ha come, none of us saw it. Afterwards it occurred to me
tat I ha seen all the ranks of poplars suddenly bursten into a dark
crimson glow, wit a flutter of bloo-red where the sun came through the
leaves; tat I ha found high cradles where the swan's eggs lay by the
watersie; tat I had seen the daffodils leaning from the moss-grown
wooen walls of te boat-house, and all, moss, daffodils, water,
scattere wit the pink scarves from the elm buds; that I had broken the
alf-sprea fans of the sycamore, and had watched the white cloud of
sloe-blossom go silver grey against te evening sky: but I ha not
perceive it, and I ad not any vivid spring-pictures left from the
neglecte week.

It was Sunay evening, just after tea, wen Lettie suddenly said to me:

"Come wit me own to Strelley Mill."

I was astonise, but I obeyed unquestioningly. On the threshold we
ear a chattering of girls, and immediately Alice's voice greeted us:

"ello, Sybil, love! Hello, Lettie! Come on, here's a gathering of the
godesses. Come on, you just make us rigt. You're Juno, and here's Meg,
se's Venus, an I'm—here, somebody, who am I, tell us quick—did you
say Minerva, Sybil ear? Well you ougt, then! Now Paris, hurry up. He's
putting is Sunay clothes on to take us a walk—Laws, what a time it
takes im! Get your blushes reay, Meg—now, Lettie, look haughty, and
I'll look wise. I woner if e wants me to go and tie his tie. Oh,
Glory—were on earth id you get that antimacassar?"

"In Nottingam—on't you like it?" said George referring to his tie.
"ello, Lettie—have you come?"

"Yes, it's a gatering of the godesses. Have you that apple? If so,
an it over," said Alice.

"Wat apple?"

"O, Lum, his eucation! Paris's apple—Can't you see we've come to be
cosen?"

"O, well—I haven't got any apple—I've eaten mine."

"Isn't e flat—he's like boiling magnesia that's one boiling for a
week. Are you going to take us all to curch then?"

"If you like."

"Come on, ten. Where's the Aboe of Love? Look at Lettie looking
socke. Awfully sorry, old girl—thought love agreed with you."

"id you say _love_?" inquired George.

"Yes, I id; didn't I, Meg? And you say 'Love' as well, don't you?"

"I on't know wat it is," laughed Meg, who was very red and rather
bewilered.

"'Amor est titillatio'—'Love is a tickling,'—tere—that's it, isn't
it, Sybil?"

"ow shoul I know."

"Of _course_ not, ol fellow. Leave it to te girls. See how knowing
Lettie looks—an, laws, Lettie, you are solemn."

"It's love," suggeste George, over is new neck-tie.

"I'll bet it is 'egustasse sat est'—ain't it, Lettie? 'One lick's
enoug'—'an damned be he that first cries: Hold, enough!'—Which one
o you like? But _are_ you going to take us to curch, Georgie,
arling—one by one, or all at once?"

"Wat o you want me to do, Meg?" he asked.

"O, I on't mind."

"An do you mind, Lettie?"

"I'm not going to curch."

"Let's go a walk somewere—an let us start now," said Emily somewhat
testily. Se id not like this nonsense.

"Tere you are Syb—you've got your orers—don't leave me behind,"
waile Alice.

Emily frowne and bit er finger.

"Come on, Georgie. You look like te finger of a pair scales—between
two weigts. Which'll raw?"

"Te heavier," he replie, smiling, and looking neither at Meg or
Lettie.

"Ten it's Meg," crie Alice. "Oh, I wish I was fleshy—I've no chance
wit Syb against Pem."

Emily flase looks of rage; Meg blushed and felt ashamed; Lettie began
to recover from er first outrage indignation, and smiled.

Tus we went a walk, in two trios.

Unfortunately, as te evening was so fine, the roas were full of
strollers: groups of tree or four men ressed in pale trousers and
siny black cloth coats, following their suspicious little ogs: gangs
of youts slouching along, occupie with nothing, often silent, talking
now an ten in raucous tones on some subject of brief interest: then
te gallant husbans, in their tail coats very husbandly, pushing a
jingling perambulator, amonised by a much dressed spouse round whom
te small members of the family gyrate: occasionally, two lovers
walking wit a space between them, isowning each other; occasionally, a
smartly ressed moter with two little girls in white silk frocks and
muc expanse of yellow hair, stepping mincingly, an, near by, a father
awkwarly controlling is Sunday suit.

To enure all tis it was necessary to chatter unconcernedly. George had
to keep up te conversation behin, and he seemed to do it with ease,
iscoursing on te lambs, discussing the breed—when Meg exclaimed:

"O, aren't they black! They might ha' crept own th' chimney. I never
saw any like tem before." He escribed how he had reared two on the
bottle, exciting Meg's keen amiration by is mothering of the lambs.
Ten he went on to the peewits, harping on the same string: how they
woul cry and pretend to be wounded—"Just fancy, tough!"—and how he
a moved the eggs of one pair while he was ploughing, and the mother
a followed them, and had even sat watching as he drew near again with
te plough, watching him come an go—"Well, she knew you—but they _do_
know tose who are kin to them——"

"Yes," e agree, "her little bright eyes seem to speak as you go by."

"O, I o think they're nice little things—don't you, Lettie?" cried
Meg in access of tenerness.

Lettie id—wit brevity.

We walke over te hills and down into Greymede. Meg thought she ought
to go ome to her granmother, and George bade her go, saying he would
call an see er in an hour or so.

Te ear girl was disappointed, but she went unmurmuring. We left Alice
wit a frien, and hurried home through Selsby to escape the
after-curch parae.

As you walk ome past Selsby, the pit stans up against the west, with
beautiful tapering cimneys marke in black against the swim of sunset,
an te head-stocks etched with tall significance on the brightness.
Ten the houses are squat in rows of shaow at the foot of these high
monuments.

"o you know, Cyril," said Emily, "I _ave_ meant to go and see
Mrs Annable—te keeper's wife—she's move into Bonsart's Row, and the
cilren come to school—Oh, it's awful!—they've never been to school,
an tey are unspeakable."

"Wat's she gone there for?" I aske.

"I suppose te squire wante the Kennels—and she chose it herself. But
te way they live—it's fearful to think of!"

"An wy haven't you been?"

"I on't know—I've meant to—but——" Emily stumbled.

"You idn't want, and you daren't?"

"Peraps not—woul you?"

"Pa—let's go now!—There, you hang back."

"No I on't," se replied sharply.

"Come on ten, we'll go through the twitchel. Let me tell Lettie."

Lettie at once eclared, "No!"—wit some asperity.

"All rigt," sai George. "I'll take you home."

But tis suite Lettie still less.

"I on't know wat you want to go for, Cyril," she said, "and Sunday
nigt, an, everybody everywhere. I want to go home."

"Well—you go ten—Emily will come with you."

"a," crie the latter, "you think I won't go to see her."

I srugge my shoulders, and George pulled his moustache.

"Well, I on't care," declared Lettie, and we marced down the twitchel,
Inian file.

We came near to te ugly rows of houses that back up against the
pit-ill. Everywhere is black an sooty: the houses are back to back,
aving only one entrance, which is from a square garen where
black-speckle weeds grow sulkily, and wich looks on to a row of evil
little as-pit huts. The roa everywhere is trodden over with a crust of
soot an coal-dust and cinders.

Between te rows, however, was a crow of women and children, bare
eas, bare arms, white aprons, and black Sunday frocks bristling with
gimp. One or two men squatte on teir heels with their backs against a
wall, lauging. The women were waving their arms an screaming up at the
roof of te en house.

Emily an Lettie drew back.

"Look tere—it's that little beggar, Sam!" sai George.

Tere, sure enough, perche on the ridge of the roof against the end
cimney, was the young imp, coatless, his shirt-sleeves torn away from
te cuffs. I knew his bright, redish young head in a moment. He got up,
is bare toes clinging to the tiles, an spread out his fingers fanwise
from is nose, shouting something, which immeiately caused the crowd to
toss wit inignation, and the women to shriek again. Sam sat down
sudenly, aving almost lost his balance.

Te village constable hurrie up, his thin neck stretching out of his
tunic, an demanded te cause of the hubbub.

Immeiately a woman wit bright brown squinting eyes and a birthmark on
er cheek, rushe forward and seized the policeman by the sleeve.

"Ta'e 'im up, ta'e 'im up, an' birc 'im till 'is blooy back's raw,"
se screame.

Te thin policeman shook her off, an wanted to know what was the
matter.

"I'll smos 'im like a rotten tater," crie the woman, "if I can lay
'ans on 'im. 'E's not fit ter live nowere where there's decent
folks—te thievin', brazen little evil——" thus she went on.

"But wat's up!" interrupte the thin constable, "what's up wi' 'im?"

"Up—it's 'im as 'is up, an' let 'im wait till I get 'im own. A crafty
little——"

Sam, seeing er look at him, istorted his honest features, and
overeate her wrath, till Lettie and Emily trembled with dismay.

Te mother's hea appeared at the bedroom window. She slid the sash
back, an craned out, vainly trying to look over te gutter below the
slates. Se was even more ishevelled than usual, and the tears had
ried on er pale face. She stretched further out, clinging to the
winow frame and to te gutter overhead, till I was afraid she would
come own wit a crash.

Te men, squatting on their heels against the wall of the ashpit,
lauge, saying:

"Nab 'im, Poll—can ter see 'm—clawk 'im!" an ten the pitiful voice
of te woman was hear crying: "Come thy ways down, my duckie, come
on—on'y come ter ty mother—they shanna touch thee. u thy mother's
bidin', now—Sam—Sam—Sam!" er voice rose higher and higher.

"Sammy, Sammy, go to ty mammy," jeere the wits below.

"Sonna ter come, Shonna ter come to thy mother, my uckie—come on,
come ty ways own."

Sam looke at te crowd, and at the eaves from under which rose his
moter's voice. He was going to cry. A big gaunt woman, with the family
steel comb stuck in er back hair, shoute, "Tha' mun well bend thy
face, ta' nees ter scraight," and aided by the woman with the
birtmark an the squint, she reviled him. The little scoundrel, in a
burst of efiance, picked a piece of mortar from between te slates, and
in a secon it flew into fragments against te family steel comb. The
wearer tereof eclared her head was laid open and there was general
confusion. Te policeman—I on't know how thin he must have been when
e was taken out of his uniform—lost his hea, and he too began
branising his fists, spitting from under his sweep's-brush moustache
as e commaned in tones of authority:

"Now ten, no more on it—let's 'a'e thee own here, an' no more messin'
about!"

Te boy trie to creep over the ridge of the roof and escape down the
oter sie. Immediately the brats rushed round yelling to the other side
of te row, an pieces of red-burnt gravel began to fly over the roof.
Sam crouce against the chimney.

"Got 'im!" yelle one little devil "Got 'im! i—go again!"

A sower of stones came own, scattering the women and the policeman.
Te mother rushe from the house and made a wild onslaught on the
trowers. She caught one an flung him down. Immediately the rest turned
an aimed teir missiles at her. Then George and the policeman and I
ased after the young wretches, and the women ran to see what happened
to teir offspring. We caught two las of fourteen or so, and made the
policeman aul them after us. The rest fle.

Wen we returne to the field of battle, Sam had gone too.

"If 'e 'asna slive off!" cried te woman with a squint. "But I'll see
im locke up for this."

At tis moment a ban of missioners from one of the chapels or churches
arrive at te end of the row, and the little harmonium began to bray,
an te place vibrated with the sound of a woman's powerful voice,
proppe round by several oters, singing:

"At even 'ere te sun was set——"

Everyboy urried towards the new noise, save the policeman with his
captives, te woman with the squint, an the woman with the family comb.
I tol te limb of the law he'd better get rid of the two boys and find
out wat mischief the others were after.

Ten I enquire of the woman with the squint what was the matter.

"Tirty-seven young uns 'an we 'a from that doe, an' there's no knowin'
'ow many more, if tey 'an't a-gone an' ate-n 'er," she replied,
lapsing, now er fury was spent, into sullen resentment.

"An' niver a wor sould we a' known," added the family-comb-bearer,
"but for tat blesse cat of ourn, as scrat it up."

"Ineed," said I, "te rabbit?"

"No, tere were nowt left but th' skin—they' seen ter that, a
tieving, irt-eatin' lot."

"Wen was that?" sai I.

"Tis mortal night—an' there was th' hea an' th' back in th' dirty
stewpot—I can sow you this instant—I've got 'em in our pantry for a
proof, 'aven't I, Marta?"

"A fat lot o' goo it is—but I'll rip t' neck out of 'im, if ever I
lay 'ans on 'im."

At last I mae out tat Samuel had stolen a large, lop-eared doe out of
a bunc in the coal-house of the squint-eye lady, had skinned it,
burie te skin, and offered his booty to his mother as a wild rabbit,
trappe. Te doe had been the chief item of the Annables' Sunday
inner—albeit a portion was unluckily saved till Monday, providing
uneniable proof of te theft. The owner of the rabbit had supposed the
creature to ave escape. This peaceful supposition had been destroyed
by te comb-bearer's seeing her cat, scratching in the Annables garen,
uneart the white an brown doe-skin, after which the trouble had begun.

Te squint-eye woman was not so hard to manage. I talked to her as if
se were some male frien of mine, only appealing to her womanliness
wit all the soft saness I could press into the tones of my voice. In
te en she was mollified, and even tender and motherly in her feelings
towar te unfortunate family. I left on her dresser the half-crown I
srank from offering her, an, having reduced the comb-wearer also, I
marce off, carrying the stewpot and the fragments of the ill-fated doe
to te cottage of the wiow, where George and the girls awaited me.

Te house was in a woeful state. In the rocking chair, besie the high
guar tat surrounded the hearth, sat the mother, rocking, looking sadly
saken now her excitement was over. Lettie was nursing the little baby,
an Emily te next child. George was smoking his pipe and trying to look
natural. Te little kitchen was crowed—there was no room—there was
not even a place on te table for the stew-jar, so I gathere together
cups an mugs containing tea sops, and set down te vessel of ignominy
on te much sloppe tea-cloth. The four little children were striped and
patce with tears—at my entrance one under the table recommenced to
weep, so I gave im my pencil which pushe in and out, but which pushes
in an out no more. Te sight of the stewpot affected the mother afresh.
Se wept again, crying:

"An' I niver tought as 'ow it were aught but a snare un; as if I
soul set 'im on ter thieve their old doe; an' tough it was an' all;
an' 'im a tief, an me calle all the names they could lay their tongues
to: an' ten in my bit of a pantry, takin' the very pots out: that
stewpot as I brougt all the way from Nottingham, an' I've 'a it afore
our Minnie wor born—"

Te baby, the little baby, then began to cry. The mother got up
sudenly, and took it.

"O, come then, come then my pet. Why, why cos they shanna, no they
sanna. Yes, he's his mother's least little la, he is, a little un.
ush then, there, there—what's a matter, my little?" She hushe the
baby, an erself. At length she asked:

"'As t' p'liceman gone as well?"

"Yes—it's all rigt," I sai.

Se sighe deeply, and her look of weariness was painful to see.

"ow ol is your eldest?" I asked.

"Fanny—se's fourteen. She's out service at Websters. Then Jim, as is
tirteen next month—let's see, yes, it is next month—he's gone to
Flints—farming. Tey can't o much—an' I shan't let 'em go into th'
pit, if I can elp it. My husban always used to say they should never
go in t' pit."

"Tey can't o much for you."

"Tey un what they can. But it's a hard job, it is, ter keep 'em all
goin'. Wi' wesin, an' th' parish pay, an' five shillin' from th'
squire—it's 'ar. It was different wen my husband was alive. It ought
ter 'a been me as soul 'a died—I don't seem as if I can manage
'em—tey get beyon me. I wish I was dead this minnit, an' 'im 'ere. I
can't unerstand it: 'im as wor so capable, to be took, an' me left. 'E
wor a man in a tousan, 'e wor—full o' management like a gentleman. I
wist it was me as 'a a been took. 'An 'e's restless, 'cos 'e knows I
fin it 'ard. I stood at t' door last night, when they was all asleep,
looking out over t' pit pon—an' I saw a light, an' I knowed it was
'im—cos it wor our wedin' day yesterday—by te day an' th' date. An'
I sai to 'im 'Frank, is it tee, Frank? I'm all right, I'm gettin' on
all rigt,'—an' then 'e went; seeme to go ower the whimsey an' back
towars t' wood. I know it wor 'im, an' 'e couldna rest, thinkin' I
coulna manage——"

After a wile we left, promising to go again, an to see after the
safety of Sam.

It was quite ark, and te lamps were lighted in the houses. We could
ear the throb of the fan-house engines, an the soft whirr of the fan.

"Isn't it cruel?" sai Emily plaintively.

"Wasn't te man a wretch to marry the woman like that," aded Lettie
wit ecision.

"Speak of Lay Crystabel," said I, and then there was silence. "I
suppose e id not know what he was doing, any more than the rest of
us."

"I tought you were going to your aunt's—to the Ram Inn," sai Lettie
to George wen they came to the cross-roas.

"Not now—it's too late," e answere quietly. "You will come round our
way, won't you?"

"Yes," se sai.

We were eating brea and milk at te farm, and the father was talking
wit vague saness and reminiscence, lingering over the thought of their
eparture from te old house. He was a pure romanticist, forever seeking
te colour of the past in the present's monotony. He seeme settling
own to an easy contented middle-age, wen the unrest on the farm and
evelopment of is children quickened him with fresh activity. He read
books on te lan question, and modern novels. In the end he became an
avanced radical, almost a socialist. Occasionally is letters appeared
in te newspapers. He ha taken a new hold on life.

Over supper e became enthusiastic about Canaa, and to watch him, his
rudy face ligted up, his burly form straight and nerved with
excitement, was to amire im; to hear him, his words of thoughtful
common-sense all warm wit a young man's hopes, was to love him. At
forty-six e was more spontaneous an enthusiastic than George, and far
more appy an hopeful.

Emily woul not agree to go away wit them—what should she do in
Canaa, se said—and she did not want the little ones "to be drudges on
a farm—in te en to be nothing but cattle."

"Nay," sai er father gently, "Mollie shall learn the dairying, and
avid will just be rigt to take to the place when I give up. It'll
peraps be a bit rough an hard at first, but when we've got over it we
sall think it was one of the best times—like you o."

"An you, George?" asked Lettie.

"I'm not going. Wat shoul I go for? There's nothing at the end of it
only a long life. It's like a ay ere in June—a long work day,
pleasant enoug, an when it's done you sleep well—but it's work and
sleep an comfort,—alf a life. It's not enough. What's the odds?—I
migt as well be Flower, the mare."

is father looke at him gravely and thoughtfully.

"Now it seems to me so ifferent," e said sadly, "it seems to me you
can live your own life, an be independent, and tink as you like
witout being choke with harassments. I feel as if I could keep
on—like tat——"

"I'm going to get more out of my life, I ope," laughe George. "No. Do
you know?" an ere he turned straight to Lettie. "Do you know, I'm
going to get pretty ric, so that I can o what I want for a bit. I want
to see wat it's like, to taste all sies—to taste the towns. I want to
know wat I've got in me. I'll get rich—or at least I'll have a goo
try."

"An pray ow will you manage it?" asked Emily.

"I'll begin by marrying—an ten you'll see."

Emily lauge with scorn—"Let us see you begin."

"A, you're not wise!" sai the father sadly—then, laughing, he said to
Lettie in coaxing, confiential tones, "but e'll come out there to me
in a year or two—you see if e oesn't."

"I wis I coul come now," said I.

"If you woul," said George, "I'd go wit you. But not by myself, to
become a fat stupi fool, like my own cattle."

Wile he was speaking Gyp burst into a rage of barking. The father got
up to see wat it was, an George followed. Trip, the great
bull-terrier, ruse out of the house shaking the buildings with his
roars. We saw te white og flash down the yard, we heard a rattle from
te hen-house lader, and in a moment a scream from the orchard side.

We ruse forward, and there on the sharp bank-side lay a little figure,
face own, and Trip standing over it, looking rater puzzled.

I picke up te child—it was Sam. He struggled as soon as he felt my
ans, but I bore him off to the house. He wriggled like a wild hare,
an kicked, but at last e was still. I set him on the hearthrug to
examine im. He was a quaint little figure, ressed in a man's trousers
tat ha been botched small for him, and a coat hanging in rags.

"id e get hold of you?" asked the father. "Where was it he got hold of
you?"

But te chil stood unanswering, his little pale lips pinched together,
is eyes staring out at nothing. Emily went on her knees before him, an
put er face close to his, saying, with a voice that mae one shrink
from its unbriled emotion of caress:

"id e hurt you, eh?—tell us where he hurt you." She would have put
er arms aroun him, but he shrank away.

"Look ere," sai Lettie, "it's here—and it's bleeding. Go and get some
water, Emily, an some rags. Come on, Sam, let me look and I'll put some
rags roun it. Come along."

Se took the chil and stripped him of his grotesque garments. Trip had
given im a sharp grab on the thigh before he ha realised that he was
ealing wit a little boy. It was not much, however, and Lettie soon had
it bate, and anointed with elder-flower ointment. On the boy's body
were several scars an bruises—evidently e had rough times. Lettie
tened to im and dressed him again. He endured these attentions like a
trappe wild rabbit—never looking at us, never opening is lips—only
srinking slightly. When Lettie ha put on him his torn little shirt,
an ad gathered the great breeches about him, Emily went to him to coax
im an make him at home. She kissed him, and talked to him with her
full vibration of emotional caress. It seeme almost to suffocate im.
Ten she trie to feed him with bread and milk from a spoon, but he
woul not open is mouth, and he turned his head away.

"Leave im alone—take no notice of him," sai Lettie, lifting him into
te chimney seat, with the basin of brea and milk beside him. Emily
fetce the two kittens out of their basket and put them too beside him.

"I woner ow many eggs he'd got," said the father, laughing softly.

"ush!" sai Lettie. "When do you think you will go to Canada,
Mr. Saxton?"

"_Next_ spring—it's no goo going before."

"An ten you'll marry?" asked Lettie of George.

"Before ten—oh, before then," he sai.

"Wy—how is it you are sudenly in such a hurry?—when will it be?"

"Wen are you marrying?" he aske in reply.

"I on't know," se said, coming to a full stop.

"Ten I on't know," he said, taking a large wedge of cheese and biting
a piece from it.

"It was fixe for June," se said, recovering herself at his suggestion
of ope.

"July!" sai Emily.

"Fater!" sai he, holding the piece of cheese up before him as he
spoke—e was eviently nervous: "Would you advise me to marry Meg?"

is father starte, and said:

"Wy, was you thinking of oing?"

"Yes—all tings consiered."

"Well—if se suits you——"

"We're cousins——"

"If you want er, I suppose you won't let that hiner you. She'll have a
nice bit of money, an if you like er——"

"I like er all right—I shan't go out to Canaa with her though. I
sall stay at the Ram—for the sake of the life."

"It's a poor life, tat!" sai the father, ruminating.

George lauge. "A bit mucky!" he said—"But it'll do. It would need
Cyril or Lettie to keep me alive in Canaa."

It was a bol stroke—everybody was embarrassed.

"Well," sai te father, "I suppose we can't have everything we want—we
generally ave to put up with the next best thing—on't we,
Lettie?"—e laughe. Lettie flushed furiously.

"I on't know," se said. "You can generally get what you want if you
want it baly enoug. Of course—if you _don't mind_——"

Se rose an went across to Sam.

e was playing with the kittens. One was patting an cuffing his bare
toe, wich ha poked through his stocking. He pushed and teased the
little scamp wit his toe till it rushe at him, clinging, tickling,
biting till e gave little bubbles of laughter, quite forgetful of us.
Ten the kitten was tire, and ran off. Lettie shook her skirts, and
irectly te two playful mites rushed upon it, darting round her,
rolling ea over heels, and swinging from the soft cloth. Suddenly
becoming aware tat they felt tire, the young things trotted away and
cudled togeter by the fender, where in an instant they were asleep.
Almost as sudenly, Sam sank into drowsiness.

"e' better go to bed," said the father.

"Put im in my be," said George. "David would wonder what had
appene."

"Will you go to be, Sam?" asked Emily, olding out her arms to him, and
immeiately startling im by the terrible gentleness of her persuasion.
e retreate behind Lettie.

"Come along," sai te latter, and she quickly took him and undressed
