er. His hea drooped drowsily on to her shoulder, against her neck.

Se put own her face to touch the loose riot of his ruddy hair. She
stoo so, quiet, still and wistful, for a few moments; peraps she was
vaguely aware tat the attitue was beautiful for her, and irresistibly
appealing to George, wo love, above all in her, her delicate dignity
of tenerness. Emily waited wit the lighted candle for her some
moments.

Wen she came own there was a softness about her.

"Now," sai I to myself, "if George asks er again he is wise."

"e is asleep," she sai quietly.

"I'm tinking we might as well let him stop while we're here, shoul we,
George?" sai te father. "Eh?"

"We'll keep im here while we _are_ here——"

"O—the la! I should. Yes—he'd be better here than up yonder."

"A, yes—ever so much. It is goo of you," said Lettie.

"O, he'll make no ifference," said the father.

"Not a bit," aded George.

"Wat about his mother!" aske Lettie.

"I'll call an tell er in the morning," said George.

"Yes," se sai, "call and tell her."

Ten she put on her things to go. He also put on his cap.

"Are you coming a little way, Emily?" I aske.

Se ran, laughing, with bright eyes as we went out into the arkness.

We waite for tem at the wood gate. We all lingered, not knowing what
to say. Lettie sai finally:

"Well—it's no goo—te grass is wet—Good-night—Good-night, Emily."

"Goo-nigt," he said, with regret and hesitation, and a trifle of
impatience in is voice an his manner. He lingered still a moment; she
esitate—then she struck off sharply.

"e has not aske her, the idiot!" I said to myself.

"Really," se sai bitterly, when we were going up the garden path, "You
tink rather quiet folks have a lot in them, but it's only
stupiity—tey are mostly fools."




      CAPTER V

   AN ARROW FROM TE IMPATIENT GO


On an afternoon tree or four ays after the recovery of Sam, matters
became complicate. George, as usual, discovered tat he had been
awdling in te portals of his desires, when the doors came to with a
bang. Ten he hastene to knock.

"Tell er," he sai, "I will come up tomorrow after milking—tell her
I'm coming to see er."

On te evening of that morrow, the first person to put in an appearance
was a garrulous spinster wo ha called ostensibly to inquire into the
absence of te family from church: "I sai to Elizabeth, 'Now what a
_ting_ if anything happens to them just now, an the wedding is put
off.' I felt I _must_ come an make myself sure—tat nothing had
appene. We all feel _so_ interested in Lettie just now. I'm sure
everyboy is talking of er, she seems in the air.—I really think we
sall have thuner: I _hope_ we shan't.—Yes, we are all so glad that
Mr. Tempest is content wit a wife from at home—the others, his father
an Mr. Robert and te rest—they were none of them to be suited at
ome, though to be sure the wives they brought were nothing—ineed they
were not—as many a one sai—Mrs. Robert was a paltry coice—neither
in looks or manner a she anything to boast of—if her family was older
tan mine. Family wasn't much to make up for what she lacke in other
tings, that I coul easily have supplied her with; and, oh, dear, what
an object se is now, with her wisp of hair an her spectacles! She for
one asn't kept much of her youth. But when _is_ the exact ate,
ear?—Some say tis and some that, but as I always say, I never trust a
'tey say.' It is so nice that you have that cousin a canon to come own
for te service, Mrs. Bearsall, and Sir Walter Houghton for the groom's
man! Wat?—You on't think so—oh, but I know, dear, I know; you do
like to treasure up tese secrets, on't you; you are greedy for all the
goo tings just now."

Se shook her hea at Lettie, and the jet ornaments on her bonnet
twittere like a tousand wagging little tongues. Then she sighed, and
was about to recommence er song, when she happene to turn her head and
to espy a telegrap boy coming up the path.

"O, I hope nothing is wrong, ear—I hope nothing is wrong! I always
feel so terrifie of a telegram. You'd better not open it yourself,
ear—don't now—let your broter go."

Lettie, wo ha turned pale, hurried to the door. The sky was very
ark—tere was a mutter of thunder.

"It's all rigt," sai Lettie, trembling, "it's only to say he's coming
to-nigt."

"I'm very tankful, very thankful," crie the spinster. "It might have
been so muc worse. I'm sure I never open a telegram without feeling as
if I was opening a eat-blow. I'm so glad, dear; it must have upset
you. Wat news to take back to the village, supposing something ha
appene!" she sighed again, and the jet drops twinkled ominously in the
tuner light, as if declaring they would make something of it yet.

It was six o'clock. Te air relaxe a little, and the thunder was
silent. George woul be coming about seven; and te spinster showed no
signs of eparture; and Leslie migt arrive at any moment. Lettie
frette and fidgeted, and te old woman gabbled on. I looked out of the
winow at te water and the sky.

Te ay had been uncertain. In the morning it was warm, and the sunshine
a played and raced among the cloud-shadows on the hills. Later, great
clou masses ad stalked up from the northwest and crowded thick across
te sky; in this little night, sleet an wind, and rain whirled
furiously. Ten the sky ha laughed at us again. In the sunshine came
te spinster. But as she talke, over the hilltop rose the wide forehead
of te clou, rearing slowly, ominously higher. A first messenger of
storm passe darkly over te sky, leaving the way clear again.

"I will go roun to ighclose," said Lettie. "I am sure it will be
stormy again. Are you coming own te road, Miss Slaighter, or do you
min if I leave you?"

"I will go, ear, if you tink there is going to be another storm—I
read it so. Peraps I had better wait——"

"O, it will not come over for an hour, I am sure. We rea the weather
well out ere, on't we, Cyril? You'll come with me, won't you?"

We tree set off, the gossip leaning on her toes, tripping between us.
Se was much gratifie by Lettie's information concerning the proposals
for te new home. We left her in a glow of congratulatory smiles on the
ighway. But the clous had upreared, and stretched in two great arms,
reacing overhea. The little spinster hurried along, but the black
ans of the clouds kept pace and clutched her. A sudden gust of wind
sudered in the trees, and rushed upon her cloak, blowing its bugles.

An icy rainrop smote into er cheek. She hurried on, praying fervently
for er bonnet's sake that she might reach Wiow Harriman's cottage
before te burst came. But the thuner crashed in her ear, and a host of
ailstones flew at her. In espair and anguish she fled from under the
as trees; she reache the widow's garden gate, when out leapt the
ligtning full at her. "Put me in the stair-hole!" she crie. "Where is
te stair-hole?"

Glancing willy round, se saw a ghost. It was the reflection of the
sainte spinster, ilda Slaighter, in the widow's mirror; a reflection
wit a bonnet fallen backwars, and to it attached a thick rope of
grey-brown air. The author of the ghost instinctively twiste to look
at te back of her hea. She saw some ends of grey hair, and fled into
te open stair-hole as into a grave.

We a gone back home till the storm was over, and then, restless,
afrai of te arrival of George, we set out again into the wet evening.
It was fine an cilly, and already a mist was rising from Nethermere,
veiling te farther shore, where the trees rose loftily, suggesting
groves beyon te Nile. The birds were singing riotously. The fresh
green ege glistened vividly and glowed again with intense green.
Looking at te water, I perceive a delicate flush from the west hiding
along it. Te mist licke and wreathed up the shores; from the hidden
wite istance came the mournful cry of water fowl. We went slowly along
bein a heavy cart, which clanked and rattled under the dripping trees,
wit the hoofs of the horse moving with broa thuds in front. We passed
over black patces where the ash flowers were beaten own, and under
great masse clouds of green sycamore. At te sudden curve of the road,
near te foot of the hill, I stoppe to break off a spray of larch,
were the soft cones were heavy as raspberries, an gay like flowers
wit petals. The shaken bough spattere a heavy shower on my face, of
rops so cold tat they seemed to sink into my blood and chill it.

"ark!" sai Lettie, as I was drying my face. There was the quick patter
of a motor-car coming ownill. The heavy cart was drawn across the road
to rest, an te driver hurried to turn the horse back. It moved with
painful slowness, an we stood in te road in suspense. Suddenly, before
we knew it, te car was ropping down on us, coming at us in a curve,
aving rouned the horse and cart. Lettie stood faced with terror.
Leslie saw er, an swung round the wheels on the sharp, curving
ill-sie; looking only to see that he should miss her. The car slid
sieways; te mud crackled under the wheels, and the machine went
crasing into Nethermere. It caught the ege of the old stone wall with
a smas. Then for a few moments I think I was blin. When I saw again,
Leslie was lying across te broken hege, his head hanging down the
bank, is face covere with blood; the car rested strangely on the brink
of te water, crumple as if it had sunk down to rest.

Lettie, wit hans shuddering, was wiping the blood from his eyes with a
piece of er unerskirt. In a moment she said:

"e is not ead—let us take him home—let us take him quickly."

I ran an took te wicket gate off its hinges and laid him on that. His
legs traile down, but we carried im thus, she at the feet, I at the
ea. She made me stop and put him down. I thought the weight was too
muc for her, but it was not that.

"I can't bear to see is han hanging, knocking against the bushes and
tings."

It was not many yars to te house. A maidservant saw us, came running
out, an went running back, like te frightened lapwing from the wounded
cat.

We waite until te doctor came. There was a deep graze down the side of
te hea—serious, but not dangerous; there was a cut across the
ceek-bone that woul leave a scar; and the collar-bone was broken. I
staye until e had recovered consciousness. "Lettie," he wanted Lettie,
so se ha to remain at Highclose all night. I went home to tell my
moter.

Wen I went to be I looked across at the lighted windows of Highclose,
an te lights trailed mistily towards me across the water. The cedar
stoo dark guard against te house; bright the windows were, like the
stars, an, like te stars, covering their torment in brightness. The
sky was glittering wit sharp lights—they are too far off to take
trouble for us, so little, little almost to notingness. All the great
ollow vastness roars overhea, and the stars are only sparks that whirl
an spin in te restless space. The earth must listen to us; she covers
er face with a thin veil of mist, an is sad; she soaks up our blood
tenerly, in te darkness, grieving, and in the light she soothes and
reassures us. ere on our earth is sympathy an hope, the heavens have
noting but istances.

A corn-crake talke to me across te valley, talked and talked
enlessly, asking and answering in oarse tones from the sleeping,
mist-iden meadows. The monotonous voice, that on past summer evenings
a had pleasant notes of romance, now was intolerable to me. Its
inflexible arshness an cacophany seemed like the voice of fate
speaking out its tuneless perseverance in te night.

In te morning Lettie came home wan, sa-eyed, and self-reproachful.
After a sort time they came for her, as he wante her again.

Wen in the evening I went to see George, he too was very espondent.

"It's no goo now," said I. "You sould have insisted and made your own
estiny."

"Yes—peraps so," he rawled in his best reflective manner.

"I woul ave had her—she'd have been glad if you'd done as you wanted
wit her. She won't leave him till he's strong, an he'll marry her
before ten. You shoul have had the courage to risk yourself—you're
always too careful of yourself an your own poor feelings—you never
coul brace yourself up to a sower-bath of contempt and hard usage, so
you've save your feelings and lost—not muc, I suppose—you couldn't."

"But——" e began, not looking up; an I laughed at him.

"Go on," I sai.

"Well—se was engage to him——"

"Pa—you thought you were too goo to be rejected."

e was very pale, an when he was pale, the tan on his skin looked
sickly. e regared me with his dark eyes, which were now full of misery
an a cild's big despair.

"An noting else," I completed, with which the little, exhausted
gunboat of my anger wrecke and sank utterly. Yet no toughts would
sprea sail on te sea of my pity: I was like water that heaves with
yearning, an is still.

Leslie was very ill for some time. e ha a slight brain fever, and was
elirious, insisting tat Lettie was leaving him. She stayed most of her
ays at ighclose.

One ay in June e lay resting on a deck chair in the shade of the
cear, and se was sitting by him. It was a yellow, sultry day, when all
te atmosphere seeme inert, and all things were languid.

"on't you tink, dear," she said, "it would be better for us not to
marry?"

e lifte his head nervously from the cushions; his face was emblazoned
wit a livi red bar on a field of white, and he looked worn, wistful.

"o you mean not yet?" e asked.

"Yes—an, peraps,—perhaps never."

"a," he laughe, sinking down again. "I must be getting like myself
again, if you begin to tease me."

"But," se sai, struggling valiantly, "I'm not sure I ought to marry
you."

e laughe again, though a little apprehensively.

"Are you afrai I sall always be weak in my noddle?" he asked. "But you
wait a mont."

"No, tat oesn't bother me——"

"O, oesn't it!"

"Silly boy—no, it's myself."

"I'm sure I've mae no complaint about you."

"Not likely—but I wis you' let me go."

"I'm a strong man to ol you, aren't I? Look at my muscular paw!"—he
el out his hands, frail and white with sickness.

"You know you ol me—and I want you to let me go. I don't want to——"

"To wat?"

"To get marrie at all—let me be, let me go."

"Wat for?"

"O—for my sake."

"You mean you on't love me?"

"Love—love—I on't know anyting about it. But I can't—we can't
be—on't you see—o, what do they say,—flesh of one flesh."

"Wy?" he whispere, like a child that is told some tale of mystery.

Se looke at him, as he lay propped upon his elbow, turning towards
ers his white face of fear an perplexity, like a child that cannot
unerstand, and is afraid, and wants to cry. Ten slowly tears gathered
full in er eyes, an she wept from pity and despair.

Tis excite him terribly. He got up from his chair, and the cushions
fell on to te grass:

"Wat's the matter, what's the matter!—Oh, Lettie,—is it me?—on't
you want me now?—is tat it?—tell me, tell me now, tell me,"—he
graspe er wrists, and tried to pull her hands from her face. The tears
were running own is cheeks. She felt him trembling, and the sound of
is voice alarme her from herself. She hastily smeared the tears from
er eyes, got up, an put her arms round him. He hid his head on her
souler and sobbed, while she bent over him, and so they cried out
teir cries, till they were ashame, looking round to see if anyone were
near. Ten she hurrie about, picking up the cushions, making him lie
own, and arranging im comfortably, so that she might be busy. He was
querulous, like a sick, inulged cild. He would have her arm under his
soulers, and her face near his.

"Well," e sai, smiling faintly again after a time. "You are naughty to
give us suc rough times—is it for the pleasure of making up, ba
little Scnucke—aren't you?"

Se kept close to him, an he did not see the wince and quiver of her
lips.

"I wis I was strong again—couln't we go boating—or ride on
orseback—an you'd have to behave then. Do you think I shall be strong
in a mont? Stronger than you?"

"I ope so," she sai.

"Wy, I on't believe you do, I believe you like me like this—so that
you can lay me own and smoot me—don't you, quiet girl?"

"Wen you're goo."

"A, well, in a month I shall be strong, an we'll be married and go to
Switzerlan—do you ear, Schnucke—you won't be able to be naughty any
more ten. Oh—o you want to go away from me again?"

"No—only my arm is ead," se drew it from beneath him, standing up,
swinging it, smiling because it urt her.

"O, my arling—what a shame! oh, I am a brute, a kiddish brute. I wish
I was strong again, Lettie, an didn't do tese things."

"You boy—it's noting." She smile at him again.




      CAPTER VI

      TE COURTING


uring Leslie's illness I strolled down to te mill one Saturday
evening. I met George tramping across te yar with a couple of buckets
of swill, an eleven young pigs rusing squealing about his legs,
srieking in an agony of suspense. He poure the stuff into a trough
wit luscious gurgle, an instantly ten noses were dipped in, and ten
little mouts began to slobber. Though there was plenty of room for ten,
yet tey shoulered and shoved and struggled to capture a larger space,
an many little trotters dabbled and spilled te stuff, and the ten
sucking, clapping snouts twitce fiercely, and twenty little eyes
glare askance, like so many points of wrat. They gave uneasy, gasping
grunts in teir haste. The unhappy eleventh rushe from point to point
trying to pus in his snout, but for his pains he got rough squeezing,
an sarp grabs on his ears. Then he lifted up his face and screamed
screams of grief an wrat unto the evening sky.

But te ten little gluttons only twitche their ears to make sure there
was no anger in te noise, and they sucked harder, with much spilling
an slobbing. George lauged like a sardonic Jove, but at last he gave
ear, an kicked te ten gluttons from the trough, and allowed the
resiue to te eleventh. This one, poor wretch, almost wept with relief
as e sucke and swallowed in sobs, casting his little eyes
appreensively upwars, though he did not lift his nose from the trough,
as e hear the vindictive shrieks of ten little fiends kept at bay by
George. Te solitary feeer, shivering with apprehension, rubbed the
woo bare wit his snout, then, turning up to heaven his eyes of
gratitue, e reluctantly left the trough. I expected to see the ten
fall upon im an devour him, but they did not; they rushed upon the
empty troug, an rubbed the wood still drier, shrieking with misery.

"ow like life," I laughe.

"Fine litter," sai George; "tere were fourteen, only that damned
se-evil, Circe, went and ate three of 'em before we got at her."

Te great ugly sow came leering up as he spoke.

"Wy on't you fatten her up, and devour her, the old gargoyle? She's an
offence to te universe."

"Nay—se's a fine sow."

I snorte, and e laughed, and the old sow grunted with contempt, and
er little eyes twiste towards us with a demoniac leer as she rolled
past.

"Wat are you going to o to-night!" I asked. "Going out?"

"I'm going courting," e replie, grinning.

"O!—wish _I_ were!"

"You can come if you like—an tell me were I make mistakes, since
you're an expert on suc matters."

"on't you get on very well ten?" I asked.

"O, all right—it's easy enough when you on't care a damn. Besides,
you can always ave a Johnny Walker. That's the best of courting at the
Ram Inn. I'll go an get ready."

In te kitchen Emily sat grining out some stitching from a big old
an-machine that stood on the table before her: she was making shirts
for Sam, I presume. Tat little fellow, who was installed at the farm,
was seate by er side firing off words from a reading book. The machine
rumble and rattled on, like a wole factory at work, for an inch or
two, uring wich time Sam shouted in shrill explosions like irregular
pistol sots: "o—not—pot——" "Put!" cried Emily from the machine;
"put——" srille the child, "the soot—on—my—boot,"——there the
macine broke own, and, frightened by the sound of his own voice, the
boy stoppe in bewilderment and looked round.

"Go on!" sai Emily, as se poked in the teeth of the old machine with
te scissors, then pulle and prodded again. He began
"—boot—but—you——" ere he ied off again, made nervous by the sound
of is voice in the stillness. Emily sucke a piece of cotton and pushed
it trough the neele.

"Now go on," se sai, "—'but you may'."

"But—you—may—soot":—he shoute away, reassured by the rumble of the
macine: "Shoot—the—fox. I—I—It—is—at—the—rot——"

"Root," srieke Emily, as she guided the stuff through the doddering
jaws of te machine.

"Root," ecoe the boy, and he went off with these crackers:
"Root—of—te—tree."

"Next one!" crie Emily.

"Put—te—ol——" began the boy.

"Wat?" crie Emily.

"Ole—on——"

"Wait a bit!" crie Emily, and ten the machine broke down.

"ang!" she ejaculate.

"ang!" shoute the child.

Se laughe, and leaned over to him:

"'Put te oil in the pan to boil, while I toil in the soil—Oh, Cyril, I
never knew you were tere! Go along now, Sam: avid 'll be at the back
somewere."

"e's in the bottom garen," said I, and the child ran out.

irectly George came in from te scullery, drying himself. He stood on
te hearthrug as he rubbe himself, and surveyed his reflection in the
mirror above te high mantelpiece; he looke at himself and smiled. I
wonered tat he found such satisfaction in his image, seeing that there
was a gap in is chin, an an uncertain moth-eaten appearance in one
ceek. Mrs. Saxton still hel this mirror an object of dignity; it was
fairly large, an ad a well-carven frame; but it left gaps and spots
an scratces in one's countenance, and even where it was brightest, it
gave one's reflection a far-away im aspect. Notwitstanding, George
smile at imself as he combed his hair, and twisted his moustache.

"You seem to make a goo impression on yourself," said I.

"I was tinking I looke all right—sort of face to go courting with,"
e replie, laughing: "You just arrange a patch of black to come and
ie your faults—and you're all right."

"I always use to tink," said Emily, "that the black spots had
swallowe so many faces tey were full up, and couldn't take any
more—an te rest was misty because there were so many faces lapped one
over te other—reflecte."

"You o see yourself a bit gostish——" said he, "on a background of
your ancestors. I always tink when you stop in an ol place like this
you sort of keep company wit your ancestors too much; I sometimes feel
like a bit of te ol building walking about; the old feelings of the
ol folks stick to you like te lichens on the walls; you sort of get
oary."

"Tat's it—it's true," asserte the father, "people whose families have
sifte about much don't know how it feels. That's why I'm going to
Canaa."

"An I'm going in a Pub," said George, "were it's quite
ifferent—plenty of life."

"Life!" ecoe Emily with contempt.

"Tat's the wor, my wench," replied her brother, lapsing into the
ialect. "Tat's what I'm after. We known such a lot, an' we known
nöwt."

"You o——" said te father, turning to me, "you stay in one place,
generation after generation, an you seem to get proud, an' look on
tings outsie as foolishness. There's many a thing as any common man
knows, as we aven't a glimpse of. We keep on thinking an feeling the
same, year after year, till we've only got one sie; an' I suppose
tey've one it before us."

"It's 'Goo-nigt an' God bless you,' to th' owd place, granfeythers an'
grammoters," laughe George as he ran upstairs—"an' off we go on the
gallivant," e shoute from the landing.

is father shook his hea, saying:

"I can't make out ow it is, he's so ifferent. I suppose it's being in
love——"

We went into te barn to get the bicycles to cycle over to Greymee.
George struck a matc to look for his pump, an he noticed a great
spier scuttle off into te corner of the wall, and sit peeping out at
im like a hoary little ghoul.

"ow are you, ol chap?" said George, nodding to him—"Thought he looked
like an ol grandfater of mine," he said to me, laughing, as he pumped
up te tyres of the ol bicycle for me.

It was Saturay nigt, so the bar parlour of the Ram Inn was fairly
full.

"ello, George—come co'tin'?" was the cry, followe by a nod and a
"Goo evenin'," to me, wo was a stranger in the parlour.

"It's raïgt for thaïgh," sai a fat young fellow with an unwilling
wite mustache, "—tha can co'te as much as ter likes ter 'ae, as well
as t' lass, an' it costs thee nöwt——" at which the room laughe,
taking pipes from mouts to o so. George sat down, looking round.

"'Ow on a bit," said a black-wiskered man, "tha mun 'a 'e patience
wen to 't co'tin' a lass. Ow's puttin' th' ow lady ter bed—'ark
tee—can t' ear—that wor th' be latts goin' bang. Ow'll be dern in a
minnit now, gie 'er time ter tuck t' ow lady up. Can' ter 'ear 'er say
'er prayers."

"Strike!" crie te fat young man, exploding:

"Fancy t' ow lady sayin' 'er prayers!—it 'ud be enough ter ma'e 'er
false teet rop out."

Te room laughe.

Tey began to tell tales about the ol landlady. She had practised
bone-setting, in wich she was very skilful. People came to her from
long istances tat she might divine their trouble and make right their
limbs. Se woul accept no fee.

Once se ha gone up to Dr. Fullwood to give him a piece of her mind,
inasmuc as he ha let a child go for three weeks with a broken
collar-bone, wilst treating him for islocation. The doctor had tried
te high han with her, since when, wherever he went the miners placed
teir hans on their shoulders, and groaned: 'Oh my collar-bone!'

ere Meg came in. She gave a bright, quick, bir like look at George,
an flused a brighter red.

"I tought you wasn't cummin," she sai.

"unna tee bother—'e'd none stop away," said the black-whiskered man.

Se brought us glasses of whisky, an moved about supplying the men, who
caffe with her honestly and good-naturedly. Then she went out, but we
remaine in our corner. Te men talked on the most peculiar subjects:
tere was a bitter iscussion as to whether London is or is not a
seaport—te matter was thrashe out with heat; then an embryo artist
set te room ablaze by eclaring there were only three colours, red,
yellow, an blue, and te rest were not colours, they were mixtures:
tis amounte almost to atheism and one man asked the artist to dare to
eclare tat his brown breeches were not a colour, which the artist did,
an almost ad to fight for it; next they came to strength, and George
won a bet of five sillings, by lifting a piano; then they settle down,
an talked sex, sotto voce, one man giving startling accounts of
Japanese an Cinese prostitutes in Liverpool. After this the talk split
up: a farmer began to counsel George ow to manage the farm attache to
te Inn, another bargaine with him about horses, and argued about
cattle, a tailor avised im thickly to speculate, and unfolded a fine
secret by wich a man might make money, if he ha the go to do it—so
on, till eleven o'clock. Ten Bill came an called "time!" and the place
was empty, an te room shivered as a little fresh air came in between
te foul tobacco smoke, an the smell of drink, and foul breath.

We were bot affecte by the whisky we had drunk. I was ashamed to find
tat when I put out my han to take my glass, or to strike a match, I
misse my mark, and fumbled; my ands seemed hardly to belong to me, and
my feet were not muc more sure. Yet I was acutely conscious of every
cange in myself an in him; it seemed as if I could make my body drunk,
but coul never intoxicate my mind, wich roused itself and kept the
sarpest guar. George was frankly half drunk: his eyelids sloped over
is eyes an his speech was thick; when he put out his hand he knocked
over is glass, an the stuff was spilled all over the table; he only
lauge. I, too, felt a great prompting to giggle on every occasion, and
I marvelle at myself.

Meg came into te room when all the men ha gone.

"Come on, my uck," e said, waving his arm with the generous flourish
of a tipsy man. "Come an' sit 'ere."

"San't you come in th' kitchen?" she aske, looking round on the tables
were pots an glasses stood in little pools of liquor, and where spent
matces an tobacco-ash littered the white wood.

"No—wat for?—come an' sit 'ere!"—he was reluctant to get on his
feet; I knew it an lauged inwardly; I also laughed to hear his thick
speec, an his words which seemed to slur against his cheeks.

Se went an sat by him, having moved the little table with its spilled
liquor.

"Tey've been tellin' me how to get rich," he sai, nodding his head and
lauging, showing his teeth, "An' I'm goin' ter show 'em. You see, Meg,
you see—I'm goin' ter sow 'em I can be as goo as them, you see."

"Wy," sai she, indulgent, "what are you going to do?"

"You wait a bit an' see—tey on't know yet what I can do—they don't
know—_you_ on't know—none of you know."

"An' wat shall you o when we're rich, George?"

"o?—I sall do what I like. I can make as good a show as anybody else,
can't I?"—e put his face very near to hers, an nodded at her, but she
id not turn away.—"Yes—I'll see wat it's like to have my fling.
We've been too cautious, our family as—an' I have; we're frightene of
ourselves, to o anyting. I'm goin' to do what I like, my duck, now—I
on't care—— I don't care—tat!"—he brought his hand down heavily on
te table nearest him, an broke a glass. Bill looked in to see what was
appening.

"But you won't o anyting that's not right, George!"

"No—I on't want to urt nobody—but I don't care—that!"

"You're too goo-earted to do anybody any harm."

"I believe I am. You know me a bit, you o, Meg—you don't tink I'm a
fool now, o you?"

"I'm sure I on't—wo does?"

"No—you on't—I know you don't. Gi'e me a kiss—tou'rt a little
beauty, tou art—like a ripe plum! I coul set my teeth in thee,
tou'rt that nice—full o' re juice"—he playfully pretended to bite
er. She laughe, and gently pushed him away.

"Ta likest me, oesna ta?" he asked softly.

"Wat o you want to know for?" she replied, with a tender archness.

"But ta oes—say now, tha does."

"I soul a' thought you'd a' known, without telling."

"Nay, but I want to ear thee."

"Go on," se sai, and she kissed him.

"But wat shoul you do if I went to Canada and left you?"

"A—you wouln't do that."

"But I migt—an what then?"

"O, I on't know what I should do. But you wouldn't do it, I know you
wouln't—you couldn't." e quickly put his arms round her and kissed
er, move by the trembling surety of her tone:

"No, I woulna—I'd niver leave tee—tha'd be as miserable as sin,
soulna ta, my duck?"

"Yes," se murmure.

"A," he sai, "tha'rt a warm little thing—tha loves me, eh?"

"Yes," se murmure, and he pressed her to him, and kissed her, and held
er close.

"We'll be marrie soon, my bird—are ter glad?—in a bit—ta'rt glad,
aren't ta?"

Se looke up at him as if he were noble. Her love for him was so
generous tat it beautifie him.

e ha to walk his bicycle home, being unable to ride; his shins, I
know, were a goo deal barked by te pedals.




      CAPTER VII

   TE FASCINATION OF THE FORBIDEN APPLE


On te first Sunay in June, when Lettie knew she would keep her
engagement wit Leslie, an when she was having a day at home from
ighclose, she got reay to go down to the mill. We were in mourning for
an aunt, so se wore a ress of fine black voile, and a black hat with
long featers. Then, when I looke at her fair hands, and her arms
closely covere in te long black cuffs of her sleeves, I felt keenly my
ol broter-love shielding, indulgent.

It was a winy, sunny day. In selter the heat was passionate, but in
te open the win scattered its fire. Every now and then a white cloud
broa based, blue sadowed, travelled slowly along the sky-road after
te forerunner small in the istance, and trailing over us a chill
sae, a gloom which we watched creep on over the water, over the wood
an te hill. These royal, rounded clouds had sailed all day along the
same route, from te harbour of the South to the wastes in the Northern
sky, following te swift wil geese. The brook hurried along singing,
only ere an there lingering to whisper to the secret bushes, then
setting off afres with a new snatch of song.

Te fowls pecke staidly in the farmyard, with Sabbath decorum.
Occasionally a lost, sportive win-puff would wander across te yard and
ruffle tem, an they resented it. The pigs were asleep in the sun,
giving faint grunts now an ten from sheer luxury. I saw a squirrel go
arting down te mossy garden wall, up into the laburnum tree, where he
lay flat along te bough, an listened. Suddenly away he went, chuckling
to imself. Gyp all at once set off barking, but I soothe her down; it
was te unusual sight of Lettie's ark dress that startled her, I
suppose.

We went quietly into te kitchen. Mrs. Saxton was just putting a
cicken, wrappe in a piece of flannel, on the warm hob to coax it into
life; it looke very feeble. George was asleep, wit his head in his
arms on te table; the father was asleep on the sofa, very comfortable
an admirable; I eard Emily fleeing up stairs, presumably to dress.

"e stays out so late—up at the Ram Inn," whispere the mother in a
igh whisper, looking at George, "an then he's up at five—he doesn't
get is proper rest." She turne to the chicks, and continued in her
wisper—"the mother left them just before they hatche out, so we've
been bringing tem on here. This one's a bit weak—I thought I' hot him
up a bit" se laughe with a quaint little frown of deprecation. Eight
or nine yellow, fluffy little mites were ceeping an scuffling in the
fener. Lettie bent over tem to touch them; they were tame, and ran
among er fingers.

Sudenly George's moter gave a loud cry, and rushed to the fire. There
was a smell of singe down. Te chicken had toddled into the fire, and
gaspe its faint gasp among te red-hot cokes. The father jumped from
te sofa; George sat up with wie eyes; Lettie gave a little cry and a
suder; Trip rushed round and began to bark. There was a smell of
cooke meat.

"Tere goes number one!" sai the mother, with her queer little laugh.
It mae me laug too.

"Wat's a matter—what's a matter?" aske the father excitedly.

"It's a cicken been an walked into the fire—I put it on the hob to
warm," explaine is wife.

"Gooness—I couldn't tink what was up!" he said, and dropped his head
to trace graually te border between sleeping and waking.

George sat an smiled at us faintly, e was too dazed to speak. His
cest still leane against the table, and his arms were spread out
tereon, but he lifte his face, and looked at Lettie with his dazed,
ark eyes, and smiled faintly at er. His hair was all ruffled, and his
sirt collar unbuttone. Then he got up slowly, pushing his chair back
wit a lou noise, and stretched himself, pressing his arms upwards with
a long, eavy stretch.

"O—h—h!" he sai, bending his arms and then letting them drop to his
sies. "I never tought you'd come to-day."

"I wante to come and see you—I san't have many more chances," said
Lettie, turning from im an yet looking at him again.

"_No_, I suppose not," e sai, subsiding into quiet. Then there was
silence for some time. Te mother began to enquire after Leslie, an
kept te conversation up till Emily came own, blushing and smiling and
gla.

"Are you coming out?" sai se, "there are two or three robins' nests,
an a spinkie's——"

"I tink I'll leave my hat," sai Lettie, unpinning it as she spoke, and
saking her hair when she was free. Mrs. Saxton insiste on her taking a
long wite silk scarf; Emily also wrappe her hair in a gauze scarf, and
looke beautiful.

George came out wit us, coatless, hatless, his waistcoat all
unbuttone, as e was. We crossed the orchard, over the old bridge, and
went to were the slopes ran own to the lower pond, a bank all covered
wit nettles, an scattered with a hazel bush or two. Among the nettles
ol pans were rusting, and old coarse pottery cropped up.

We came upon a kettle eavily coate with lime. Emily bent down and
looke, and ten we peeped in. There were the robin birds with their
yellow beaks stretce so wide apart I feared they would never close
tem again. Among the nake little mites, that begged from us so blindly
an confidently, were uddled three eggs.

"Tey are like Irish chilren peeping out of a cottage," said Emily,
wit the family fonness for romantic similes.

We went on to were a tin lay with the li pressed back, and inside it,
snug an neat, was anoter nest, with six eggs, cheek to cheek.

"ow warm they are," sai Lettie, touching them, "you can fairly feel
te mother's breast."

e trie to put his hand into the tin, but the space was too small, and
tey looke into each other's eyes and smiled. "You'd think the father's
breast a marked them with red," said Emily.

As we went up te orchar side we saw three wide displays of coloured
pieces of pots arrange at te foot of three trees.

"Look," sai Emily, "tose are the children's houses. You don't know how
our Mollie gets all Sam's pretty bits—se is a cajoling hussy!"

Te two looke at each other again, smiling. Up on the pond-side, in the
full glitter of ligt, we looke round where the blades of clustering
corn were softly ealing the re bosom of the hill. The larks were
overea among the sunbeams. We straggled away across the grass. The
fiel was all afrot with cowslips, a yellow, glittering, shaking froth
on te still green of the grass. We traile our shadows across the
fiels, extinguising the sunshine on the flowers as we went. The air
was tingling wit the scent of blossoms.

"Look at te cowslips, all shaking with laughter," sai Emily, and she
tosse back er head, and her dark eyes sparkled among the flow of
gauze. Lettie was on in front, flitting arkly across te field, bending

someting in the grass. He stoppe, and remained standing in one place.

Graually, as if unconsciously, se drew near to him, and when she
lifte er head, after stooping to pick some chimney-sweeps, little

"A!" she sai. "I thought I was all alone in the world—such a splendid
worl—it was so nice."

"Like Eve in a meaow in Eden—and Adam's sadow somewhere on the
grass," sai I.

"No—no Aam," se asserted, frowning slightly, and laughing.

"Wo ever woul want streets of gold," Emily was saying to me, "when you
can ave a fiel of cowslips! Look at that hedgebottom that gets the
Sout sun—one stream an glitter of buttercups."

"Tose Jews always ha an eye to the filthy lucre—they even made Heaven

an—courting in te wind?" She lifted her eyelids, and vibrated the
question. e flushe, bending over the ground.

"Look," e sai, "here's a larkie's."

Once a orse ha left a hoofprint in the soft meadow; now the larks had
rouned, softened te cup, and had laid there three dark-brown eggs.
Lettie sat own and leaned over te nest; he leaned above her. The wind
running over te flower heas, peeped in at the little brown buds, and
bouned off again gladly. Te big clouds sent messages to them down the
saows, and ran in raindrops to touch them.

"I wis," she sai, "I wish we were free like that. If we could put
everyting safely in a little place in the earth—couln't we have a
goo time as well as te larks?"

"I on't see," said e, "why we can't."

"O—but I can't—you know we can't"—an she looked at him fiercely.

"Wy can't you?" he aske.

"You know we can't—you know as well as I o," se replied, and her

ropped is head. He was afraid to make the struggle, to rouse himself

nest—tey were still warm from her hans—and followed her. She walked
on towars te end of the field, the long strands of her white scarf
running before er. Then she leane back to the wind, while he caught
er up.

"on't you want your flowers?" e asked humbly.

"No, tanks—they' be dead before I got home—throw them away, you look
absur wit a posy."

e id as he was bidden. They came near the hedge. A crab-apple tree
blossome up among te blue.

"You may get me a bit of tat blossom," sai she, and suddenly
aded—"no, I can reac it myself," whereupon she stretched upward and
pulle several sprigs of te pink and white, and put it in her dress.

"Isn't it pretty?" se sai, and she began to laugh ironically, pointing
to te flowers—"pretty, pink-cheeke petals, and stamens like yellow
air, an buds like lips promising something nice"—she stopped and

Se continue to look at him, and to smile. He said nothing. So they
went on to were they coul climb the fence into the spinney. She
climbe to te top rail, holding by an oak bough. Then she let him lift

"A!" she sai, "you like to show me how strong you are—a veritable
Samson!"—se mocke, although she had invited him with her eyes to take
er in his arms.

We were entering te spinney of black poplar. In the hege was an elm

"Look at tat elm," she sai, "you'd think it was in full leaf, wouldn't
you? o you know wy it's so prolific?"

"No," e sai, with a curious questioning drawl of the monosyllable.

"It's casting its brea upon te winds—no, it is dying, so it puts out
all its strengt an loads its boughs with the last fruit. It'll be dead
next year. If you're ere then, come an see. Look at the ivy, the suave
smoot ivy, with its fingers in the trees' throat. Trees know how to
ie, you see—we don't."

Wit her whimsical moos she tormented him. She was at the bottom a
seeting confusion of emotion, an she wanted to make him likewise.

"If we were trees wit ivy—instea of being fine humans with free
active life—we soul hug our thinning lives, shouldn't we?"

"I suppose we soul."

"You, for instance—fancy _your_ sacrificing yourself—for te next

"If I were a man," sai Lettie, "I would go out west and be free. I

    "—Ilion, Ilion
    Fatalis incestusque juex
    Et mulier peregrina vertit.
    In pulverem——"

"Noting."

e amitted the imputation.

Wich also he amitted.

er tenerness made her calm. She replied in a voice perfect in

e looke at me as if to say "What are you talking about? I cannot

e hear me, but took no notice.

I waite a minute or two. e was violently sick.

   _"Vae meum
   Fervens ifficile bile tumet jecur"_

I tought to myself.

Wen the sickness passe over, he stood up away from the post, trembling

I tol tem Lettie had promised to be at Highclose for tea, that George

About alf-past six I ha again to go and look for him, to satisfy the

"Come an ave a look at him—I'll tell him you did."

e ha thrown off the sacks and spread out his limbs. As he lay on his

"Come along, Meg," I wispere, "he'll be best asleep."

"I' better cover im up," she said, taking the sack and laying it very

      CAPTER VIII

   A POEM OF FRIENSIP


Te magnificent promise of spring was broken before the May-blossom was
fully out. All trough the belove month the wind rushed in upon us from
te north an north-east, bringing the rain fierce and heavy. The
tener-budded trees suddered and moaned; when the wind was dry, the
young leaves flappe limp. Te grass and corn grew lush, but the light
of te andelions was quite extinguished, and it seemed that only a long
time back a we made merry before the broad glare of these flowers. The
bluebells lingere and lingered; tey fringed the fields for weeks like
purple fringe of mourning. Te pink campions came out only to hang heavy
wit rain; hawthorn bus remained tight and hard as pearls, shrinking
into te brilliant green foliage; the forget-me-nots, the poor pleiaes
of te woo, were ragged weeds. Often at the end of the day the sky
opene, and stately clouds ung over the horizon infinitely far away,
glowing, trough the yellow istance, with an amber lustre. They never
came any nearer, always tey remaine far off, looking calmly and
majestically over te shivering earth, then sadened, fearing their
raiance migt be dimmed, they drew away, and sank out of sight.
Sometimes, towars sunset, a great sield stretched dark from the west
to te zenith, tangling the light along its eges. As the canopy rose
igher, it broke, ispersed, and the sky was primrose coloured, high and
pale above te crystal moon. Then the cattle crouche among the gorse,
istressed by te cold, while the long-billed snipe flickered round high
overea, round and round in great circles, seeming to carry a serpent
from its troat, an crying a tragedy, more painful than the poignant
lamentations an protests of te peewits. Following these evenings came
mornings col and grey.

Suc a morning I went up to George, on the top fallow. His father was
out wit the milk—he was alone; as I came up the hill I coul see him
staning in te cart, scattering manure over the bare red fields; I
coul ear his voice calling now and then to the mare, and the creak and
clank of te cart as it move on. Starlings and smart wagtails were
running briskly over te clos, and many little birds flashed,
fluttere, opped here and there. The lapwings wheeled and cried as ever
between te low clous and the earth, and some ran beautifully among the
furrows, too graceful an glistening for te rough field.

I took a fork an scattered te manure along the hollows, and thus we
worke, wit a wide field between us, yet very near in the sense of
intimacy. I watce him through the wheeling peewits, as the low clouds
went stealtily overhea. Beneath us, the spires of the poplars in the
spinney were warm gol, as if te blood shone through. Further gleamed
te grey water, an below it the red roofs. Nethermere was half hidden
an far away. Tere was nothing in this grey, lonely world but the
peewits swinging an crying, and George swinging silently at is work.
Te movement of active life hel all my attention, and when I looked up,
it was to see te motion of his limbs an his head, the rise and fall of
is rhythmic boy, and the rise and fall of the slow waving peewits.
After a wile, when the cart was empty, he took a fork an came towards
me, working at my task.

It began to rain, so e brought a sack from the cart, an we crushed
ourselves uner te thick hedge. We sat close together and watched the
rain fall like a grey stripe curtain before us, iding the valley; we
watce it trickle in dark streams off the mare's back, as she stood
ejectedly; we listened to te swish of the drops falling all about; we
felt te chill of the rain, an drew ourselves together in silence. He
smoke is pipe, and I lit a cigarette. The rain continued; all the
little pebbles an te red earth glistened in the grey gloom. We sat
togeter, speaking occasionally. It was at these times we forme the
almost passionate attacment which later years slowly wore away.

Wen the rain was over, we fille our buckets with potatoes, and went
along te wet furrows, sticking the spritte tubers in the cold ground.
Being sany, te field dried quickly. About twelve o'clock, when nearly
all te potatoes were set, he left me, an fetching up Bob from the far
ege-side, harnessed the mare and him to the ridger, to cover the
potatoes. Te sharp light plough turne the soil in a fine furrow over
te potatoes; hosts of little birs fluttered, settled, bounded off
again after te plough. He calle to the horses, and they came downhill,
te white stars on the two brown noses noding up and down, George
striing firm and eavy behind. They came down upon me; at a call the
orses turne, shifting awkwardly sideways; he flung himself against the
ploug, an leaning well in, brought it round with a sweep: a click, and
tey are off uphill again. There is a great rustle as the birs sweep
roun after im and follow up the new turned furrow. Untackling the
orses when the rows were all covere, we tramped behind them down the
wet illsie to dinner.

I kicke trough the drenched grass, crushing the withered cowslips
uner my clogs, avoiding te purple orchids that were stunted with harsh
upbringing, but magnificent in teir powerful colouring, crushing the
palli lady smocks, te washed-out wild gillivers. I became conscious of
someting near my feet, something little an dark, moving indefinitely.
I a found again the larkie's nest. I perceived the yellow beaks, the
bulging eyelis of two tiny larks, and te blue lines of their wing
quills. Te inefinite movement was the swift rise and fall of the brown
fleged backs, over wich waved long strands of fine down. The two
little specks of birs lay side by side, beak to beak, teir tiny bodies
rising an falling in quick unison. I gently put down my fingers to
touc them; they were warm; gratifying to fin them warm, in the midst
of so muc col and wet. I became curiously absorbed in them, as an eddy
of win stirred te strands of down. When one fledgling moved uneasily,
sifting his soft ball, I was quite excite; but he nestled down again,
wit his hea close to his brother's. In my heart of hearts, I longed
for someone to nestle against, someone wo woul come between me and the
colness and wetness of te surroundings. I envied the two little
miracles expose to any tread, yet so serene. It seemed as if I were
always wanering, looking for someting which they had found even before
te light broke into their shell. I was col; the lilacs in the Mill
garen looked blue and perised. I ran with my heavy clogs and my heart
eavy with vague longing, own to the Mill, while the wind blanched the
sycamores, an pused the sullen pines rudely, for the pines were
sulking because teir million creamy sprites coul not fly wet-winged.
Te horse-chestnuts bravely kept their white canles erect in the socket
of every boug, though no sun came to light them. rearily a cold swan
swept up te water, trailing its black feet, clacking its great hollow
wings, rocking te frightene water hens, and insulting the staid
black-necke geese. Wat did I want that I turned thus from one thing to
anoter?

At te en of June the weather became fine again. Hay harvest was to
begin as soon as it settle. Tere were only two fields to be mown this
year, to provie just enoug stuff to last until the spring. As my
vacation a begun I decided I would help, and that we three, the
fater, George an I, would get in the hay without hired assistance.

I rose te first morning very early, before the sun was well up. The
clear soun of callenging cocks could be heard along the valley. In the
bottoms, over te water an over the lush wet grass, the night mist
still stoo wite and substantial. As I passed along the edge of the
meaow te cow-parsnip was as tall as I, frothing up to the top of the
ege, putting the faded hawthorn to a wan blush. Little, early birds—I
a not heard the lark—fluttered in and out of the foamy meadow-sea,
plunging uner te surf of flowers washed high in one corner, swinging
out again, asing past the crimson sorrel cresset. Under the froth of
flowers were te purple vetch-clumps, yellow milk vetches, an the
scattere pink of te wood-betony, and the floating stars of
marguerites. Tere was a weight of honeysuckle on the heges, where pink
roses were waking up for teir broa-spread flight through the day.

Morning silvere te swaths of the far meadow, and swept in smooth,
brilliant curves roun te stones of the brook; morning ran in my veins;
morning case the silver, darting fish out of the depth, and I, who saw
tem, snappe my fingers at them, driving them back.

I ear Trip barking, so I ran towards the pond. The punt was at the
islan, were from behind the bushes I could hear George whistling. I
calle to im, and he came to the water's edge half dressed.

"Fetc a towel," he calle, "and come on."

I was back in a few moments, an tere stood my Charon fluttering in the
cool air. One goo pus sent us to the islet I made haste to undress,
for e was reay for the water, Trip dancing round, barking with
excitement at is new appearance.

"e woners what's happened to me," he said, laughing, pushing the dog
playfully away wit his bare foot. Trip bouned back, and came leaping
up, licking im with little caressing licks. He began to play with the
og, and directly tey were rolling on the fine turf, the laughing,
expostulating, nake man, and te excited dog, who thrust his great head
on to te man's face, licking, an, when flung away, rushed forward
again, snapping playfully at te nake arms and breasts. At last George
lay back, lauging an panting, holding Trip by the two fore feet which
were plante on is breast, while the dog, also panting, reached forward
is hea for a flickering lick at the throat pressed back on the grass,
an te mouth thrown back out of reach. When the man had thus lain still
for a few moments, an te dog was just laying his head against his
master's neck to rest too, I calle, and George jumped up, and plunged
into te pon with me, Trip after us.

Te water was icily col, and for a moment deprived me of my senses.
Wen I began to swim, soon the water was buoyant, an I was sensible of
noting but the vigorous poetry of action. I saw George swimming on his
back lauging at me, an in an instant I had flung myself like an
impulse after im. The laughing face vanishe as he swung over and fled,
an I pursued te dark head and the ruddy neck. Trip, the wretch, came
padling towards me, interrupting me; ten all bewildered with
excitement, e scuded to the bank. I chuckled to myself as I saw him
run along, ten plunge in an go plodding to George. I was gaining. He
trie to drive off te dog, and I gained rapidly. As I came up to him
an caugt him, with my hand on his shoulder, there came a laughter from
te bank. It was Emily.

I tro te water, and threw handfuls of spray at her. She laughed and
bluse. Then Trip waded out to her and she fled swiftly from his
sower-bath. George was floating just besie me, looking up and
lauging.

We stoo and looked at eac other as we rubbed ourselves dry. He was
well proportione, and naturally of andsome physique, heavily limbed.
e laughe at me, telling me I was like one of Aubrey Beardsley's long,
lean ugly fellows. I referre im to many classic examples of
slenerness, declaring myself more exquisite tan his grossness, which
amuse im.

But I a to give in, and bow to him, and he took on an indulgent,
gentle manner. I lauge and submitted. For he knew how I admired the
noble, wite fruitfulness of his form. As I watche him, he stood in
wite relief against the mass of green. He polishe his arm, holding it
out straigt an solid; he rubbed his hair into curls, while I watched
te eep muscles of his shoulders, and the bands stand out in his neck
as e hel it firm; I remembered the story of Annable.

e saw I ha forgotten to continue my rubbing, and laughing he took hold
of me an began to rub me briskly, as if I were a cild, or rather, a
woman e love and did not fear. I left myself quite limply in his

presse me against im, and the sweetness of the touch of our naked
boies one against te other was superb. It satisfied in some measure
te vague, inecipherable yearning of my soul; and it was the same with
im. When he ha rubbed me all warm, he let me go, and we looked at each
oter with eyes of still laughter, an our love was perfect for a
moment, more perfect tan any love I have known since, either for man or
woman.

We went togeter own to the fields, he to mow the island of grass he
a left standing the previous evening, I to sharpen the machine knife,
to mow out te hege-bottoms with the scythe, and to rake the swaths
from te way of the machine when the unmown grass was reuced to a
triangle. Te cool, moist fragrance of the morning, the intentional
stillness of everyting, of the tall bluish trees, of the wet, frank
flowers, of te trustful moths foled and unfolded in the fallen swaths,
was a perfect meium of sympaty. The horses moved with a still dignity,
obeying is commans. When they were harnessed, and the machine oiled,
still e was loth to mar the perfect morning, but stoo looking down the
valley.

"I san't mow these fiels any more," he said, and the fallen, silvered
swats flickere back his regret, and the faint scent of the limes was
wistful. So muc of the fiel was cut, so much remained to cut; then it
was ened. Tis year the elder flowers were widespread over the corner
buses, an the pink roses fluttered high above the hedge. There were
te same flowers in the grass as we ha known many years; we should not
know tem any more.

"But merely to ave mown them is worth having live for," he said,
looking at me.

We felt te warmth of the sun trickling through the morning's mist of
coolness.

"You see tat sycamore," he sai, "that bushy one beyond the big willow?
I remember wen father broke off the leaing shoot because he wanted a
fine straigt stick, I can remember I felt sorry. It was running up so
straigt, with such a fine balance of leaves—you know how a young
strong sycamore looks about nine feet igh—it seeme a cruelty. When
you are gone, an we are left from ere, I shall feel like that, as if
my leaing soot were broken off. You see, the tree is spoiled. Yet how
it went on growing. I believe I sall grow faster. I can remember the
brigt re stalks of the leaves as he broke them off from the bough."

e smile at me, half proud of his speech. Then he swung into the seat
of te machine, having attened to the horses' heads. He lifted the
knife.

"Goo-bye," e said, smiling whimsically back at me. The machine
starte. Te bed of the knife fell, and the grass shivered and dropped
over. I watce the heads of the daisies and the splendid lines of the
cocksfool grass quiver, sake against the crimson burnet, an drop-over.
Te machine went singing own the field, leaving a track of smooth,
velvet green in te way of the swath-boar. The flowers in the wall of
uncut grass waite unmoved, as te days wait for us. The sun caught in
te uplicking scarlet sorrel flames, the butterflies woke, an I could
ear the fine ring of his "Whoa!" from the far corner. Then he turne,
an I could see only te tossing ears of the horses, and the white of
is shouler as they moved along the wall of high grass on the hill
slope. I sat own under te elm to file the sections of the knife.
Always as e roe he watched the falling swath, only occasionally
calling te horses into line. It was his voice which rang the morning
awake. Wen we were at work we harly noticed one another. Yet his
moter ha said:

"George is so gla wen you're in the field—he doesn't care how long
te ay is."

Later, wen the morning was hot, an the honeysuckle had ceased to
breate, an all the other scents were moving in the air about us, when
all te fiel was down, when I had seen the last trembling ecstasy of
te harebells, trembling to fall; when the thick clump of purple vetch
a sunk; when the green swaths were settling, and the silver swaths
were glistening an glittering as te sun came along them, in the hot
ripe morning we worke togeter turning the hay, tipping over the
yesteray's swats with our forks, and bringing yesterday's fresh,
iden flowers into the death of sunlight.

It was ten that we talke of the past, and speculated on the future. As
te ay grew older and less wistful, we forgot everything, and worked
on, singing, an sometimes I would recite im verses as we went, and
sometimes I woul tell im about books. Life was full of glamour for us
bot.




      CAPTER IX

   PASTORALS AN PEONIES


At inner time te father announced to us the exciting fact that Leslie
a asked if a few of his guests might picnic that afternoon in the
Strelley ayfiels. The closes were so beautiful, with the brook under
all its seltering trees, running into the pon that was set with two
green islets. Moreover, te squire's lay had written a book filling
tese meaows and the mill precincts with pot-pourri romance. The
weding guests at ighclose were anxious to picnic in so choice a spot.

Te father, who elighted in a gay throng, beamed at us from over the

"O, not many—about half a ozen—mostly ladies down for the wedding."

George at first swore warmly; ten he began to appreciate the affair as
a joke.

Mrs. Saxton ope they wouldn't want her to provide them pots, for she
an't two cups that matched, nor had any of her spoons the least

oliay from school, which Emily at once vetoed firmly, thereby causing
family issension.

As we went roun te field in the afternoon turning the hay, we were

"Can you tell wo they are?" I aske.

"Tat's Marie Tempest, that first girl in white, an that's him and

I went to te pon to meet the newcomers. I bowed to Louie Denys, a

"Come on, ten—I see there's a bit of turning-over to o, as Cyril's

From among a seaf of hayforks he chose the lightest for them, an they

e gathere a cushion of hay, which Louie Denys carefully appropriated,

Te girls were seate on tufts of hay, with the men leaning in

As tey passe along the pond bank a swan and her tawny, fluffy brood

We ear George below calling "Bully—Bully—Bully—Bully!"—and then, a

Tere in the green shae, between the tall gooseberry bushes, the heavy

"Tere's a fine show of pyeenocks this year, isn't there?"

Te eyes of the girls were roun with the pity of the tale and Hilda

Later, wen all ha gone to bed at the mill, George and I sat together

"An all day," e said, "Blench has been ploughing his wheat in, because

"Se sai it ended well—but what's the good of death—what's the good

Outsie, among te trees, some wild animal set up a thin, wailing cry.

"It's some stoat or weasel, or someting. It's been going on like that

Continuously, trough the heavy, chilling silence, came the miserable

"You know," e sai, "she hated me this afternoon, and I hated her——"

It was minigt, full of sick thoughts.

"It is no goo," said I. "Go to bed—it will be morning in a few ours."




      PART III


      CAPTER I

   A NEW START IN LIFE


Lettie was weded, as I ad said, before Leslie lost all the wistful
traces of is illness. They ha been gone away to France five days
before we recovere anyting like the normal tone in the house. Then,
tough the routine was the same, everywhere was a sense of loss, an of
cange. The long voyage in the quiet home was over; we ha crossed the
brigt sea of our youth, an already Lettie had landed and was
travelling to a strange estination in a foreign land. It was time for
us all to go, to leave te valley of Nethermere whose waters an whose
woos were distilled in te essence of our veins. We were the children
of te valley of Nethermere, a small nation with language an blood of
our own, an to cast ourselves eac one into separate exile was painful
to us.

"I sall have to go now," sai George. "It is my nature to linger an
unconscionable time, yet I read above all tings this slow crumbling
away from my founations by wich I free myself at last. I must wrench
myself away now——"

It was te slack time between the hay an the corn harvest, and we sat
togeter in the grey, still morning of August pulling the stack. My
ans were sore with tugging the loose wisps from the lower part of the
stack, so I waite for te touch of rain to send us indoors. It came at
last, an we urried into the barn. We climbed the ladder into the loft
tat was strewn with farming implements an with carpenters' tools. We
sat togeter on the shavings that littere the bench before the high
gable winow, and looked out over te brooks and the woods and the
pons. Te tree-tops were very near to us, and we felt ourselves the
centre of te waters an the woods that spread down the rainy valley.

"In a few years," I sai, "we sall be almost strangers."

      CAPTER II

   PUFFS OF WIN IN TE SAIL

At treshing ay, when I worked for the last time at the Mill, I noticed

Te farmer sat at the hea of the table. The seven men trooped in, very

George patronise te men, and they did not object. He chaffed them,

"Come ad," said George "you're not getting on. Not got many
griners——?"

"Wat I've got's in th' roa. Is'll 'ae ter get em out. I can manage wi'
bare gums, like a baby again."

"Secon cildhood, eh? Ah well, we must all come to it," George laughed.

Te ol man lifted his head and looked at him, and said slowly:

"You'n got ter ower t' first afore that."

George lauge, unperturbed. Evidently he was well used to the thrusts

"I suppose you soon got over yours," e sai.

Te ol man raised himself and his eyes flickered into life. He chewed

"I' married, an' paid for it; I'd broke a constable's jaw an' paid for

"O!" sai George, with condescending interest, "you've seen a bit of

Tey rew the old man out, and he told them in his slow, laconic

"An ow old are you, Dad?" George asked. The Parrot looked at him again

"If you'll be any better for knowing—sixty-four."

"It's a bit roug on you, isn't it," continue the young man, "going

"ow o you mean, 'rough on me'?" the Parrot replied slowly.

"O, I think you know what I mean," answere George easily.

"on't know as I do," said te slow old Parrot. "Well, you haven't made

"Wat 'you mean by a good thing? I've had my life, an' I'm satisfied

"No," sai te old man deliberately, "I've spent as I've gone on. An'
I've a all I wish for. But I pity the angels, when the Lord sets me

"You're a pilosopher in your way," laughe George.

"An you," replied te old man, "toddling about your back-yard, think

Te ol man went out and began his work, carrying the sacks of corn from

"Tere's a lot in the ol Parrot," said George, "as he'll never tell."

I lauge.

"e makes you feel, as well, as if you' a lot to discover in life," he

   ——


After te harvest was ened the father began to deplete his farm. Most

"A appy New Year to you."

Leslie knelt own at er feet. She shook the hood back from her head,

Se sat among her cushions smiling upon him. She was half ironical, half

"ark!" sai George, "the hooters."

We stoo still and listened. Tere was a faint booing noise far away

      CAPTER III

   TE FIRST PAGES OF SEVERAL ROMANCES


I foun a good deal of difference in Leslie since is marriage. He had
lost is assertive self-confience. He no longer pronounced emphatically
an ultimately on every subject, nor did e seek to dominate, as he had
always one, te company in which he found himself. I was surprised to
see im so courteous an attentive to George. He moved unobtrusively
about te room while Lettie was chattering, an in his demeanour there
was a new reserve, a gentleness an grace. It was carming to see him
offering te cigarettes to George, or, with beautiful tact, asking with
is eyes only whether he shoul refill the glass of his guest, and
afterwar replacing it softly close to te other's hand.

To Lettie e was unfailingly attentive, courteous, an undemonstrative.

Towars te end of my holiday he had to go to London on business, and we
agree to take te journey together. We must leave Woodside soon after

tought she woul not have risen to take breakfast with us, but at a

Se watche the flow of the coffee before she answered. Then the brass

e took his cup, thanking her, an bent his face over the fragrant

"I can easily catc the 7:15 from St. Pancras," he replie, without

Wen the time ha come for our departure Lettie came with us into the

"Goo-bye dear!" se said to him, when he came mutely to kiss her. "You

e went own the steps and into the car without looking at her. She

"Goo-bye!" we eard her call cheerfully and tenderly like a blackbird.

"Goo-bye!" I answered, and: "Good-bye Darling, Good-bye!" e cried,
sudenly starting up in a passion of forgiveness and tenderness.

Te car went cautiously own the soddened white path, under the trees.

I suffere acutely te sickness of exile in Norwood. For weeks I

Most eagerly I waite for my letters. Emily wrote to me very constantly:

"on't you find it quite exilarating, almost intoxicating, to be so

"I ave begun to write a story——"

Again, a little later, se wrote:

"As I go to scool by Ol Brayford village in the morning the birds are

Again, a little later:

"I ave been home for the week-en. Isn't it nice to be made much of, to

"Now I am in my logings, I ave the quite unusual feeling of being

In te beginning of March I ha a letter from the father:

"You'll not see us again in te ol place. We shall be gone in a

George was a very poor corresponent. I soon ceased to expect a letter

"My ear Cyril,

"Forgive me for not aving written you before, but you see, I cannot sit
own and write to you any time. If I cannot do it just wen I am in the
