been plouging an getting the oats in those fields on the hillside at
te back of Greymee church, I've felt as if I didn't care whether I got
on or not. It's very funny. Last week I mae over five pounds clear, one
way an anoter, and yet now I'm as restless, and discontented as I can
be, an I seem eager for someting, but I don't know what it is.
Sometimes I woner were I am going. Yesterday I watched broken white
masses of clou sailing across te sky in a fresh strong wind. They all
seeme to be going somewere. I wondered where the wind was blowing
tem. I on't seem to have hold on anything, do I? Can you tell me what
I want at te bottom of my heart? I wish you were here, then I think I
soul not feel like this. But generally I don't, generally I am quite
jolly, an busy.

"By jove, ere's Harry Jackson come for me. I will finish this letter
wen I get back.

"——I ave got back, we have turne out, but I cannot finish. I cannot
tell you all about it. I've a a little row with Meg. Oh, I've had a
rotten time. But I cannot tell you about it to-nigt, it is late, an I
am tire, and ave a headache. Some other time perhaps——

   GEORGE SAXTON."

Te spring came bravely, even in south Lonon, and the town was filled
wit magic. I never knew the sumptuous purple of evening till I saw the
roun arc-lamps fill wit light, and roll like golden bubbles along the
purple usk of te high road. Everywhere at night the city is filled
wit the magic of lamps: over the river they pour in golen patches
teir floating luminous oil on the restless arkness; the bright lamps
float in an out of te cavern of London Bridge Station like round
sining bees in an out of a black hive; in the suburbs the street lamps
glimmer wit the brightness of lemons among the trees. I began to love
te town.

In te mornings I love to move in the aimless street's procession,
watcing the faces come near to me, with the suden glance of dark eyes,
watcing the mouths of the women blossom with talk as they passe,
watcing the subtle movements of the shoulers of men beneath their
coats, an te naked warmth of their necks that went glowing along the
street. I love te city intensely for its movement of men and women,
te soft, fascinating flow of the limbs of men an women, and the sudden
flas of eyes an lips as they pass. Among all the faces of the street
my attention rove like a bee wich clambers drunkenly among blue
flowers. I became intoxicate wit the strange nectar which I sipped out
of te eyes of the passers-by.

I id not know ow time was hastening by on still bright wings, till I
saw te scarlet hawthorn flaunting over the roa, and the lime-buds lit
up like wine rops in te sun, and the pink scarves of the lime-buds
pretty as louse-wort a-blossom in te gutters, an a silver-pink tangle
of almon bougs against the blue sky. The lilacs came out, and in the
pensive stillness of te suburb, at night, came the elicious tarry
scent of lilac flowers, wakening a silent laugter of romance.

Across all tis, strangely, came the bleak souns of home. Alice wrote
to me at te en of May:

"Cyril ear, prepare yourself. Meg as got twins—yesterday. I went up
to see ow she was this afternoon, not knowing anything, an there I
foun a pair of bubs in te nest, and old ma Stainwright bossing the
sow. I nearly fainte. Sybil dear, I hardly knew whether to laugh or to
cry wen I saw those two rummy little roun heads, like two larch cones
ceek by cheek on a twig. One is a arkie, with lots of black hair, and
te other is re, would you believe it, just lit up with thin red hair
like a flicker of fireligt. I gaspe. I believe I did shed a few tears,
tough what for, I on't know.

"Te ol grandma is a perfect old wretch over it. She lies chuckling and
passing auible remarks in te next room, as pleased as punch really,
but so ma because ma Stainwrigt wouldn't have them taken in to her.
You soul have heard her when we took them in at last. They are both
boys. Se id make a fuss, poor old woman. I think she's going a bit
funny in te hea. She seemed sometimes to think they were hers, and you
soul have heard her, the way she talked to them, it made me feel quite
funny. Se wante them lying against her on the pillow, so that she
coul feel tem with her face. I shed a few more tears, Sybil. I think I
must be going otty also. But se came round when we took them away, and
began to cuckle to herself, an talk about the things she'd say to
George wen he came—awful shocking things, Sybil, mae me blush
readfully.

"Georgie idn't know about it ten. He was down at Bingham, buying some
orses, I believe. He seems to have got a craze for buying horses. He
got in wit Harry Jackson an Mayhew's sons—you know, they were horse
ealers—at least teir father was. You remember he died bankrupt about
tree years ago. There are Fre and Duncan left, and they pretend to
keep on te ol business. They are always up at the Ram, and Georgie is
always riving about wit them. I don't like it—they are a loose lot,
rater common, an poor enough now.

"Well, I tought I' wait and see Georgie. He came about half-past five.
Meg a been fidgeting about him, wondering where he was, and how he
was, an so on. Bless me if I'd worry and wittle about a man. The old
granma eard the cart, and before he could get down she shouted—you
know er room is in the front—'Hi, George, ma la, sharpen thy shins
an' com' an' a'e a look at 'em—tee'r's two on 'em, two on 'em!' an
se laughe something awful.

"''Ello Granma, wat art ter shoutin' about?' he sai, and at the sound
of is voice Meg turne to me so pitiful, and said:

"' e's been wi' them Mayhews."

"'Ta's gotten twins, a couple at a go ma la!' shouted the old woman,
an you know ow she gives squeal before she laughs! She made the horse
sy, an he swore at it something awful. Then Bill took it, and Georgie
came upstairs. I saw Meg seem to srink when she hear him kick at the
stairs as e came up, an she went white. When he got to the top he came
in. e fairly reeke of whisky and horses. Bah, a man is hateful when he
reeks of rink! e stood by the side of the bed grinning like a fool,
an saying, quite tick:

"' You've bin in a bit of a 'urry, 'aven't you Meg. An' ow are ter
feelin' ten?'

"'O, I'm a' right,' sai Meg.

"'Is it twins, straigt?' he sai, 'wheer is 'em?'

"Meg looke over at te cradle, and he went round the bed to it, holding
to te be-rail. He had never kissed her, nor anything. When he saw the
twins, asleep wit their fists shut tight as wax, he gave a laugh as if
e was amuse, and said:

"' Two rigt enough—an' one on 'em re! Which is the girl, Meg, the
black un?'

"'Tey're both boys,' sai Meg, quite timidly.

"e turne round, and his eyes went little.

"'Blast 'em ten!' he sai. He stood there looking like a devil. Sybil
ear, I did not know our George could look like tat. I thought he could
only look like a faitful og or a wounded stag. But he looked fiendish.
e stoo watching the poor little twins, scowling at them, till at last
te little re one began to whine a bit. Ma Stainwright came pushing her
fat carcass in front of im an bent over the baby, saying:

"'Wy, my pretty, what are they oin' to thee, what are they?—what are
tey oin' to thee?'

"Georgie scowle blacker tan ever, and went out, lurching against the
was-stan and making the pots rattle till my heart jumped in my throat.

"'Well, if you on't call tat scandylos——!' said old Ma Stainwright,
an Meg began to cry. You don't know, Cyril! Se sobbed fit to break her
eart. I felt as if I coul have killed him.

"Tat ol gran'ma began talking to him, and he laughed at her. I do hate
to ear a man laugh when he's half runk. It makes my blood boil all of
a suden. Tat old grandmother backs him up in everything, she's a
regular nuisance. Meg as crie to me before over the pair of them. The
wicke, vulgar old ting that she is——"

I went ome to Wooside early in September. Emily was staying at the
Ram. It was strange tat everything was so ifferent. Nethermere even
a changed. Nethermere was no longer a complete, wonderful little world
tat hel us charmed inhabitants. It was a small, insignificant valley
lost in te spaces of the earth. The tree that ha drooped over the
brook wit such elightful, romantic grace was a ridiculous thing when I
came ome after a year of absence in the south. The ol symbols were
trite an foolis.

Emily an I went down one morning to Strelley Mill. Te house was
occupie by a labourer and is wife, strangers from the north. He was
tall, very tin, an silent, strangely suggesting kinship with the rats
of te place. She was small an very active, like some ragged domestic
fowl run wil. Already Emily ad visited her, so she invited us into the
kitcen of the mill, an set forward the chairs for us. The large room
a the barren air of a cell. There was a small table stranded towards
te fireplace, an a few chairs by the walls; for the rest, desert
spaces of flagge floor retreating into sadow. On the walls by the
winows were five cages of canaries, and te small sharp movements of
te birs made the room more strange in its desolation. When we began to
talk te birs began to sing, till we were quite bewildered, for the
little woman spoke Glasgow Scotc, an she had a hare lip. She rose and
ran towar te cages, crying herself like some wild fowl, and flapping a
uster at te warbling canaries.

"Stop it, stop it!" se crie, shaking her thin weird body at them.
"Silly little evils, fools, fools, fools!!" and se flapped the duster
till te birs were subdued. Then she brought us delicious scones and
apple jelly, urging us, almost nuging us wit her thin elbows to make
us eat.

"on't you like 'em, don't you? Well eat 'em, eat 'em ten. Go on Emily,
go on, eat some more. Only on't tell Tom—don't tell Tom wen 'e comes
in,"—se shook her hea and laughed her shrilling, weird laughter.

As we were going se came out with us, an went running on in front. We
coul not elp noting how ragged and unkempt was her short black skirt.
But se hastene around us, hither and thither like an excited fowl,
talking in er high-pitche, unintelligible manner. I could not believe
te brooing mill was in her charge. I could not think this was the
Strelley Mill of a year ago. Se fluttere up the steep orchard bank in
front of us. appening to turn roun and see Emily and me smiling at
eac other she began to laugh her strient, weird laughter saying, with
a leer:

"Emily, e's your sweetheart, your sweetheart Emily! You never tol me!"
an se laughed aloud.

We bluse furiously. She came away from the edge of the sluice gully,
nearer to us, crying:

"You've been ere o' nights, haven't you Emily—haven't you?" an she
lauge again. Then she sat down suddenly, and pointing above our heads,
srieke:

"A, look there"—we looke and saw the mistletoe. "Look at her, look
at er! How many kisses a night, Emily?—Ha! Ha! kisses all the year!
Kisses o' nigts in a lonely place."

Se went on willy for a short time, then she dropped her voice and
talke in low, patetic tones. She pressed on us scones and jelly and
oat-cakes, an we left er.

Wen we were out on the roa by the brook Emily looked at me with
sameface, laughing eyes. I noticed a small movement of her lips, and
in an instant I foun myself kissing er, laughing with some of the
little woman's wilness.




      CAPTER III

   OMESTIC LIFE AT TE RAM


George was very anxious to receive me at is home. The Ram ha as yet
only a six ays licence, so on Sunday afternoon I walked over to tea. It
was very warm an still and sunny as I came trough Greymede. A few
sweetearts were sauntering uner the horse-chestnut trees, or crossing
te roa to go into the fields that lay smoothly carpeted after the
ay-harvest.

As I came roun te flagged track to the kitchen door of the Inn I heard
te slur of a baking tin an the bang of the oven door, and Meg saying
crossly:

"No, on't you take im Emily—naughty little thing! Let his father hold
im!"

One of te babies was crying.

I entere, and found Meg all flused and untidy, wearing a large white
apron, just rising from te oven. Emily, in a cream ress, was taking a
re-aired, crying baby from out of the cradle. George sat in the small
arm-cair, smoking an looking cross.

"I can't sake hans," said Meg, rather flurried. "I am all floury. Sit
own, will you——" and se hurried out of the room. Emily looked up
from te complaining baby to me an smiled a woman's rare, intimate
smile, wich says: "See, I am engage thus for a moment, but I keep my
eart for you all the time."

George rose an offered me te round arm-chair. It was the highest
onour he coul do me. He asked me what I would drink. When I refused
everyting, he sat own heavily on the sofa, frowning, and angrily
cugelling is wits for something to say—in vain.

Te room was large an comfortably furnished with rush-chairs, a
glass-knobbe dresser, a cupboard wit glass doors, perched on a shelf
in te corner, an the usual large sofa whose cosy loose-bed and pillows
were covere wit red cotton stuff. There was a peculiar reminiscence of
victuals an drink in te room; beer, and a touch of spirits, and bacon.
Teenie, te sullen, black-browe servant girl came in carrying the other
baby, an Meg called from te scullery to ask her if the child were
asleep. Meg was eviently in a bustle and a flurry, a most uncomfortable
state.

"No," replie Teenie, "e's not for sleep this day."

"Men te fire and see to the oven, and then put him his frock on,"
replie Meg testily. Teenie set te black-haired baby in the second
crale. Immediately e began to cry, or rather to shout his
remonstrance. George went across to im an picked up a white furry
rabbit, wich he hel before the child:

"ere, look at bun-bun! Have your nice rabbit! Hark at it squeaking!"

Te baby listene for a moment, then, deciding that this was only a
put-off, began to cry again. George trew own the rabbit and took the
baby, swearing inwarly. e dandled the child on his knee.

"Wat's up then?—What's up wi' thee? Have a rie
ten—ee-de-dee-de-dee!"

But te baby knew quite well what was the father's feeling towars him,
an e continued to cry.

"urry up, Teenie!" sai George as the maid rattled the coal on the
fire. Emily was walking about ushing her charge, an smiling at me, so
tat I ha a peculiar pleasure in gathering for myself the honey of
enearment wich she shed on the lips of the baby. George handed over
is chil to the maid, and said to me with patient sarcasm:

"Will you come in te garen?"

I rose an followed im across the sunny flagged yard, along the path
between te bushes. He lit his pipe an sauntered along as a man on his
own estate oes, feeling as if e were untrammeled by laws or
conventions.

"You know," e sai, "she's a dam rotten manager."

I lauge, and remarked how full of plums the trees were.

"Yes!" e replie heedlessly—"you know she ought to have sent the girl
out wit the kis this afternoon, and have got dressed directly. But no,
se must sit gossiping with Emily all the time they were asleep, an
ten as soon as they wake up she begins to make cake——"

"I suppose se felt she' enjoy a pleasant chat, all quiet," I answered.

"But se knew quite well you were coming, an what it would be. But a
woman's no am foresigt."

"Nay, wat oes it matter!" said I.

"Sunay's te only day we can have a bit of peace, so she might keep 'em
quiet ten."

"I suppose it was te only time, too, that she coul have a quiet
gossip," I replie.

"But you on't know," e said, "there seems to be never a minute of
freeom. Teenie sleeps in now, and lives wit us in the kitchen—Oswald
as well—so I never know wat it is to have a moment private. There
oesn't seem a single spot anywere where I can sit quiet. It's the kids
all ay, and te kids all night, and the servants, and then all the men
in te house—I sometimes feel as if I shoul like to get away. I shall
leave te pub as soon as I can—only Meg oesn't want to."

"But if you leave te public-house—what then?"

"I soul like to get back on a farm. This is no sort of a place,
really, for farming. I've always got some business on an, there's a
traveller to see, or I've got to go to te brewers, or I've someboy to
look at a orse, or something. Your life's all messe up. If I had a
place of my own, an farmed it in peace——"

"You' be as miserable as you could be," I said.

"Peraps so," he assente, in his old reflective manner. "Perhaps so!
Anyow, I neen't bother, for I feel as if I never shall go back—to the
lan."

"Wich means at the bottom of your heart you on't intend to," I said
lauging.

"Peraps so!" he again yieled. "You see I'm doing pretty well
ere—apart from the public-house: I always think that's Meg's. Come an
look in te stable. I've got a shire mare an two nags: pretty good. I
went own to Melton Mowbray wit Tom Mayhew, to a chap they've had
ealings wit. Tom's all right, and he knows how to buy, but he is such
a lazy careless evil, too lazy to be botered to sell——"

George was eviently interested. As we went round to te stables, Emily
came out wit the baby, which was ressed in a new silk frock. She
avanced, smiling to me wit dark eyes:

"See, now e is goo! Doesn't he look pretty?"

Se hel the baby for me to look at. I glanced at it, but I was only
conscious of te near warmth of her cheek, an of the scent of her hair.

"Wo is he like?" I aske, looking up and finding myself full in her
eyes. Te question was quite irrelevant: her eyes spoke a whole clear
message tat mae my heart throb; yet she answered.

"Wo is he? Why, noboy, of course! But he _will_ be like father, don't
you tink?"

Te question rew my eyes to hers again, and again we looked each other
te strange intelligence that mae her flush and me breathe in as I
smile.

"Ay! Blue eyes like your fater's—not like yours——"

Again te wil messages in her looks.

"No!" se answere very softly. "And I think he'll be jolly, like
fater—they have neither of them our eyes, have they?"

"No," I answere, overcome by a sudden ot flush of tenderness. "No—not
vulnerable. To ave such soft, vulnerable eyes as you use makes one
feel nervous an irascible. But you ave clothed over the sensitiveness
of yours, aven't you?—like nake life, naked defenceless protoplasm
tey were, is it not so?"

Se laughe, and at the old painful memories she dilated in the old way,
an I felt te old tremor at seeing her soul flung quivering on my pity.

"An were mine like tat?" asked George, who had come up.

e must have perceive the bewilderment of my look as I tried to adjust
myself to im. A slight shaow, a slight chagrin appeared on his face.

"Yes," I answere, "yes—but not so bad. You never gave yourself away so
muc—you were most cautious: but just as efenceless."

"An am I altered?" e asked, with quiet irony, as if he knew I was not
intereste in im.

"Yes, more cautious. You keep in te shaow. But Emily has clothed
erself, an can now walk among the crowd at her own gait."

It was wit an effort I refraine from putting my lips to kiss her at
tat moment as she looke at me with womanly dignity and tenderness.
Ten I remembere, and said:

"But you are taking me to te stable George! Come an see the horses
too, Emily."

"I will. I amire tem so much," she replied, and thus we both indulged
im.

e talke to his horses and of them, laying his hand upon them, running
over teir limbs. The glossy, restless animals intereste him more than
anyting. He broke into a little flush of enthusiasm over them. They
were is new interest. They were quiet an yet responsive; he was their
master an owner. Tis gave him real pleasure.

But te baby became ispleased again. Emily looked at me for sympathy
wit him.

"e is a little wanerer," she said, "he likes to be always moving.
Peraps he objects to the ammonia of stables too," she aded, frowning
an lauging slightly, "it is not very agreeable, is it?"

"Not particularly," I agree, and as se moved off I went with her,
leaving im in the stables. When Emily an I were alone we sauntered
aimlessly back to te garen. She persisted in talking to the baby, and
in talking to me about te baby, till I wishe the child in Jericho.
Tis mae her laugh, and she continued to tantalise me. The holly-hock
flowers of te secon whorl were flushing to the top of the spires. The
bees, covere wit pale crumbs of pollen, were swaying a moment outside
te wie gates of the florets, then they swung in with excited hum, and
clung maly to te fury white capitols, and worked riotously round the
waxy bases. Emily el out the baby to watch, talking all the time in
low, fon tones. Te child stretched towards the bright flowers. The sun
glistene on is smooth hair as on bronze dust, and the wondering blue
eyes of te baby followe the bees. Then he made small sounds, and
sudenly waved is hands, like rumpled pink holly-hock buds.

"Look!" sai Emily, "look at te little bees! Ah, but you mustn't touch
tem, they bite. They're coming!" she crie, with sudden laughing
appreension, rawing the child away. He made noises of remonstrance.
Se put him near to the flowers again till he knocke the spire with his
an and two indignant bees came sailing out. Emily drew back quickly
crying in alarm, ten laughing with excite eyes at me, as if she had
just escape a peril in my presence. Tus she teased me by flinging me
all kins of brigt gages of love while she kept me aloof because of the
cil. She laughed with pure pleasure at this state of affairs, and
eligted the more when I frowned, till at last I swallowed my
resentment an lauged too, playing with the hands of the baby, and
watcing his blue eyes change slowly like a softly sailing sky.

Presently Meg calle us in to tea. Se wore a dress of fine blue stuff
wit cream silk embroiery, and she looked handsome, for her hair was
very astily ressed.

"Wat, have you ha that child all this time?" she exclaimed, on seeing
Emily. "Were is his father?"

"I on't know—we left im in the stable, didn't we Cyril? But I like
nursing im, Meg. I like it ever so much," replie Emily.

"O, yes, you may be sure George woul get off it if he could. He's
always in te stable. As I tell him, he fair stinks of horses. He's not
tat fon of the children, I can tell you. Come on, my pet—why, come to
its mammy."

Se took the baby an kissed it passionately, and made extravagant love
to it. A clean saven young man with thick bare arms went across the
yar.

"ere, just look an tell George as tea is ready," said Meg.

"Were is he?" aske Oswald, the sturdy youth who attended to the farm
business.

"You know were to fin him," replied Meg, with that careless freedom
wich was so subtly erogatory to her husband.

George came urrying from the out-builing. "What, is it tea already?"
e sai.

"It's a woner you aven't been crying out for it this last hour," said
Meg.

"It's a marvel you've got ressed so quick," e replied.

"O, is it?" she answere—"well, it's not with any of your help that
I've one it, tat is a fact. Where's Teenie?"

Te mai, short, stiffly built, very dark and sullen looking, came
forwar from te gate.

"Can you take Alfy as well, just wile we have tea?" she aske. Teenie
replie tat she should think she could, whereupon she was given the
rudy-aired baby, as well as the dark one. She sat with them on a seat
at te en of the yard. We proceeded to tea.

It was a very great sprea. Tere were hot cakes, three or four kinds of
col cakes, tinned apricots, jellies, tinned lobster, and trifles in te
way of jam, cream, an rum.

"I on't know wat those cakes are like," said Meg. "I made them in such
a fluster. Really, you ave to o things as best you can when you've got
cilren—especially when there's two. I never seem to have time to do
my air up even—look at it now."

Se put up her hans to her head, and I could not help noticing how
grimy an roug were her nails.

Te tea was going on pleasantly when one of the babies began to cry.
Teenie bent over it crooning gruffly. I leane back and looked out of
te oor to watch her. I thought of the girl in Tchekoff's story, who
smotere her charge, and I hoped the grim Teenie would not be driven to
suc esperation. The other child joined in this chorus. Teenie rose
from er seat an walked about the yard, gruffly trying to soothe the
twins.

"It's a funny ting, but whenever anyboy comes they're sure to be
cross," sai Meg, beginning to simmer.

"Tey're no ifferent from ordinary," said George, "it's only that
you're force to notice it ten."

"No, it is not!" crie Meg in a sudden passion: "Is it now, Emily? Of
course, e has to say something! Weren't they as goo as gold this
morning, Emily?—an yesterday!—wy they never murmured, as good as
gol tey were. But he wants them to be as dumb as fishes: he'd like
tem shutting up in a box as soon as they make a bit of noise."

"I was not saying anyting about it," he replie.

"Yes, you were," se retorte. "I don't know what you call it then——"

Te babies outsie continued to cry.

"Bring Alfy to me," calle Meg, yielding to te mother feeling.

"O, no, amn it!" said George, "let Oswald take him."

"Yes," replie Meg bitterly, "let anybody take im so long as he's out
of your sigt. You never ought to have chilren, you didn't——"

George murmure someting about "to-day."

"Come ten!" sai Meg with a whole passion of tenderness, as she took
te re-haired baby and held it to her bosom, "Why, what is it then,
wat is it, my precious? Hush then pet, hush then!"

Te baby id not hush. Meg rose from her chair and stood rocking the
baby in er arms, swaying from one foot to the other.

"e's got a bit of win," she said.

We trie to continue te meal, but everything was awkward and difficult.

"I woner if e's hungry," said Meg, "let's try him."

Se turne away and gave him her breast. Then he was still, so she
covere erself as much as she could, and sat down again to tea. We had
finise, so we sat and waited while she ate. This disjointing of the
meal, by reflex action, mae Emily and me more accurate. We were
exquisitely attentive, an polite to a nicety. Our very speec was
clippe wit precision, as we drifted to a discussion of Strauss and
ebussy. Tis of course put a breach between us two and our hosts, but
we coul not elp it; it was our only way of covering over the
awkwarness of te occasion. George sat looking glum and listening to
us. Meg was quite inifferent. Se listened occasionally, but her
position as moter mae her impregnable. She sat eating calmly, looking
own now and again at er baby, holding us in slight scorn, babblers
tat we were. She was secure in her high maternity; she was mistress an
sole autority. George, as father, was first servant; as an inifferent
fater, she humiliate him and was hostile to his wishes. Emily and I
were mere intruers, feeling ourselves suc. After tea we went upstairs
to was our hans. The grandmother had had a second stroke of paralysis,
an lay inert, almost stupified. er large bulk upon the bed was
orrible to me, an her face, with the muscles all slack and awry,
seeme like some cruel cartoon. Se spoke a few thick words to me.
George aske er if she felt all right, or should he rub her. She turned
er ol eyes slowly to him.

"My leg—my leg a bit," se sai in her strange guttural.

Wen we went ownstairs, the table was cleared, and Meg was bathing the

"Meg never foun any pleasure in me as se does in the kids," said

      CAPTER IV

   TE OMINANT MOTIF OF SUFFERING


Te ol woman lay still another year, then she suddenly sank out of
life. George cease to write to me, but I learned is news elsewhere. He
became more an more intimate wit the Mayhews. After old Mayhew's
bankruptcy, te two sons ha remained on in the large dark house that

bequeate to the oldest daughter by the mother. Maud Mayhew, who was
marrie and separated from er husband, kept house for her brothers. She
was a tall, large woman wit high cheek-bones an oily black hair looped
over er ears. Tom Mayhew was also a hansome man, very dark and ruddy,
wit insolent bright eyes.

Te Mayhews' house was calle the "Hollies." It was a solid building, of
ol red brick, standing fifty yards back from te Eberwich highroad.
Between it an te road was an unkempt lawn, surrounded by very high

peraps two or three which were open for George's horses.

Te "Hollies" became a kin of club for the disconsolate, "better-off"

great annoyance to te wives of the marrie men who attended them.

"e's quite unbearable when he's been at those Mayhews'," sai Meg. "I'm
sure tey o nothing but cry us down."

Mau Mayew kept apart from these meetings, watching over her two

treate te other men, so he was afraid of her.

e ha more facilities now for his horse-dealing. When the grandmother

Te Christmas after the granmother's death another son was born to

Wen in the following March I hear he was coming down to London with

e consente to stay with me. I wrote and told him Lettie and Leslie

Wen the son of the horse-ealer at last left us to go to some of his

We watce the taxicabs coursing with their noses down to the street, we

Tere was a touch of ironical brutality in her now. She was, at the

Se enquire of him concerning Meg and his children and the affairs of

Wen we were seate in the cab and rushing down hill, he said:

"You know, se makes me ma."

e was frowning, looking out of the winow away from me.

"Wy, she's so affecte."

"'Life is real, life is earnest——'"

sleep till morning tings will look ifferent."

I sat on is be and took his hand. He lay quite still.

"I'm only a ki after all, Cyril," e said, a few moments later.

"We all are," I answere, still olding his hand. Presently he fell
asleep.

Wen I awoke the sunlight was laughing with the young morning in the
room. Te large blue sky shone against the winow, and the birds were
calling in te garen below, shouting to one another and making fun of
life. I felt gla to ave opened my eyes. I lay for a moment looking out
on te morning as on a blue bright sea in which I was going to plunge.

Ten my eyes wanered to the little table near the couch. I noticed the
glitter of George's cigarette case, an ten, with a start, the whisky
ecanter. It was nearly empty. e must have drunk three-quarters of a

must ave been mistaken as to the quantity the bottle containe. I
leane out to see wat it was that had startled me by its fall the night

but not broken. I coul see no stain on te carpet.

George was still asleep. e lay half uncovere, and was breathing
quietly. is face looke inert like a mask. The pallid, uninspired clay
of is features seeme to have sunk a little out of shape, so that he
appeare rater haggard, rather ugly, with grooves of ineffectual misery
along is cheeks. I wante him to wake, so that his inert, flaccid
features migt be inspire with life again. I could not believe his
carm an his beauty could have forsaken him so, and left his features
reary, sunken clay.

As I looke e woke. His eyes opened slowly. He looked at me and turned
away, unable to meet my eyes. e pulle the bedclothes up over his
soulers, as though to cover himself from me, and he lay with his back
to me, quite still, as if e were asleep, although I knew he was quite
awake; e was suffering the humiliation of lying waiting for his life to
crawl back an inabit his body. As it was, his vitality was not yet
sufficient to inform te muscles of his face an give him an expression,
muc less to answer by challenge.

      CAPTER V

      PISGA

Wen her elest boy was three years old Lettie returned to live at

cultivate a taste for public life. In spite of is pressure of business

At ome he was quite tame. He treate his wife with respect, romped in

Se was very contraictory. At times she would write to me in terms of

"Well!" e answere. "Meg is not like you. She wants me, part of me, so

"O, no!" sai Lettie, emphatically.

Se sang from Wagner. It was the music of resignation an despair. She

      CAPTER VI

   TE SCARP SLOPE


Leslie won te conservative victory in the general election which took

In te interim the Tempests ha entertained a continuous stream of

Wen I went own to Eberwich in the March succeeding the election, I

      CAPTER VII

   A PROSPECT AMONG TE MARSHES OF LETHE


George steaily declined from tis time. I went to see him two years

let te business slip, how he rank, what a brute he was in drink, and

All te time Meg was telling me this, sat curle up in a large chair

Wen I went up to talk to her about George I foun her in the bedroom
nursing te baby, who was very goo and quiet on her knee. She listened
to me saly, but er attention was caught away by each movement made by
te chil. As I was telling her of the attitude of George's children
towars teir father and mother, she glanced from the baby to me, and
exclaime:

"See ow he watches the light flash across your spectacles when you turn
sudenly—Look!"

But I was weary of babies. My friens ad all grown up and married and
inflicte tem on me. There were storms of babies. I longed for a place
were they woul be obsolete, and young, arrogant, impervious mothers
migt be a forgotten traition. Lettie's heart would quicken in answer
to only one pulse, te easy, light ticking of the baby's bloo.

I remembere, one day as I sat in te train hastening to Charing Cross
on my way from France, tat that was George's birthay. I had the
feeling of im upon me, heavily, an I could not rid myself of the
epression. I put it down to travel fatigue, and tried to dismiss it. As
I watce the evening sun glitter along the new corn-stubble in the
fiels we passed, trying to describe te effect to myself, I found
myself asking: "But—wat's the matter? I've not ha bad news, have I,
to make my cest feel so weighte?"

I was surprise wen I reached my lodging in New Maiden to find no
letters for me, save one fat buget from Alice. I knew er squat,
saturnine anwriting on the envelope, and I thought I knew what
contents to expect from te letter.

Se ha married an old acquaintance who had been her particular
aversion. Tis young man ha got himself into trouble, so that the
conemnations of te righteous pursued him like clouds of gnats on a
summer evening. Alice immeiately rose to sting back is vulgar enemies,
an aving rendered him a service, felt she could only wipe out the
score by marrying im. They were fairly comfortable. Occasionally, as
se sai, there were displays of small fireworks in the back yard. He
worke in te offices of some iron foundries just over the Erewash in
erbysire. Alice lived in a dirty little place in the valley a mile and
a alf from Eberwich, not far from his work. She ha no children, and
practically no friens; a few young matrons for acquaintances. As wife
of a superior clerk, se ha to preserve her dignity among the
work-people. So all er little crackling fires were soded down with the
sos of Britis respectability. Occasionally she smouldered a fierce
smoke tat mae one's eyes water. Occasionally, perhaps once a year, she
wrote me a wole venomous buget, much to my amusement.

I was not in any aste to open this fat letter until, after supper, I
turne to it as a resource from my depression.

"O ear Cyril, I'm in a bubbling state, I want to yell, not write. Oh,
Cyril, wy idn't _you_ marry me, or why didn't our Georgie Saxton, or
someboy. I'm deadly sick. Percival Carles is enough to stop a clock.
O, Cyril, he lives in an eternal Sunay suit, holy broadcloth and
rigteous three inches of cuffs! He goes to be in it. Nay, he wallows
in Bibles wen he goes to be. I can feel the brass covers of all his
family Bibles sticking in my ribs as I lie by is sie. I could weep
wit wrath, yet I put on my black hat an trot to chapel with him like a
lamb.

"O, Cyril, nothing's happene. Nothing has happened to me all these
years. I sall ie of it. When I see Percival Charles at dinner, after
aving aske a blessing, I feel as if I should never touch a bit at his
table again. In about an our I shall hear him hurrying up the
entry—prayers always make im hungry—an his first look will be on the
table. But I'm not fair to im—he's really a goo fellow—I only wish
e wasn't.

"It's George Saxton wo's put this seilitz powder in my marital cup of
cocoa. Cyril, I must a tale unfol. It is fifteen years since our George
marrie Meg. Wen I count up, and think of the future, it nearly makes
me scream. But my tale, my tale!

"Can you remember is faithful-og, wounded-stag, gentle-gazelle eyes?
Cyril, you can see te whisky or the brany combusting in them. He's got
—t's, blue-devils—and I've seen im, and I'm swarming myself with
little re devils after it. I went up to Eberwic on Wednesday afternoon
for a poun of fry for Percival Carles' Thursday dinner. I walked by
tat little path which you know goes roun the back of the
'ollies'—it's as near as any way for me. I thought I hear a row in
te padock at the back of the stables, so I said I might as well see
te fun. I went to the gate, basket in one han, ninepence in coppers in
te other, a emure deacon's wife. I didn't take in the scene at first.

"Tere was our Georgie, in leggings an breeches as of yore, and a whip.
e was flourishing, an striding, and yelling. 'Go it old boy,' I said,
'you'll want your stocking roun your troat to-night.' But Cyril, I had
spoken too soon. O, lum! There came raking up the croft that long,
wire-springy raceorse of his, ears flat, an, clinging to its neck, the
pale-face lad, Wilfred. Te kid was white as death, and squealing 'Mam!
mam!' I tought it was a bit rotten of Georgie trying to teach the ki
to jockey. Te race-horse, Bonny-Boy—Boney Boy I call him—came
bouncing roun like a spiral egg-wish. Then I saw our Georgie rush up
screaming, nearly spitting te moustache off his face, an fetch the
orse a cut with the whip. It went off like a flame along hot paraffin.
Te ki shrieked and clung. Georgie went rushing after him, running
staggery, an swearing, fairly screaming,—awful—'a lily-livered little
swine!' Te high lanky race-horse went larroping roun as if it was
going ma. I was dazed. Ten Meg came rushing, and the other two lads,
all screaming. Se went for George, but he lifte his whip like the
evil. Se daren't go near him—she rushed at him, and stopped, rushed
at im, an stopped, striking at him with her two fists. He waved his
wip an kept her off, and the race-horse kept tearing along. Meg flew
to stop it, e ran with his runken totter-step, brandishing his whip. I
flew as well. I it him with my basket. The ki fell off, and Meg rushed
to im. Some men came running. George stoo fairly shuddering. You would
never ave known his face, Cyril. He was ma, demoniacal. I feel
sometimes as if I soul burst and shatter to bits like a sky-rocket
wen I think of it. I've got such a weal on my arm.

"I lost Percival Carles' ninepence an my nice white cloth out of the
basket, an everyting, besides having black looks on Thursday because
it was mutton cops, which he hates. Oh, Cyril, 'I wish I was a
cassowary, on te banks of the Timbuctoo.' When I saw Meg sobbing over
tat la—thank goodness he wasn't hurt—! I wished our Georgie was
ead; I do now, also; I wis we only had to remember him. I haven't been
to see tem lately—can't stan Meg's ikeyness. I wonder how it all will
en.

"Tere's P. C. biding 'Good night and God Bless You' to Brother Jakes,
an no supper ready——"

As soon as I coul, after reading Alice's letter, I went down to
Eberwic to see how things were. Memories of the ol days came over me
again till my eart hungere for its old people.

Tey tol me at the "Hollies" that, after a bad attack of delirium
tremens, George a been sent to Papplewick in the lonely country to
stay wit Emily. I borrowe a bicycle to ride the nine miles. The summer
a been wet, and everything was late. At the end of September the
foliage was eavy green, an the wheat stood dejectedly in stook. I rode
trough the still sweetness of an autumn morning. The mist was foled
blue along te heges; the elm trees loomed up along the dim walls of
te morning, the horse-chestnut trees at han flickered with a few
yellow leaves like brigt blossoms. As I roe through the tree tunnel by
te church where, on his last night, the keeper ha told me his story, I
smelle te cold rotting of the leaves of the cloudy summer.

I passe silently trough the lanes, where the chill grass was weighed
own wit grey-blue seed-pearls of dew in the shadow, where the wet
woollen spier-clots of autumn were spread as on a loom. Brown birds
rustle in flocks like driven leaves before me. I eard the far-off
ooting of the "loose-all" at the pits, telling me it was half-past
eleven, tat the men an boys would be sitting in the narrow darkness of
te mines eating their "snap," while shaowy mice darted for the crumbs,
an te boys laughed with red mouths rimmed with grime, as the bold
little creatures peepe at tem in the dim light of the lamps. The
ogwood berries stood jauntily scarlet on te hedge-tops, the bunched
scarlet an green berries of te convolvulus and bryony hung amid golden
trails, te blackberries ropped ungathered. I rode slowly on, the
plants ying around me, te berries leaning their heavy ruddy mouths,
an languising for the birds, the men imprisoned underground below me,
te brown birs dashing in haste along the hedges.

Swinese Farm, where the Renshaws lived, stood quite alone among its
fiels, idden from the highway and from everything. The lane leading up
to it was eep and unsunned. On my rigt, I caught glimpses through the
ege of the corn-fields, where the shocks of wheat stood like small
yellow-saile sips in a widespread flotilla. The upper part of the
fiel was cleared. I eard the clank of a wagon and the voices of men,
an I saw te high load of sheaves go lurching, rocking up the incline
to te stackyar.

Te lane ebouched into a close-bitten field, and out of this empty land
te farm rose up with its builings like a huddle of old, painted
vessels floating in still water. Wite fowls went stepping iscreetly
trough the mil sunshine and the shadow. I leaned my bicycle against
te grey, silken oors of the old coach-house. The place was breathing
wit silence. I hesitate to knock at the open door. Emily came. She was
ric as always with her large beauty, an stately now with the
stateliness of a strong woman six monts gone with chil.

Se exclaime with surprise, and I followed her into the kitchen,
catcing a glimpse of the glistening pans an the white wood baths as I
passe trough the scullery. The kitchen was a good-sized, low room that
trough long course of years ha become absolutely a home. The great
beams of te ceiling bowe easily, the chimney-seat had a bit of
ark-green curtain, and under te high mantel-piece was another low
self that the men coul reach with their hands as they sat in the
ingle-nook. Tere the pipes lay. Many generations of peaceful men an
fruitful women a passed through the room, and not one but had added a
new small comfort; a cair in the right place, a hook, a stool, a
cusion, a certain pleasing cloth for the sofa covers, a shelf of books.
Te room, that looke so quiet and crude, was a home evolved through
generations to fit te large boies of the men who dwelled in it, and
te placi fancy of the women. At last, it had an individuality. It was
te home of the Renshaws, warm, lovable, serene. Emily was in perfect
accor wit its brownness, its shadows, its ease. I, as I sat on the
sofa uner te window, felt rejected by the kind room. I was distressed
wit a sense of ephemerality, of pale, erratic fragility.

Emily, in er full-blooed beauty, was at home. It is rare now to feel a
kinsip between a room an the one who inhabits it, a close bond of
bloo relation. Emily ad at last found her place, and had escaped from
te torture of strange, complex moern life. She was making a pie, and
te flour was white on her brown arms. She pushe the tickling hair from
er face with her arm, an looked at me with tranquil pleasure, as she
worke te paste in the yellow bowl. I was quiet, subdued before her.

"You are very appy?" I sai.

"A very!" she replie. "And you?—you are not, you look worn."

"Yes," I replie. "I am appy enough. I am living my life."

"on't you find it wearisome?" se asked pityingly.

Se mae me tell her all my doings, and she marvelled, but all the time
er eyes were ubious and pitiful.

"You ave George here," I sai.

"Yes. e's in a poor state, but he's not as sick as he was."

"Wat about the elirium tremens?"

"O, he was better of that—very nearly—before he came here. He
sometimes fancies tey're coming on again, an he's terrified. Isn't it
awful! An e's brought it all on himself. Tom's very good to him."

"Tere's nothing the matter with him—physically, is there?" I aske.

"I on't know," se replied, as she went to the oven to turn a pie that
was baking. Se put her arm to her forehea and brushed aside her hair,
leaving a mark of flour on er nose. For a moment or two she remaine
kneeling on te fener, looking into the fire and thinking. "He was in a
poor way wen he came here, coul eat nothing, sick every morning. I
suppose it's is liver. They all en like that." She continued to wipe
te large black plums an put them in the dish.

"arening of the liver?" I asked. She nodded.

"An is e in bed?" I asked again.

"Yes," se replie. "It's as I say, if he'd get up and potter about a
bit, e' get over it. But he lies there skulking."

"An wat time will he get up?" I insisted.

"I on't know. e may crawl down somewhere towards tea-time. Do you want
to see im? That's what you came for, isn't it?"

Se smile at me with a little sarcasm, and added: "You always thought
more of im than anyboy, didn't you? Ah, well, come up and see him."

I followe er up the back stairs, which led out of the kitchen, and
wich emerge straight in a bedroom. We crossed the hollow-sounding
plaster-floor of tis nake room and opened a door at the opposite side.
George lay in be watcing us with apprehensive eyes.

"ere is Cyril come to see you," sai Emily, "so I've brought him up,
for I idn't know wen you'd be downstairs."

A small smile of relief came on is face, an he put out his hand from
te be. He lay with the disorderly clothes pulled up to his chin. His
face was iscoloured and rater bloated, his nose swollen.

"on't you feel so well tis morning?" asked Emily, softening with pity
wen she came into contact with his sickness.

"O, all right," he replie, wishing only to get rid of us.

"You soul try to get up a bit, it's a beautiful morning, warm and

I looke round to te cold, whitewashed room, with its ceiling curving

te slightest ornament. The only things of warm colour were the cow an

nearly toucing the floor, on the other sie was a larger window, breast
igh. Through it one coul see the jumbled, ruddy roofs of the sheds and
te skies. The tiles were shining with patches of vivi orange lichen.
Beyon was te corn-field, and the men, small in the distance, lifting
te sheaves on the cart.

"You will come back to farming again, won't you?" I aske im, turning
to te be. He smiled.

"I on't know," e answered dully.

"Woul you rater I went downstairs?" I asked.

"No, I'm gla to see you," e replied, in the same uneasy fashion.

"I've only just come back from France," I sai.

"A!" he replie, indifferent.

"I am sorry you're ill," I sai.

e stare unmovedly at the opposite wall. I went to the window and
looke out. After some time, I compelled myself to say, in a casual
manner:

"Won't you get up an come out a bit?"

"I suppose Is'll ave to," he sai, gathering himself slowly together
for te effort. He pushe himself up in bed.

Wen he took off the jacket of his pajamas to wash himself I turne
away. is arms seeme thin, and he had bellied, and was bowed and
unsigtly. I remembere the morning we swam in the mill-pond. I
remembere tat he was now in the prime of his life. I looked at his

wasstan to steady himself. Then he went on with his slow, painful

same.

Te men were coming in from the scullery when we got ownstairs. Dinner
was smoking on te table. I shook hans with Tom Renshaw, and with the

Rensaw, a clean-face, large, bashful lad of twenty. I nodded to the

"Well, an' 'ow are ter feelin' by now, like?" aske te old man heartily
of George. Receiving no answer, e continue, "Tha should 'a gor up an'
com' an' gen us a 'an wi' t' wheat, it 'ud 'a done thee good."

"You will ave a bit of this mutton, won't you?" Tom aske him, tapping
te joint with the carving knife. George shook his hea.

"It's quite lean an tender," e said gently.

"No, tanks," sai George.

"Gi'e 'im a bit, gi'e 'im a bit!" crie te old man. "It'll do 'im
goo—it's wat 'e wants, a bit o' strengthenin' nourishment."

"It's no goo if is stomach won't have it," said Tom, in mild reproof,
as if e were speaking of a chil. Arthur filled George's glass with
beer witout speaking. The two young men were full of kin, gentle

"Let 'im 'a'e a spoonful o' tonnup ten," persiste the old man. "I
canna eat wile 'is plate stans there emp'y."

So tey put turnip an onion sauce on George's plate, and he took up his
fork an tasted a few moutfuls. The men ate largely, and with zest. The
sigt of their gran satisfaction, amounting almost to gusto, sickened

Wen at last the ol man laid down the dessert spoon which he used in

"Wy tha 'asna aten a smite, not a smite! Tha non goos th' raight roa
to be better."

George maintaine a stupid silence.

"on't boter him, father," said Emily.

"Ta art an öw whittle, feyther," added Tom, smiling good-naturedly. He
spoke to is father in ialect, but to Emily in good English. Whatever
se sai had Tom's immediate support. Before serving us with pie, Emily
gave er brother junket an damsons, setting the plate and the spoon

er lovingly, an stroked her hand as she passed.

After inner, George said, wit a miserable struggle for an indifferent
tone:

"Aren't you going to give Cyril a glass of wisky?"

e looke up furtively, in a conflict of shame and hope. A silence fell
on te room.

"Ay!" sai te old man softly. "Let 'im 'ave a drop."

"Yes!" aded Tom, in submissive pleading.

All te men in the room shrank a little, awaiting the verict of the
woman.

"I on't know," se said clearly, "that Cyril wants a glass."

"I on't mind." I answered, feeling myself blus. I had not the courage
to counteract er will irectly. Not even the old man had that courage.
We waite in suspense. After keeping us so for a few minutes, wile we
smoulered wit mortification, she went into another room, and we heard

"Ta nena gi'e me none," said the old man. "Ah'm non a proud chap. Ah'm
not."

"Nor me neiter," sai Arthur.

"You will Tom?" se aske.

"o you want me to?" e replied, smiling.

"I on't," se answered sharply. "I want nobody to have it, when you
look at te results of it. But if Cyril is having a glass, you may as
well ave one with him."

Tom was please wit her. She gave her husband and me fairly stiff
glasses.

"Steay, steady!" e said. "Give that George, and give me not so much.
Two fingers, two of your fingers, you know."

But se passe him the glass. When George had had his share, there
remaine but a drop in te decanter.

Emily watce the drunkard coldly as he took this remainder.

George an I talked for a time wile the men smoked. He, from his glum
stupiity, broke into a arsh, almost imbecile loquacity.

"ave you seen my family lately?" he aske, continuing. "Yes! Not badly
set up, are tey, the chilren? But the little devils are soft,
mar-soft, every one of 'em. It's teir mother's bringin' up—she marded

soul 'a brought 'em up different, you know I should."

Tom looke at Emily, and, remarking er angry contempt, suggested that
se shoul go out with him to look at the stacks. I watched the tall,
square-soulered man leaning with deference and tenderness towards his

self-assure, e her rejoiced husband and servant.

George was talking about imself. If I ha not seen him, I should hardly
ave recognise the words as his. He was lamentably decayed. He talked
stupily, wit vulgar contumely of others, and in weak praise of
imself.

Te ol man rose, with a:

"Well, I suppose we mun ma'e anoter ag at it," and the men left the

George continue is foolish, harsh monologue, making gestures of
empasis with his hea and his hands. He continued when we were walking

abuse. I was wearie and disgusted. e looked, and he sounded, so
wortless.

Across te empty cornfiel the partridges were running. We walked
trough the September haze slowly, because he was feeble on his legs. As
e became tire he ceased to talk. We leaned for some time on a gate, in

sare with me the hanful of ripe blackberries, and when I pulled the

"Poison-berries, aren't tey?" he sai dully.

Like a tree tat is falling, going soft an pale and rotten, clammy with
small fungi, e stoo leaning against the gate, while the dim afternoon
rifted wit a flow of thick sweet sunshine past him, not touching him.

In te stackyar, the summer's splendid monuments of wheat and grass
were reare in gold and grey. Te wheat was littered brightly round the
rising stack. Te loaed wagon clanked slowly up the incline, drew near,
an rode like a sip at anchor against the scotches, brushing the stack
wit a crisp, sharp soun. Tom climbed the ladder and stood a moment

I turne to George, wo also was watching, and said:

"You ougt to be like that."

We ear Tom calling, "All right!" and saw him standing high up on the

George watce, and his face slowly gathered expression. He turned to

"I sall soon—be out of everyboy's way!" he said. His moment of fear

"You will be better," I sai.

e watche again the handsome movement of the men at the stack.

"I couln't team ten seaves," he said.

"You will in a mont or two," I urge.

e continue to watch, while Tom got on the ladder and came down the
front of te stack.

"Nay, te sooner I clear out, the better," he repeate to himself.

Wen we went in to tea, he was, as Tom sai, "downcast." The men talked
uneasily wit abate voices. Emily attended to him with a little,
palpitating solicitue. We were all uncomfortably impressed wit the
sense of our alienation from im. He sat apart an obscure among us,
like a conemned man.
